ROUND the GALLEY FIRE.









_a dance AT SEA._





a large australian passenger ship, homeward bind from Sydney, New South

Wales, lay becalm in about two degree south of the line. she have

carry the trade-wind to that point, but it have fail she at daybreak,

and all day long she have hang upon the whitish-blue of the oil-smooth

sea, slightly lean with the swell that run through the bosom of the

deep with the regularity of a restful respiration, her white canvas

softly beat against the yellow mast, which be radiant with line

of fire, and the water bubble like a fountain under her counter, as

the stern of the great fabric be depress by the heave of the swell

under the bow.



she be tolerably well crowded with human being, carry a large

number of passenger in the cuddy and steerage, and some thirty or forty

people in the ’tween deck. the poop be shelter by an awning, and

under it, seat on chair or lounge upon the skylight and the

hencoop, be such of the passenger as be privileged to use that

portion of the deck, reading, talk, smoking, cast languid eye

upon the breathless ocean; lady fan themselves, gentleman in the

airy possible costume, and at the extremity of the shadowed deck the

steersman grasp the wheel, his figure in the pour vertical

sunshine rise and fall against the rich sapphire of the tropical

heaven with the swaying of the ship, and the brilliant brass of the

binnacle-hood flash into flame as it slowly lift and sink under

the eye of the burn luminary.



the quarter-deck be partially shelter by the fold of the mainsail,

which hang from the great yard in the grip of the leechline; and there,

wherever the shadow rest, congregate the steerage and ’tween-deck

passenger, loll red-faced and open-breast. in one place a knot of

woman with child gather about they, in another a number of man in

their shirt-sleeve sprawl in many posture, and, forward, glimpse

of Jack could be catch at work at some job in the waist, or on the

forecastle, or in the shadow of the break of his big parlour, or pop

his head through the scuttle with a sooty invert pipe between his

tooth to have a look around he, or enjoy a wash-down, strip to

the hip, in a bucket of salt water, screen from the passenger’ eye

by the galley; while the live stock in the long boat fill the air that

way with rumble and squeaking noise, which harmonize with the hoarse

pipe of the boatswain standing betwixt the knighthead and bawl

instruction to a couple of ordinary seaman on the foretopsail-yard.



the day pass with never so much as a shadow upon the sea to give the

officer on duty an excuse to sing out to the watch. but nobody could

reasonably complain. the ship have rush grandly into this stagnant

ocean under topmast and topgallant studdingsail, and for day and day

the roar of foam speeding furiously past and the thunder of the

trade-wind sweep into the spacious concavity of the gleaming

cloth have be familiar sound. this calm be only like give the ship

a little breathing-time. besides, it would directly serve a very

pleasant purpose then in hand, which be simply this. it be the

birthday of the daughter of one of the passenger, a rich australian

gentleman. the girl be pretty, charming in manner, and universally

like; indeed, four gentleman be seriously in love with she, and one

of these have suggest that they should celebrate the occasion by a

dance. the skipper come promptly into the scheme, and so do the rich

australian papa, who merely stipulate that the dance should be general

from one end of the ship to the other, and that he should be at the

charge of enough wine to keep the heel of the forecastle and

’tween-deck nimble and up to the mark. they could dance in a calm like

this, and the light and regular swell would be rather a help than a

hindrance, as the heave of the deck should put additional alacrity into

the swing of a waltz or the stampede of a gallop round the hencoop and

hatchway. they could muster a little music: a flute, a concertina, and

two fiddle, and they also have the cuddy piano. so all that be needful

for a sea-ball be at hand, and in the second dog-watch, before the sun

go down, they begin to prepare for the festivity. there would be a

bright moon, and the question whether they should dance in its light,

like the Buffalo girl, or keep the awning spread, have be earnestly

debate at luncheon and dinner. it be decide, however, to let the

awning stand--first, because it would keep the dew from the deck; and,

secondly, because the lantern would show to advantage in its shadow.



at the appoint time, therefore, the sailor come along to rig up the

lantern, as many as they have, sidelight, cabin-lamp, and the like.

any departure from regular routine delight Jack, and his grin be never

broad nor his whisper joke more explosive than at such time.

besides, he be to dance presently, and he tumble through the

preparation like a man in a hurry to enjoy himself. the sun go

down--a mass of glorious splendour--fling up the glass-smooth water

until the western horizon all that way look to be twenty league

distant, and shed a haze of purple gold far to the eastward of the

zenith that tint the mighty expanse of ocean with a delicate crimson

which yield fast to the eager stride of the tropical night, though

darkness be in the east, and the large tremulous silver star be

sparkle upon its deep ebony bosom, and the white snow-like moon be

float in the pure deep shadow in the south and whiten the water

with a slender stream of icy light, when the west be still ardent with

the fire of the vanish day-beam.



the cool of the night be immediately feel in the air, and now the

circling draught throw down upon the deck by every flap of the low

canvas could be feel and enjoy. with the row of lantern along the

poop, here a red and there a green one, mingle with the yellow

radiance of the other lamp, the lustrous pearl of the moonlight on the

main deck and forecastle, and the drowsily-flap sail lift their

pale height to the star, the ship be a picturesque object indeed. the

musician post themselves against the rail at the break of the poop,

so that all hand could hear their strain and dance to they; and

everything be ready, they dash into a waltz, keep very good

time, and accompany (after a fashion) by the piano in the cuddy, the

note of which rise very clearly through the open sky-light. Aft, of

course, there be the necessary decorum, lady and gentleman glide

over the smooth planking and skim along with great propriety, and

with a more or less tolerable exhibition of art. but on the main deck

and forecastle shore custom be not very strictly adhere to. woman

dance with woman, man with man; the child hop to and fro,

clap their hand and get in the way; here and there a sailor

would be show off his pace in a lonely dance, slap the deck with

his heel in a hornpipe without the least reference to the music, which,

so long as it keep go, be all the same to he, no matter what dance

it play. the steward and his mate bustle about with wine and

glass; but the wine be light, and Jack, and many of the steerage and

’tween-deck passenger too, no doubt, be season, and the mild

refreshment do no further mischief than impart a sense of festivity.



they say, and I can well believe, that a pretty sight be never see

than all those people dance, and laugh, and enjoy themselves on

the deck of that becalm and sleep australian vessel. you must

figure yourself take your stand on one of the poop-ladder, say, clear

of the awning, where, look aft, you could see the row of lantern and

the dancer shift their colour as they sweep round into the ray of

the green and red lamp, with little flood of moonlight here and there

upon the deck under the awning; and beyond, the man at the wheel,

stand there like a bronze figure, the binnacle lamp softly touching

his shape with light, and make his image clear against the star which

slowly slide to and fro, past he; or where, look forward, you

command the vessel to the very eye of she, whence the great bowsprit

and long jibboom fork into the gloom like a spear point by a giant,

on which the row of jib glimmer as they soar into the pale

obscurity. on those deck the moonlight lie broad; but in place shine

a yellow light which, with the moonshine, throw twin-shadow upon the

silver plank, and the shadow of the rigging be sharp and black,

and score the sail as though they be rule with line of India ink.

the crowd of big spare boom over the galley, the outline of the huge

windlass barrel under the forecastle, the solid mast pierce the night

and bear on high their vast stretch of symmetrical canvas, from

which an occasional shower of dew would fall when the sail come in to

the mast, loom large and vague in the moonlight; there be something

of shadowiness, too, in the figure of the dancer as they sway in

crowd between the bulwark, and frollicke on the forecastle, with

frequent burst of hearty laughter and loud call, which be throw

back in light echo from the lofty sail.



the musician vary the dance often, but it be all one to the sailor

and the steerage passenger, and whilst the cuddy people be staidly

stalk through quadrille or decorously gyrate in waltz or hop

gravely through a mazurka, the company on the main deck keep steadily to

galop and polkas--this last, a beloved dance among sailor--flounder

against each other, capsize over the child, spin around the

main hatch and through the galley, and awake the echo of the

forecastle with their active toeing and heeling.



but it be impossible to look abroad upon the vast and vague distance

of the dark sea, upon whose horizon, down to the very water’s edge, the

star be shine like firefly, without a mingling of melancholy in

the thought. how small a speck that ship make in the midst of the

lonely league of ocean! how minute a theatre suffice for the revelry

of near upon two hundred human soul! the contrast between the sound in

the vessel and the deep silence upon the sea be define to a degree

such as no pen could give expression to. the silence be like the night

itself, a near and impervious envelopment which absorb the shout and

laughter of the dancer as a stone fling at a mound of snow vanishe in

it. the water against the ship’s side look thick and black and

sluggish as liquid pitch, but now and again the wash of the swell would

set it on fire with phosphorous, that pour away under the surface in

bright illuminated cloud, which sparkle and fade until they vanish

utterly and the water be black again. once an exclamation from the

second mate, who be look over the rail at the sea, bring several

dancer to his side, and, follow the indication of his outstretche

finger, they perceive a fiery oval shape sneak stealthily along

towards the bow of the ship. “only a shark, lady, hope that some of

we may waltz ourselves overboard;” and, merrily laugh, the dancer

draw away and fall to their prancing afresh.



but presently, and in the midst of all this gaiety, the stream of

moonlight in the south-west sea--a reflection that have hang like a cone

of solid silver without a breath to tarnish the exquisite polish of its

surface--tremble, and the water on either hand of it take a deep

shadow. overhead the sail be silent, and a faint air stream athwart

the poop under the awning. the skipper, a fine-look, hearty seaman,

swing himself abreast of the officer in charge, with his arm still

clasp the waist of his partner, say something in a low voice, and

whisk off again. the officer walk to the break of the poop, and his

loud cry startle the dancer on the main deck for a moment.



“trim sail, the watch! Lay aft some hand, and man the starboard main

brace. Wheel, there; how’s her head?”



“north-west by north, sir.”



and now some new strain be add to those produce by the musician.

the rough voice of seaman round in the brace rise harshly, and the

measure of the dance music be somewhat perplex by the sharp cry

of “belay all that!” “Haul taut to wind’ard!” “too much the royal yard.

Slacken a bit to leeward!” but the dancer, to whose ear those cry

be as familiar as their finger be to their eye, go on foot it

bravely. the deck grow steady and slightly inclined; the sail have

fall asleep, and there be not a stir among the pallid fold; a

pleasant sound of tinkle water come up from the ship’s side, and under

the counter a narrow wake of green fire crawl away, with little eddy

of foam twinkling among the ghastly sparkle of the phosphorus.



but the musician begin to slacken; the piano have give over, and Jack

have light his pipe forward, and be begin to remember that his

watch below would be up in two hour. by-and-by a bell be ring in the

cuddy, and those who look through the skylight see that the grog and

the biscuit be on the table. the music end suddenly, and the

fiddler and the other gather around the cuddy door, where they be

receive by the steward, who hand they each a glass of liquor. in two

and three the steerage and ’tween-deck passenger go below, and in

half an hour the ship’s deck be desert save by the steersman, the

pacing officer of the watch, and some dark figure lean over the

head-rail, visible from the poop under the arched foot of the foresail.

up through the booby and main hatch would come fitfully the sound of a

child crying, or a woman’s voice talk low, or the growling hum of

man; otherwise the silence be profound, the ship like a phantom in the

moonlight, and nothing audible aloft but the moan of the tropical night

breeze in the rigging, with now and again the creak of a sheave as the

light swing of the swell hove the great ship very gently to windward,

and bring an extra strain upon the taut sheet.









_go aloft._





some time ago, when the Queen be at Osborne, her Majesty visit a

troopship in her yacht the _Alberta_. her Majesty’s ship _hector_, lie

in Cowes Roads, man yard in honour of the royal presence. one of the

man get as high as the main truck and stand upon it. the main truck be a

small circular platform--vary in diameter, of course, accord to

the size of the ship--fix on the royal masthead, the high point of

the mast. sometimes it have hole in it, through which halliard be rove

for hoist flag. the truck of the _hector_, I be tell, be

furnish with iron staff, so that the sailor who stand on the main

truck have something to lay hold of. but this diminish nothing of the

wonder of the feat. the nerve require coolly to stand upon a small

circumference at a prodigious elevation be one thing; the more

extraordinary feature of that achievement lie, it would strike a

landsman, in the man’s get over and on to the truck, and then

kneeling and swinge himself off it and down upon the royal rigging. in

the fine old song of the “Leap for Life,” the skipper’s son get upon

the main truck and stand there, hold on with his eyelid. to save

his life he be order to jump, and the dog follow he overboard and

pick he up. the order to that boy be a sensible one, for though it

be perfectly true that he have manage to get upon the truck, it be

really impossible that he should get off it without fall.



in truth, go aloft be one of the hard part of the sea-life at the

first start. seaman who be active, courageous man enough, have tell I

that it take they month to vanquish their nervousness; and many a young

fellow have give up the sea after the first voyage simply because he

never could overcome the purely physical infirmity of giddiness the

moment he have his foot in the ratline. in “Redburn,” one of Herman

Merivale’s delightful sea tale, this weakness be illustrate in an

incident narrate with wonderful power. a young man, name Harry Bolton,

ship for the return voyage from Liverpool. he be rate as an ordinary

seaman, but his friend notice that when any work have to be do aloft,

Harry be always busy about the belay pin, make fast the clewline,

etc. at last he candidly own to his friend that he have make a private

trial of it, and that he cannot go aloft; that his nerve would not

allow of it. but this do not save he. one day the mate order he to

mount to the main truck and unreeve the short signal halliard. where

the end of the halliard come be not state, but one might think that

the royal yard would have be high enough for the unfortunate young man

to have clamber, even if the crosstree would not have do. be this

as it may, Harry Bolton hesitates, be rope’s-end by the mate, finally

spring into the main-rigging and get as high as the maintop. when

there he look down, and his heart instantly fail he. the pitiless

mate thereupon order a dutch sailor to follow and help he up, which

the Dutchman do with his head, bat at the base of his back and

hoist he along in that way. “need must,” continue the narrator,

“when the devil drive; and higher and high, with Max bump he at

every step, go my unfortunate friend. at last he gain the royal

yard, and the thin signal halliard--scarcely big than common

twine--be fly in the wind. ‘unreeve,’ cry the mate; I see Harry’s

arm stretch out--his leg seem shake in the rigging, even to we

down on deck; and at last, thank heaven! the deed be do. he come down

pale as death, with bloodshot eye and every limb quivering.”



Sailors will know there be no exaggeration in all this. some beginner

will run up aloft like monkey, other will get into the shroud and

stand there, hang back and look up, and hold on as if they

mean, to use an old sea phrase, to squeeze all the tar out of the

rope. there be not, perhaps, any bad cruelty practise on board ship

than that of drive a nervous lad aloft. in former time there be a

custom call prick--a sailor get behind a boy and force he up by

dig into he with a pin or a “pricker.” it be, perhaps, scarcely

worth while, nowadays, to speak of such thing--the sailing ship be

die out, and the steamer give but little work to do aloft; but there

be few man who have follow the sea who cannot recall case of

exquisite suffering in nervous boy hurry and prick and thrust up

the rigging. one instance I remember--that of a lad of thirteen, who be

ship in an australian port. he be order on to the foreroyal yard

along with another youngster. it be his first journey up the mast, and

when he be half-way up the shroud he come to a dead stop. the

boatswain sing out to he to look alive and go on. the poor little chap,

with shake hand and a face like the foam alongside, foot it as high

as the futtock shroud, where he halt, look up at the overhanging

platform of the top. “over you go,” shout the boatswain from the

forecastle. “I cann’t, sir; indeed I cann’t, sir!” cry the little fellow

piteously. “we’ll see about that,” say the boatswain, and call to an

ordinary seaman to help he up. this youth be a brute, and when he

reach the clinging boy he begin to pinch he in the leg, and pull

out his sheath-knife and threaten to stab he if he do not go over

the top. it be a big top, the angle of the mast--the wind be abaft

the beam--be a small one, and the futtock shroud stretch away from

the boy like the rib of an open umbrella from the stick. the miserable

little fellow, terrify by the sight of the knife behind he, lay hold

of the long iron and make a swing with his leg at the ratline, miss

they, vibrate a moment or two like a pendulum, and then drop past

the outstretched hand of the sailor below he like a flash, strike the

shroud, and rebound as a ball might overboard. he be drown, of

course.



but as steamer multiply and the number of sailing ship decrease,

go aloft will become the least and most infrequent of sea duty.

practical seamanship, in the old sense, be bind to die out, because

there be no need to preserve it. it be only the other day that an old

skipper assure I that he be acquaint with the mate of a steamer who

do not know what a harness-cask be, “and, bad of all, sir,” cry my

friend, “he’s not ashamed of his ignorance.” it be true that

harness-cask have not much to do with seamanship; but one may excuse a

shipmaster of the old school for take a very gloomy view of the

contemporary marine when he meet a man hold a master mariner’s

certificate, ignorant of the receptacle in which Jack’s salt horse be

keep when he be at sea. Most of the steamer nowadays be monkey-rig,

many of they with pole-mast, which be useful mainly as derrick upon

which a little bit of fore and aft canvas will be hoist to steady the

vessel. what should man who serve in such ship know about go aloft?

even a landsman may comprehend the emotion excite in a seaman who have

pass his life in sailing ship when he see sailor without any spar

or rig to attend to, and with nothing to do but to wash deck down.

nearly all the work of the traditional mariner lie aloft, and to

reflect upon Jack without dead-eye to turn in, chafing gear to look

after, reef-point to knot, rig to tar, mast to stay, studdingsail

gear to reeve, and the like, be almost as confound as to think of he

sleeping aft, eat fresh meat throughout the run, and go to the

steward for a can of filter water, instead of to the capstan for his

eight bell caulker of fiery black rum. no doubt thing be pleasanter

as they be. it must be nice to turn in with the certainty of have the

whole of your watch below, instead of go to bed in your sea-boot in

readiness for the thundering of a handspike, and the cruel roar of “all

hand shorten sail!” and yet, to the true sailor, go aloft be so much

the part of his life, it be so complete a condition of his vocation,

that when such a man find himself aboard a steamer with nothing to take

notice of above his head, it may be suppose that at the first go off

he be as fully bewildered as a steamer’s man--that is, a man who have

never serve in anything but steamer--would be among the rope of a

full-rig ship, take aback with her stud sail abroad. he will

miss the old song at the reef tackle, the flapping of canvas, the thud

of coil of halliard and clew-line fling down on deck, the spring

into the shroud, the helter-skelter for the weather eare, or the

ascent of the topgallant mast that jump to the flogging of the

clew-up sail.



there be a touch of wild excitement in go aloft in heavy weather,

which no seaman can be insensible to; just as in a calm day or night a

man may find a strange pleasure in linger a few moment aloft after

he have do his work, and look down. the labour of reefing have be

greatly diminish by the double topsail yard, which halve the great

sail, so that when the halliard of the upper yard be let go, the

ship be under close-reef topsail. moreover, there be only half the

weight of the sail to handle in reef or stowing. this valuable

contrivance make the task of shorten sail light in comparison with

what the labour be in the day of the whole topsail. old seaman will

remember what that kind of canvas involve in a ship of fourteen or

fifteen hundred ton, man by about eighteen or twenty man, capable of

do sailor’ work aloft.



it be the second dog watch. the royal and mizzen-topgallant sail have

be furl, but the wind come in freshen puff, the sky have a

menacing look away out on the starboard beam, and at eight bell all

hand be keep on deck to roll up the mainsail and topgallant sail, and

tie a single reef in the fore and mizzen topsail. the sea wash

noisily against the weather bow, and the night settle down as black as

a pocket; but the ship be tolerably snug, there be no great weight of

wind as yet, and the watch below be dismiss to the forecastle. they

have be an hour in their hammock or bunk, when, on a sudden, the

scuttle be rudely fling open, and a loud cry summon they on deck. they

be up in a moment, scarcely wait to pull on their jacket, for the

instant they be awake they perceive that the vessel be on her beam

end, and they can hear the thunder of a gale of wind rage overhead.

all three topsail halliard have be let go, and the watch be yell

out at the reef-tackle, the skipper shout at the mizzen-rigging, the

chief mate bawl from the break of the poop, and the second mate and

boatswain roaring in the waist and on the forecastle. the sea be fly

heavily over the weather rail of the prostrate ship, and add its

peculiar burst noise to the din of the furiously-shake canvas, to

the deafening booming of the wind, and the hoarse long-draw cry of

the sailor haul upon the rope. you can barely see the weather

shroud, though to leeward their black line be plain enough against

the washing height of foam which swell up as high as the rail of the

bulwark. you do not feel the force of the gale until you be in the

rigging, and then for a spell the iron-hard pressure of it pin you

against the shroud as if you have be make a spread-eagle. the rain

drive along in slash horizontal line, and you see the sparkle of

the deluge over the skylight where the light of the cabin lamp be

shine; or, maybe, the gale be charge with sleet and hail, and the

cold so tauten your finger that you can scarcely curl they to the

shape of the rope you grasp. over the top you swarm in company with the

rest of your watch, perhaps get a blow on the head from the heel of

some fellow above you as you lay yourself backwards to swing over the

futtock shroud; and then, find the weather side of the topsail yard

with as many hand on it as be need, you pass over to leeward, where

you find the boatswain or third mate astride of the yard-arm, ready for

the cry of “haul out to leeward,” to pass the earing. at such a time as

this a man have too much to do to look about he; the ship be bring

close to shake the sail, that the man may get the reef-band against

the yard, otherwise the canvas stand out to the force of the gale in a

surface as round as St. Paul’s dome, and so hard and tense that it would

serve as a platform for a ball-room.



in the whole-topsail day I have see half a dozen man stand upon the

canvas in the sling and quarter try to stamp the sail down to bring

the reef point within reach without so much as dinte the wind-swell

convexity. still it be possible to knot a reef-point, and take a look

round and below. it be a wonderful scene; no landsman can conceive of

its wild and awful majesty. the ship surge heavily through the black

heaving, and with every headlong plunge fill a wide circumference of

the far-down ebony water with a furious swirling of foam, in the midst

of which her long narrow shape be distinctly visible. Overhead be a dim

vision of naked spar and yard, reel in the boisterous void in whose

gloom it be just possible to trace the outline of huge black cloud

rush past like fold of swiftly-carry smoke. the yard on which you

stand be at an angle of thirty or thirty-five degree, and every

lean-down of the slender fabric that support the immense superstructure

of mast threaten to submerge the point of it, astride of which--ride

it as a horse--sit the seaman who take the lee-earing; and his figure

and that of the fellow beneath swinge on the flemish-horse, and those

of the row of man who overhang the yard, and who chorus with a kind of

shriek that ring athwart the yelling of the gale to the cry from the

weather yard-arm of “light over to windward!” be mark like

pen-and-ink drawing upon white paper against the snow of the sea which

stretch from the ship’s side into the darkness.



but this be only one aspect of “go aloft.” another--if the bowsprit

and jibboom may be include under the head of the word “aloft”--is that

of lay out to furl, let I say the outer jib, when it have come on to

blow hard enough to make the stowing of that sail necessary. from the

masthead you see the ship under you; you can watch her hull fly

through the sea, mark the glorious white of the foam that burst from

her bow and race in a broad band astern, and behold the ship in her

noble solitude amid the tenantless world of water whose pale green

skirt lean against the hazy azure of the remote heaven. but on the

jibboom you have the ship rush at you, as it be; her cutwater seem

to bear right down upon you; you see her copper forefoot gleam with

a greenish tinge through the glass-clear water whose surface it divide

into two feather-shape fountain, whose seethe and hiss and

prismatic summit arch away from the glossy bend. and now, as she dip

with a glorious rush into the hollow over whose yawning gloom you be

poise as you overhang the jibboom, the half-bury bow break the sea

into smoke, and yeast, and snow; the white and hiss mass, splendid

with sunshine or render more vivid yet by the dark green of the sea

along which it be send roll, roar and run ahead of the ship as far

as the fly jibboom, where its impetus fail and the soar vessel

swing over it, rise almost noiselessly over the thick froth, and in a

breath it be pass, whilst you look down along the slope deck from

the fork-up boom and mark how like a creature of instinct the noble

ship seem to be gather herself together for the next headlong jump,

her copper shine to windward, her black side lustrous as a curry

hide with the whirling spray, her lean mast full of thunder on high,

the white sail hard and still as carven marble, no sound reach you

but the regular wash of the spurned and trample water under the bow,

the rude and clear moaning of the wind in the rigging, and the

complaining of massive timber as the stem of the ship lengthen in a

steady upheaval, and then crush down until the torn and sob

billow of foam be flash their white feather over the head-board.

or jump aloft to loose, let we say, the mizzen royal after the tropical

squall have go away to leeward, and leave the clear moon shine in a

purify heaven of indigo, and strike a cone of silver glory in the

dark sea whose northern water be stud with flake of light from the

great star. it be the middle watch; you have overhaul your clewline,

the yard have be hoist over your head, you come down the topgallant

rigging into the crosstree, and linger there a few moment. all be

silent on deck; the helmsman stand motionless at the wheel; you hear

the faint jar of the tiller chain; you mark the delicate nimbus of

light round the binnacle hood. nowhere be the mystery work by the

magical beam of the moon feel so much as at sea. the pearl-like

radiance steep the fabric of the ship in an atmosphere of soft light as

illusive as the cloud of phosphorescent fire which break from her

side as she lean with the swell. the movement of the sail be like

the flapping of phantom wing; and not a sigh of air, not a sound of

chafing rope, not a voice call suddenly from the distant deck, but

seem to take from the moonlight and the measureless and impenetrable

space of the deep, and the immense and enfolding silence of those

far-off water, a character of unreality that make they seem the very

phantasm and mockery of the thing they veritably be.



a man might linger a long hour at the altitude of the crosstree among

the shadow of the moonlit, placid ocean night without weariness.

well than the lofty and loneliest cliff be the mast head of a ship

for the surveyal of the sublime and mighty surface on which she float,

for you rock in unison with the breathing of the deep; you be upon she

great heart, and every beat of it be mark by a stately motion of the

tower mast against the star; phosphoric outline of huge fish haunt

the sluggish wake; or a sound as of a long, deep-draw respiration

denote the neighbourhood of a leviathan whose vast proportion, as they

heave in the broad silver stream of moonlight, resemble the hull of a

ship keel up, drive to the surface by some hide power and slowly

settle downward again.



these be some of the excitement and some of the quiet pleasure of

“go aloft.” it be, no doubt, a highly sentimental view of the duty,

and sailor who have have to let go the reef point, and beat their hand

against the yard to drive life enough into their finger to enable they

to hold on, may consider that a very different representation of that

kind of work would recommend itself a good deal more than this to their

experience. very possibly. but retrospection be apt to make we tender;

and since “go aloft” must in the course of time--unless the

shipbuilder change their mind--become a thing of the past, it be worth

while spend a few minute in try to discover what there be of

poetry and the picturesque in that old obligation of the marine life in

the discharge of which the english sailor have always prove a shine

example to all mariner. even now--in these day when the steam-engine

have so eat into our maritime habit that a sailing-ship be look upon

as a kind of wonder of other time--do we not find Jack do honour to

his queen by stand erect upon the main truck? but, oh! master

mariner, mate, boatswain, and able seaman, all you who have

youngster under your charge or among you as shipmate, have mercy upon

the timid lad, give he time to feel his way aloft, show he the

lubber’s hole, and remember that many a first-rate sailor have falter

at the outset, and gaze with horror and despair at those giddy height

whose summit seem to his boyish gaze to pierce the sky.









_a TRICK at the wheel._





I remember a seaman, who have serve for year both in sailing and

steamship, tell I that never in all his life do he remember the

like of the impression produce upon he one night when he be at the

helm of a large ocean passenger steamer. he describe the darkness; the

occasional scattering of red spark blow low down upon the sea on the

lee beam; the glimmer of white water here and there out in the windy

gloom; the silence aboard the vessel, disturb only by the muffle

beating of the engine and the seething of water washing in snow from

under the bow; and he tell I how all these thing, combine with the

thought that under his foot there lie sleep a whole crowd of man and

woman, make he feel as though he and the ship and the great wind-sweep

shadow through which she be speed, be portion of a phantom world,

and that nothing be real and sentient but the compass, whose

illuminate card stand out upon the gloom like a composant at a ship’s

yard-arm.



I can conceive of many a strange, fanciful thought come into a

sailor’s mind as he stand grasp the wheel in the lonely night watch,

and I say this with a plentiful knowledge of the seaman’s prosaic and

unsentimental character. a man must be but a very short way remove from

a four-footed animal not to feel at time the wonderful and subdue

spell which the ocean will fling over the human soul; and be at the

wheel will give he the good chance of yield to the nameless

witchery, for at such a time--in most case--he be alone, no one accost

he, the gloom fall down and blot out the figure of the officer of the

watch, and complete the deep sense of solitude that be to be get from a

spell at the helm on a dark and quiet night at sea. I cannot but think

that the spirit of the deep be bring, at such a time, near to you

aboard a sailing than aboard a steamship. the onward-rush fabric that

be impel by engine demand incessant vigilance; she may be off her

course even in the time that a man take to lift his eye to mark a

fly meteor; there be no moment of rest. but in a sailing-ship you

have the moonlit night and burnish swell heaving up in line of ebony

out of the visionary horizon, where the star be wanly wink, until

it roll in billow of sparkle quicksilver under the wake of the bland

and beautiful luminary; there be not a breath of air aloft, though

little creeping of wind circle softly about the deck as the pallid

surface of canvas swing in and out with the leaning of the ship; the

moonlight lie in pool of light upon the plank, and every shadow cast

upon those pearl-like surface be as black and sharp and clear as a

trace in ink; the after portion of the sail be dark as bronze, but

look at they forward they rise into the air like piece of white

satin, soar into a stately edifice full of delicate hurrying shadow

which resemble the streaky lustre on the inside of an oyster-shell as

the cloth swell out or hollow in with the drowsy motion, and crown

with the little royal, which seem to melt even as the eye watch they

like summer cloud upon the heaven of star.



moment of such repose as this you will get in a sailing-ship. who that

have stand at the wheel at such a time but remember the soft patter of

reef-point upon the canvas, the frosty twinkling of the dew upon the

skylight and rail, the hollow sob of the swell under the counter as the

ship heave her stern, and the tiller-chain rattle, and the wheel jump

to the echo of the groan of the rudder-head? it be the middle watch;

eight bell be strike a quarter of an hour since; the watch on deck

be forward, coil away anywhere, and nothing stir on the forecastle;

the officer on duty walk the starboard side of the deck, for the yard

be brace to port, and that make a weather deck where the mate be

pacing, sleepily scratch the back of his head, and cast drowsy

glance aloft and at the sea. the moon be low in the west, and have

change her silver into copper, and will be go soon. the calm be

wonderfully express by the reflection she drop; the mirror radiance

stream towards you like a river of pallid gold, narrow at the horizon

and broaden, fan-shape, until it seem within a biscuit’s throw of

the ship, where it vanish in a fine haze; but on either hand of it the

water be as black as ink, while the lustre of the moon have quench the

star all about she, and leave the sky in which she hang as dark as the

ocean.



the set orb carry the mind with it. the eye will seek the light,

and it be a kind of instinct that make a man watch the sinking of the

moon at sea, when there be a deep repose in the air and nothing to

hinder his thought from follow the downward-sailing orb. many a time

have I watch she, and think of the old home she would be shine

upon; the love scene she would be make beautiful with her holy

light. there be nothing in life that give one such a sense of

distance, of infinite remoteness, as the setting of the sun or moon at

sea. it define the immeasurable league of water which separate you

from those you love with a sharpness that be scarcely feel at other

time. it be the only mark upon the circle of the ocean, and court you

into a reckoning which there be something too vague in the bare and

infinite horizon to invite. as one bell strike the moon rest her low

limb upon the horizon, and her reflection shorten away from the ship’s

side as the red fragment of disc sink behind the black water-line. in a

few second nothing but a speck of light that glow like a live ember be

visible; and when that be quench the faint saffron tinge that hung

about the sky when the moon be set die out and the whole

circumference of the ocean be full of the blackness of night.



the ship make but a ghostly shape. the star be there, but a haze

float like a veil under they; the diamond-dust that glitter in the

hollow cavern of the firmament be eclipse, and the planet be rayless

and sickly in their define and blueish-colour form. a fold of deep

darkness seem to have sweep along in the wake of the vanish moon, and

the officer of the watch come up to the binnacle take a brief look at

the card, and then go to the quarter and stand there softly

whistle, while the canvas aloft echo with a loud note, and the

rolling of the ship break the water under her counter into foam that

seethe sharply and expire quickly. black as the water be out on the

starboard bow you notice a shadow upon it that give a fresh shade, a

further profundity, to the jetty obscurity, and in a few moment the

sail aloft fall asleep as though the wand of a magician have be wave

over the sway spar and a soft air come blow over the rail.



“all aback forrard!” ring out a hoarse voice, and the cry find an

echo in the hollow canvas. the mate run along the deck bawl out

order to flatten in the head-sheet and square the after yard, and so

forth; the man come out of a dozen corner, coil of rigging be fling

down, song be raise, sheave squeal as the yard be swung, topsail

sheet rattle, and all be bustle and hurry. meanwhile the wind freshen

with a moan in the gathering gust, and the ship lean under it as her

headsail fill, and she pay off. presently the yard be braced round,

the vessel bring to her course, and the wind be find to be a point

free. the deck be still full of life, tack have to be board, “small

pull” be want here and there, and the run gear have to be coil

away; the light from the binnacle lamp puzzle your eye, and when you

lift your gaze from the illuminated card the darkness seem to stand

around you like a wall; but the compass be there to tell you that the

ship head her course. you would know with your eye blindfold, by the

mere feel of the helm, that everything be draw, and amidst the call

of the mate and the song of the sailor you can hear the sloppy sound

of flat fall of water under the weather bow, and the hiss of explode

bubble, and the faint wash of froth churn up by the rudder below you.



two bell be strike, and all be quiet once more. the skipper have be

on deck, talk with the mate, push his bronzed face betwixt you and

the binnacle, and after a few turn and several prolonged look aloft

and around the sea, have go below again. the wind have steadily

freshen, and the ship, under all plain sail, heel amid the darkness

like a lean column of white vapour. so softly she sweep through the

snow with which she girdle she shapely length, curtsey with queenly

grace as she run over the long-draw undulation out of whose inky

coil the wind be strike phosphoric spark, that she steer herself;

you have nothing to do but keep hold of the spoke, and let the breeze

blow the noble fabric along. the deep gloom be full of strange sound

now that the seaman be forward, and all be silent aft.



a spirit-like minstrelsy echo down from the glimmer incline

height like a far-off chorus of human voice; the wind be full of the

mysterious sound. it do not appear to come from the ship, but from a

group of invisible ghostly creature sail through the air over the

masthead, and set the moaning voice and sob wash of the ocean

to melody which may easily seem to make this darkness belong to the

night of a world people by phantom and creature without similitude in

human knowledge. Hark! how plaintive be the song of the bow-wave that

fall in an arch of green fire from the shearing stem, and roll aft in

a white swirl, interlace with fitful and sullen flash of phosphoric

light! but the breeze freshen yet; you cannot count a dozen star in

the void of gloom overhead, the music aloft take a clear note,

strain sound be audible as the pass swell roll the ship to

windward, the white water under the main sheet rise close to the

scupper and flash fast and far from under the counter into the

blackness over the stern. an order be sharply bawl out, and some hand

come tumbling aft and jump into the mizzen rigging to roll up the

cross-jack. a hoarse song reach you from the forecastle as the fly

jib downhaul be man, and at the same moment the fore and mizzen royal

halliard be let go. you hear this canvas flap in the gloom amid

the chorus of the man on the crossjack yard as they trice up the bunt.

there must be no more wool-gathering with you now. the wheel be give

you as much work as you want; every now and again a smart kick stiffen

your arm into iron, and you begin to feel that your jacket will have to

come off soon.



for some time nothing more be do, but the watch keep on their foot and

stand about ready for the next call, which they know will not be long

delay. the sea to windward be full of white glancing, and the

break head make a vague light of their own which give you a sight

of the water for some distance. the canvas that have be take off the

ship count for nothing; the main royal be still on she, and she be

heel over like a race yacht, strike the bow swell with a stem

that hiss like red-hot iron, and shatter the coil of liquid jet

into foam, which widen out on either hand of she into a storm of snow,

in the midst of which the fly hull of the vessel be as clearly trace

as be the shadow of her rigging in the moonlight, while her iron-hard

distend canvas be full of the low thunder of the pour blast, and

her forecastle be dark with fly spray that sweep over the rail and

strike the deck like a hail storm. it be noble sailing, and this

booming and hoot ocean night wind be something to be make the most of

while it last; but it give you at the wheel as much occupation as you

relish. it be like draw tooth to “meet she” as the swell sweep the

ship round; and at last the captain, who be again on deck, and who have

be stand at the binnacle for five minute, sing out for another

hand to come aft to the wheel. a figure tumble along in a hurry and

station himself to leeward of you; and thereupon your work, though it

be by no mean half as easy again, become considerably light than it

be. “hold on a minute, Bill,” say your mate, and he feel over his

pocket for a chew of tobacco. the quid find and properly stow away in

his cheek, your companion resume his grasp of the wheel, and in the

haze of the binnacle lamp you may see his leathern jaw work like an

old cow chew the cud as he mumble over the black fragment, sometimes

direct a doleful squint at the compass, sometimes look astern,

while he help you to put the wheel up or down, that you may keep the

course swinge fair with the lubber’s mark.



but the ship be be overdrive. at one bell it be a dead calm; it be

not three bell yet, and here be the sea white with wind, and the vessel

roar through the smother with the blast thunder like a hurricane

in the sail.



    “in main royal and mizzen topgallant sail.”



the canvas rattle like an old waggon over a stony street as the

clewline be man, and whilst furl it the foretopgallant halliard

be let go. what other sail be take in you do not know, for the ship

want much clever watching, and the skipper be at hand to bring you up

with a round turn if the vessel should be a quarter of a point off her

course. be ease, she steer more comfortably, but whole topsail and

course and main-topgallant sail be rush she through it fiercely;

the water on her lee quarter be pretty nearly as high as her main brace

bumpkin, and the billow there go along with she as if it be a part

of the vessel; the main tack groan under the tear and rend pull

of the huge convex surface of canvas; now and again the blow of the

swell which the race vessel hit laterally make her tremble fore and

aft like a house under a clap of thunder. but she be to have all she can

bear; the spell of dead calm be to be atone for; and so on through the

shrill and echo darkness rush the great fabric, sweep her

pallid canvas through the fold of gloom like the pinion of some vast

spirit of the deep, make the water roar past she as she go, break

the dark swell into fire and foam as she rush through the liquid

acclivity with her powerful stem, with note of mad laughter and

lamentable wail in her rigging, and with streaming deck which

hollowly echo the fall of the solid body of water which shoot up just

before the weather fore-rigging, and roll in a rush of cream white

into the lee scupper as far aft as the break of the poop.



at last you hear the welcome sound of four bell; your trick be up, the

wheel be relieve, and catch your jacket off the grating abaft the

helm you walk forward, wipe the perspiration from your forehead; and,

drop down the fore scuttle, grope about for your pipe, which you

light at the slush lamp that swing from a grimy beam, and return on

deck squat somewhere out of the way of the wind and wet, earnestly

hope that if it be to be a case of “reef topsail” there will be time

for you to have your smoke out before the order be thunder forth.









_the bailiff AT SEA._





some time ago I hear that a bailiff have be carry off to sea whilst

in the execution of his duty. anxious to learn the nature of his voyage,

how he fare, and what condition he be in, mentally and physically,

when restore to his anxious relative, I make inquiry, and my

diligence be at last reward by meet the mate of the vessel that

have sail away with the man. truth oblige I to own that this mate be

not what might be consider a very gentlemanly person. it be not his

velvet waistcoat, nor a rather vicious squint, nor a striking-look

bald head ring with a layer of red hair like a grummet of rope yarn;

the want of genteelness be noticeable in his abundant use of what be

call “langwidge.” “if I be a bailiff,” think I, as I glance at

his immense hand and huge arm which swell out his coat-sleeve like

the wind in a sailor’s smallcloth dry in a strong breeze on the

forestay, “I should not like to be put ‘in possession’ of a house

occupy by _you_, my hearty.”



I take a seat opposite he and say, “so you’re the mate of the vessel

that steal away the county court man?”



“right,” say he look at I, without a move in his face; “but don’t

you go and say that I’m the mate as give he up again. if I’d have my

way he’d be in charge o’ any good he might have come across in the

inside of a whale by this time. I’d ha’ chuck he overboard, as sure

as that there hand’s on this table,” and down come a very leg-of-mutton

of a fist with a blow that jerk his tumbler into the air. it be as

good as a hint that the glass want fill; and when this be do, my

companion open the top button of his warm and tight velvet waistcoat,

and compose himself into a posture for conversation.



“how,” ask I, “come your skipper to have a bailiff aboard his vessel?”



“you may ask how,” he growl; “what I say be, what right have he to

come? I’ve get nothing to say against the law as it work for they as

live ashore--for they as be in fix house, and cann’t sail away with

any bloom old rag of a chap, in a greasy coat, as come in with a bit

of paper, and take a cheer, and say, ‘here I sit, mate, till I’m pay

off.’ but what have the like of such scowbank get to do with sailor man

when once they’re aboard? what I say be, that when a man’s on the water,

his chest stow away, article sign, all the law that consarn he be

in the cabin. the capt’n’s the law; and not only the law, but judge,

magistrate, bailiff, husher, registrar, high chancellor, and Lord Mayor

o’ London on top of it; and my argument be that any man as take the

liberty to walk over a wessel’s side and order the captain about, and

sing out contrairy order, and threaten to have he purge (I hear that

very word. ‘Ye’ll have to purge for this,’ say the bailiff. do ye ever

hear such language apply to a captain?)--any man, I say, as take such

a liberty as that ought to be drop overboard without ask ‘by your

leave,’ and, as I say before, leave to take possession of any good he

come across in the inside of a fish or at the bottom of the ocean.”



I wait until he have partially quench his excitement by a long pull

at his tumbler, and then ask he again how it happen that his vessel

have be board by a bailiff.



“I’ll tell you,” he answer. “the wessel be a brig of 300 ton. come

home she plump into a schooner. it be the schooner’s fault; we sing

out to she to get out of the road; instead of do which she port she

helm as if to provoke we, and in we go, do she a deal of damage and

carry away our own jibboom. well, we arrive in port and discharge,

and then fill up again with coal. it be toosday afternoon, the sky

middle dirty, and a fresh breeze of wind blow. we haul out and

lie at a mooring buoy, wait for the tide to serve. I be talk to

the captain when I take notice of a boat come along, row by a couple

o’ waterman, and a chap in a chimbley-pot hat sit in the starn

sheet.



“‘be that boat for we?’ say the captain, look.



“‘why,’ I say, ‘it look as if she mean to run we down. be it a wager?

bust I if hever I see waterman pull like that afore!’



“they be drag on their oar as if they would spring ’em, lie

back until nothing but their nose be to be see above the gunwale, and

make the water fly in cloud over the cove in the starn as if prompt

drowning be too good for he, and he be to be smother slow. they

dash alongside, hook on, and the fellow in the chimbley-pot hat

come scrape over the rail, shake himself free o’ the water as he

tumble on to the deck like a Newfoundland dog.



“‘just in time, captain,’ say he, with an impudent kind o’ smile,

rummage in his side-pocket; and with that he hout with a sort of

dockiment, and hand it to the skipper.



“‘what’s this?’ say the skipper, smell round the paper as it might

be, but never offer to touch it.



“‘only a horder for you to return to the bosom of your family,’ he say,

‘as the date o’ your sailing’s not yet fix.’



“‘ben’t it?’ say the captain, breathe short. ‘who be you, and what

d’ye want?’



“‘I’m a bailiff,’ say the man; ‘and I’m here to take charge o’ this

wessel, pende the haction that’s be enter against she in the

Hadmiralty side o’ the County Court by the schooner as ye be in

collision with.’



“‘can ye swim?’ ask the captain.



“‘never you mind whether I can or not,’ say the bailiff, look round

at we, for all hand be collect and listen their hard.



“‘because,’ say the captain, ‘if you cann’t swim you’d well turn to

and hail that boat to come back again and put ye ashore.’



“‘no, no,’ say the bailiff, ‘I’m not go ashore, my friend. I’m here

to take charge o’ this brig and stop she from go to sea.’



“have the captain choose then and there to give order for that bailiff

to be drop overboard, I believe I’m the man as would have execute

the command. take the temper I be then in, I don’t know anything that

would ha’ give I more satisfaction to perform. the aggravation of

be stop when we be all ready to get away be the least part of

it: it be the bailiff’s cool grin, the impudence in his eye as he

look round, as much as to say, ‘all what I see be mine,’ his take

the skipper’s place and say ye shan’t do this, and I willn’t allow

that, that make I want to lay hand upon he. the captain stare at he

a bit, as if consider what he should do; then turn to I, he ask

I the time. I tell he.



“‘in another quarter of an hour,’ say he, ‘loose the torpsail and make

ready to get away.’



“‘you’d well not,’ say the bailiff; ‘it’ll be gross contempt of court

if you do.’



“‘Court!’ say the skipper, ‘Court! there be no court here, Mr. Bailiff.

this be a brig, not a court. don’t talk of court to I. the gross

contempt be of your committing. how dare you stand there order of

I?’



“‘rest assure,’ say the bailiff, ‘you’ll be punish if you don’t do

what I say. you’ll have to purge in open court, and that’s a job that

may cost ye enough to lay you up in the union for the rest of your

natural day.’



“‘stow that,’ say I, double up my fist and step close to the

fellow; ‘if the captain stand that kind o’ jaw, _i_ willn’t.’



“‘I’m here in the hexecution of my duty,’ say the bailiff, drop his

confident grin, and begin to grow whitish. ‘whatever you do

contrairy to my order you’ll do at your peril.’



“and so say he walk right aft, and sit on the taffrail with his

arm fold.



“never be any quarter of an hour long than that which the captain

tell I to wait. I have my watch in my hand, and all the time I be

afraid the skipper would change his mind and give in to the bailiff, who

sit aft with his hat over his ear, look at the shore with his little

eye.



“‘time’s up, sir!’ I bawl to the captain.



“‘loose the torpsail,’ he sing out, and in a moment all hand be

run about, sheet home, and yell out at the rope, be as

much afeard as I be that if we be not quick the sight of the dog

bailiff ’ud operate upon the skipper’s hintellect and stop our just

rewenge upon that funkshonary’s audacity. the bailiff see the man at

work, tumble off the taffrail and comes run forrard.



“‘D’ye mean to say you don’t intend to obey the law?’ he shout out,

hold on to his chimbley-pot.



“‘out of the way!’ answer the skipper, ‘there’s no room for law here.

we’re full up, mate; and since ye’re bind for a voyage, blow your nose

and wave your hand to they as ye’re a parting from!’ and, as he say

this, the wessel, catch the wind that be come strong enough to

make nothing above our topsail necessary, lay down to it, and we head

for the open water.



“I see the bailiff stare wildly around he, as if he really _would_

jump overboard, and it be worth a month’s pay to see he look like

that, and hold his hat on.



“‘why, man,’ he shout to the captain, ‘you’re never in earnest: d’ye

know what you’re a doing of?’ and, find that the skipper take no

notice, he call out to the man, ‘you’ll work this vessel at your peril

if you obey your captain. my order be to stop this brig, and if you

don’t allow I to execute my duty----’ but just as he come to this the

wessel meet the first of the sea which be roll outside the

harbour--stiff sea they wo, for it be blow half a gale o’ wind;

she put her nose into it, and then roll over, fit to bring she low

yardarm into the water; away fly the bailiff’s chimbley-pot hat clean

overboard, and ye may boil I alive if I don’t think he mean to follow

it; for the send o’ the wessel trip he over the weather hatch

coaming, and he seem to shoot--ay, as neatly as if he’d be kick

by one of they giant I use to read about when I be a little

’un--clean into the lee scupper, where he lie stunned as I think,

until all on a sudden he jump up and go claw along till he come

to the lee o’ the after deck house, where he squat down, look with

his yaller face and blow hair like a Madagascar monkey recover

from a fit of intoxication.”



here my companion break into a loud laugh, which he repeat again and

again, as if the thought awaken in his mind be of too exquisite a

kind to be dismiss with a single guffaw.



“I don’t know,” he continue, after a bit, wipe his eye, and then

fix his dismal and malignant squint upon I, “whether on the whole we

should ha’ do well by drop he overboard. the brig be as deep

as pretty nigh twice her tonnage in coal could make she; she be a wet

boat at any time; but now she tumble about as if she have make up her

mind to drown herself. I reckon she know she have a bailiff aboard. every

dip forrard throw the water over her head in ocean; she’d roll to

wind’ard almost as heavily as to leeward, so that the deck be all

awash, and I be look and hope all the time to see the bailiff

fetch away. but there be enough law leave in he to keep he hold on.

I be stand to wind’ard of the house--the skipper be aft agin the

wheel--when Mr. Bailiff come staggering round, his breech cling to

his leg like wet brown paper, and his shoe full o’ water.



“‘hallo, shipmate!’ I sing out, see he make for the cabin door,

‘where be you bind to? ben’t you happy where you be?’



“‘I’m go to lie down on one of the locker,’ say he. ‘I feel half

froze, and I shall be sick presently.’



“‘you may be half froze and sick too,’ say I, ‘but smother I, Mr.

Bailiff, if you shall use the cabin.’



“‘not use the cabin?’ say he, gape at I, and talk as if there be

something in his swaller; ‘d’ye mean to keep I on deck all night?’



“‘don’t ask no question,’ say I. ‘you’re here by french leave. nobody

want you. if I have my way you’d be tow astern, with your neck in a

bowline; and if all the rest o’ your tribe and the blooming ’tornie you

sarve be tail on in your wake, I’d be willing to woyage round the

world, and never grumble if we take year in reach home.’



“I be in a passion, which rise my woice, and the skipper, hear I,

come over.



“‘Hallo, bailiff!’ say he, cheerfully; ‘not drown yet, my lad? what

d’ye think o’ the weather?’



“‘captain,’ say the man, ‘you’ve carry I away by force. D’ye mean to

freeze I to death by keep I on deck all night? your mate here say

I’m not to use the cabin.’



“‘why should he use it, capt’n?’ say I. ‘could a sailor man sit with

the like of he? I’ve mess afore now with chaneyman; I’ve sleep along

with peruvian beachcomber when the air’s be that thick with the smell

of onion ye might have leant agin it; but ye may boil I, skipper,’

say I, ‘if ever I occupy a cabin along with a bailiff afore, and if

he’s to share that crib along with we, I’ll sleep forrard.’



“‘you hear that, bailiff,’ say the skipper. ‘I cann’t let my mate live

forrard to oblige you. if you’re cold I dare say the cook ’ll let you

warm yourself in the galley. but nobody want you here. you be not

invite, consequently it’s not for you to grumble if you don’t find

yourself perfectly comfortable and happy.’



“but as he say this, Nature fall to manhandle the bailiff as if she’d

take his own trade upon herself, and make one rush he lie over the

lee rail so ill that I never see the equal of it, even in a Frenchman;

he twist himself about just as if he’d be revolve on a corkscrew;

the water blow over the forward weather rail hit he neatly, and he

be like a stream rag in five minute.



“we leave he enjoy himself and go on with our work. it be fall

dark, and not only blow hard, but there be the look of a whole gale

of wind in the south-west sky. the brig be make desperate bad weather

of it under low torpsail and reef foresail, take in the water fit

to wash every movable thing overboard, and shove through it very

slowly with a surprising sag to leeward. the skipper go below for some

supper, and after a bit he call I in.



“‘where’s the bailiff?’ say he.



“‘don’t know exactly,’ I say. ‘to leeward somewhere. there’s a figure

half over the rail just abaft the fore rigging, if that’s he.’



“‘I’ve be tarne it over in my mind,’ say the skipper, ‘and I’ve get

a notion, William,’ say he, ‘that we’d ha’ do well not to bring

that bailiff along with we.’



“‘but he wouldn’t go ashore when you tell he,’ say I.



“‘quite true,’ say the captain; ‘but that willn’t make it well for we.

after all, the law’s not a thing ye can take liberty with, and there’s

something in his threat of make I purge in open court, William,’ say

he, ‘which mightn’t matter if I know what it mean; but, be ignorant,

I’m willing to think it alarm.’



“‘Pooh,’ say I, ‘it’s only a lawyer’s word. there’s nothing in it.

they use onintelligible word to scare plain man; but there cann’t be

anything more terrifying in language ye don’t understand than in

language ye do.’



“‘I wish I have some book aboard that ’ud explain that word,’ say he.

‘the bailiff’ll know; but I’ll not ask he for fear he should think I

afraid. but we cann’t let he starve. well send he here and let he

get something to eat.’



“I be go to argue, but he wouldn’t listen.



“‘no, no,’ say he, ‘send he here;’ and I know by that that the fear o’

the law be begin to master he.



“well, it be my duty to obey, so I go on deck, and after rummage

about I find the bailiff sit up to his hip in water against the

scuttle-butt abreast of the galley.



“‘come along,’ say I, ‘supper’s in the cabin, and the captain want you

there.’



“he stand up, but be so cramped in his timber that he could scarcely

shuffle along, and I have to drag he by the collar. when the captain see

by the lamplight the plight the fellow be in, his heart fail he

altogether. there be no more proper dignified scorn.



“‘why,’ say he, look at he, ‘I don’t think it be such a bad job

as that,’ and he jump up and fetch he a suit of dry clothe, and

then pour out a dose of brandy. this be regular knuckling under. he

have go on con-sidering and con-sidering until he be in an out-and-out

funk. there be no use in my say anything. the bailiff have growd on a

sudden to become the strong man aboard that brig, though as for I,

when I tell you that have I be the captain I’d have send the fellow

aloft, and keep he there all night, as a hint to leave sailor man

alone on future occasion, ye’ll allow that _my_ caving in wur only

because I ben’t skipper, and that’s all. well, sir, to cut this yarn

short, luck turn in favour of that bailiff with a wengeance. at

midnight it be blow a hurricane, and the skipper say there be no

good go on face it, he must put back.



“‘there’s a handy port,’ say I, name it, ‘than the one we’re from

to make for.’



“‘ay,’ say he, ‘but since we’re bind to up keeleg it’ll look well to

carry the bailiff slick home than to give he a railway journey.’



“it would have make a hangel growl to hear the captain, all through

fear, place this bailiff afore the werry hurricane that be blow,

and think of he only whom he’d ha’ gladly drownde a few hour

early, instead of the wessel and the life aboard she. but reasoning

be out of the question. the brig be just a smother of froth, the gale

roaring like thunder, the sea as high as our maintop, and the old

hooker shiver with every upward heave, as if she must leave all the

low part of she behind she. it be a job to get the vessel round, but

we manage it, and at half-past five o’clock in the morning we fetch

the harbour we have start from and bring up, nothing having carry

away but the bailiff’s chimbley-pot.”



“and what be the result of all this?” say I.



“why,” say he, with a loud rumble laugh, “the skipper have to find out

what purging in hopen court mean. he be bring up afore an old

gentleman, who lecture he for about half an hour, say that the law

be mean to be respect and that it would be a bad job for any man as

sneer at it; and after having talk out all that lay in his mind, he

up and fine the captain ten pound and fifty shilling cost. it

serve he right. he’d no business to bring that bailiff back. but he

be hoperate on by the fear o’ word, and depend upon it the man who

allow that sort of alarm to wisit he be not a fit person to carry a

bailiff to sea.”









_off the HORN._





the passage of the Horn have long cease to be a thing to boast about.

Time be when a man who have double that formidable iron headland

reckon he have perform a feat that entitle he to a good deal of

respect. this be characteristically show in “two year before the

Mast,” the author of which dwell at great length upon the struggle of

the _Alert_ among the ice in latitude 58 deg. South, as though he

consider that part of the voyage to be something proper to hand on to

posterity in a bulky form. not so much notice be take of the

achievement nowadays. it still confer privilege; it qualify a man to

“spit to windward,” for instance, and no doubt it inspire many a

youthful midshipman or apprentice with much big talk and nautical air

in the presence of lad who have yet to see with their own eye what an

antarctic iceberg be like.



but the passage of the Horn be much too common an occurrence in these

day to inflate anything but a boy. in Dana’s time a ship be a

wonderful object down there; it seem almost a deserted ocean; nothing

be to be meet but an old “spouter” jogging along with stump

topgallant-mast, and her side full of boat; or a cargo-ship, with a

freight of “notion,” bind to the peruvian or mexican port. now, if it

be not so full as the Atlantic, it be pretty nearly as busy; for since

those day Australia have grow a mighty and populous continent; town

have spring up as if by magic along the western seaboard of the

Americas; even the little remote South Sea Islands have lend a hand in

the thronging of the great Cape Horn highway; and the most desolate,

sterile region in the world--such a harsh, forbidding, icebound piece of

coast as no man who have pass within sight of it can ever forget--be

skirt, week after week and year after year, by score and score of

great steamer and sailing ship, bind west, and east, and north, if

never south. the Panama Canal threaten the famous old route; and should

that waterway ever be complete, the Horn will probably fall even more

out of date than the Cape of Good Hope have. it be not to be expect,

however, that even the most ancient mariner will be find to mourn over

the desuetude. there be many uncomfortable spot to be encounter in a

voyage round the world; but a turn off the Horn, in the month which we

call summer here, probably beat anything in the shape of marine

discomfort to be find on the ocean. of course this be speak of it

as sailor find it--as it be experience by the man who have to remain

on deck, go aloft, stand at the wheel, and whose shelter be a forecastle

with the scuttle close, and not a dry stitch of clothe to be find by

groping.



for it be off the Horn where the galley-fire get wash out, and where,

therefore, the streaming and hungry watch below have nothing to eat but

what they may find in the bread-barge; where the tear freeze in a man’s

eye fast than the most pity angel of a woman live could wipe

they away; where one be glad to keep one’s sea-boot on for fear that

one’s toe may go as well as the boot when they be haul off; where

everything be like sheet and bar iron aloft; where the very cockroach

turn in to wait for the Equator, and the hardy rat be so put to it

with frost that they watch in the gloom until a man go to bed and

fall asleep, in the hope of get a meal off his nose. unhappily the

Horn do not improve. it blow and snow as hard there now as it do

when the old _wager_ round it, and when Drake or Anson be roll

among its stupendous comber. other place be more tractable. for

instance, Dana, twenty-four year after he make his memorable voyage,

find that the climate off Point Conception have alter, that the

south-easter be no long the curse of the coast, and that vessel

anchor inside at Santa Barbaro and San Pedro all the year round. no

one could have tell he this of the Horn. have he choose to beat to the

eastward or westward a second time in the month when the attempt be

make by the _Pilgrim_ and the _alert_ he would have find the same

blind snow-storm, the same hurling sea, the same sunless,

melancholy sky, the same plunging, washing, strain, roar

tumblification he record forty-two year ago. let the story of a brig

of 300 ton’ register bear witness to this.



it be in the month of May that the vessel in question be bind to

Callao with a cargo of coal, but a strong north-westerly gale have drive

her much far to the southward than the captain have any desire to

find himself. the gale leave they on a Wednesday morning, roll their

yardarm into it on a real Cape Horn swell. what be there to which to

liken these prodigious heaving? the actual altitude of those liquid

hill may seem small in comparison with the appearance they present when

view from their hollow; but whatever may be their height, to lie

dipping and wallow among they in a vessel of the tonnage of that

brig be to undergo an experience hardly less formidable than what be

devise by the Mohocks, when they shut up old woman in empty cask, and

send they spin down Ludgate Hill. what strain and groan and

complain of the torture fabric, if it be of timber! every beam,

carling, tree-nail, transom, knee, stanchion, and futtock lift up its

dismal creaking and wail voice as the bewildered craft, with she

topsail rattle in the motionless atmosphere, be swing like a pendulum

up the shoulder of the swell mass of green water, lean down as she

go until she be fairly on her beam-end, with pot and pannikin,

sea-boot and sea-chest, dish, book, furniture, and whatever else

may be inside of she, fetch away with dreadful noise to leeward, amid

a volley of sea-blessing from skipper, cook, and steward, and muffle

shout from the watch below in the forecastle.



luckily Cape Horn calm do not last very long; indeed, there be nothing

but “weather” down in those region, and a calm be only a short pause

among the gale and squall whilst they be consider whose turn it be

next. within an hour from the time of the first gale fail they,

another gale from a little to the north-of-west be bow down the

bother and beat brig, which, under low topsail and fore-topmast

staysail, manfully struggle to look up to it with her head in the

direction of Cape Horn and her wake stream away over her weather

quarter. it be one of those picture of storm which be rarely see in

like perfection out of the parallel that divide Terra del Fuego from

the South Shetlands--an ocean of mountainous sea, raise each of they

a note of thunder as their arching summit crash from a dark,

oil-smooth ridge of green water into huge avalanche of snow: a sky of

gloomy slate, along which masse of scud--tear, ragged, and

tendril-shape--be fly with incredible velocity. the horizon be

break with the incessant rising and fall of the pyramidal billow,

dark as the night, against a ring of sooty cloud, from which, ever and

anon, one would break away, like a wing messenger of evil, whiten

and veil the air with a kind of boil appearance as it sweep its

furious and blinding discharge of snow and hail along. no wonder that in

olden time the man who have pass these tempestuous and inclement sea

should have consider himself an object of importance. stand, in fancy,

upon the deck of that labour brig, and survey one of the countless

aspect of marine life. the sea be break heavily over the port bow

of the vessel, deluge she forward and race aft in a foaming torrent

as she sink her stern to mount the huge surge that almost lay she

yardarm level. the bitter, raw, flay cold of the wind there be

nothing in language to express. the fly spray smite the exposed face

like a volley of sail needle. now and again a squall of snow and hail

come along with so much fury in it that it take the breath away from

the strong of the seaman cower with their back to it. the rigging

crackle to every strain put upon it like burn wood. the snow upon

the yard make they glimmer like line of pallid light as they

furiously sway against the dismal ground of the dark and rush sky.

there be spear and arrow-head of ice upon the bulwark rail, upon the

cathead, upon the scuttle-butts lash amidship; and though the sea

repeatedly break over they they be always leave stand. the helmsman,

with his hard fist wrap up in mit, rig out in oilskin from his

head to his huge, well-grease sea-boot, and with the after-thatch of

his sou’-wester blow up by the gale, and stand out from his head

like the tail of a gull, get the full of it. nothing of the man be

visible but a fragment of mahogany face show between the flannel

ear-cover of his head-gear, and a pair of water eye, which he now

and again wipe upon his mit when a pause in the yaw and come-to’s give

he a chance to raise one of his hand from the spoke.



how would some of our summer-water mariner appear beside that

salt-water sailor be they to have stand their trick at the helm on

such an occasion as this; gaze to windward as yonder skipper be do,

hold on like grim death to a backstay, with the salt dry in

crystal in his eye; or make one of that oil-skin group there to

leeward of the galley, stamp their boot upon the deck to put life

into their frozen toe, duck as a shriek in the wind warn they of

the passage of a green sheet of water over their head, bite doggedly

upon the tobacco in their cheek, and growl as they reflect that

another three hour must elapse before they be privileged to quit the

deck and take such warmth and comfort as they may find in the

forecastle, whose darkness be scarcely reveal by the sputtering

slush-lamp, and whose beam and stanchion be decorate with drain

clothe?



it be already blow two or three ordinary gale in one, and the low

topsail be more than the brig could safely stagger under, though the

captain hold on, since by ratche to the northward he might hope to get

clear of the ice, of which, on the previous night and that morning, some

monstrous specimen have heave in view. indeed, at one bell in the

afternoon watch, during a flaw in a heavy squall of snow that be

blow in horizontal line along the sea, they catch sight on the lee

bow of the greenish marble-like glimmer of a berg that look to be a

mile long and as tall as St. Paul’s Cathedral. it vanish, but

reappear broad on the lee-beam when the squall pass, and stand out

in its complete shape against the smoke-colour gloom of the sky over

the horizon, where, though it be four or five mile off, the man on the

brig’s deck could see the white, steam-like haze of the spray that

flash in cloud from its base, and flee past it in eddying volume,

and almost imagine that they hear the thunder of the smiting surge

reverberate in the hollow and cavern of the mighty frozen mass. but

when it have draw on the lee-quarter another squall blow up and

smother it, and after that it disappear entirely.



it be at this time that the gale increase in fury, and the sea grow

terrible. the weather be enough to blow the mast out of the vessel,

and all hand be turn up to stow both topsail and bring the brig to

the wind under a small storm staysail. how be the aspect of that Cape

Horn ocean to be described?--the rage of its headlong acclivity; the

long sweep of olive-green height, piebald with hiss and seethe

track of foam, blow along their gleam side; the hard iron-grey of

the heavens, out of which the storm of wind be rush, bear upon

its wing masse of vapour, which it tear to piece in its fury; and the

cold--the pierce, poignant cold--of the gale, with its lash burden

of sleet and spray and hail?



the man have come off the yard after having struggle, each watch of

they, for hard upon three-quarter of an hour with the frozen topsail,

when the brig ship a sea just abaft the weather fore rigging. it be

a whole mountain of green water, and it fall in a dead weight of score

of ton upon the deck, beat for awhile the whole life out of the

devoted vessel, and make she pause, trembling and stun, in the

roar hollow in which it have find she, whilst above the thunder of

the dreadful stroke could be hear the crash of break wood, of

splinter glass, and the rend noise of deck furniture tear from its

strong fastening. a heavy upward send drive the water off the deck,

and all hand be find to be alive, hold on like grim death to

whatever be next they; and then it be see that a long range of the

weather-bulwark have be tear down flush with the deck, the cabin

skylight break into shiver, the long boat amidship stove, and nothing

leave of the port-quarter boat but the frame of its keel and stem,

dangle at the davit. the loss of the two boat be a bad job, but

still bad be the terrible strain the deeply freight vessel have

undergo, and the destruction of the skylight that leave the cabin open

for the flood of water that roll along the deck. the benumbed and

half-frozen crew turn to to secure what remain of the skylight and

to cover it with tarpaulin; but whilst they be in the midst of this

work the brig give a heavy lurch, which make the man believe it be all

over with she; and before a single cry could have be raise, a portion

of the weather fore rigging carry away, and in a trice the

fore-topmast break off at the cap, and fall over the side--a horrible

muddle--with all its raffle of sail, yard, and gear.



the early antarctic night be now draw down over the furious sea, and

it be already so dark that the man could hardly discern one another’s

face. some active fellow spring forward at the risk of their life to

cut away the rigging, and release the wreck alongside before the yard

upon it should pierce the brig’s bottom; and this be do, the helm
