be put hard up, with the idea of wear ship, in order to secure the

foremast. but the storm-fiend have mark this unhappy brig, and the

successive blow come thick and fast. Scarcely be the wrecked spar send

adrift and the helm shift, when all the rest of the port fore rigging

carry away, and the foremast fall down, carry with it the bowsprit,

main topmast, and a portion of the port main rigging.



by this time it be as dark as the bottom of a well; the brig wallow

before the sea with a mass of wreckage over her side, pitch

miserably in the fearful hollow, and huge surge curl their white

height around she. a man have need to be a seaman indeed, and to have a

seaman’s heart in he too, to act at all in such a moment as this. the

full extent of the mischief could not be guess. nothing be certain

but that the brig be dispossess of all but her mainmast, and that

there be some heavy spar over the side, pound at she like

batter-ram with every hurl of the rage sea. the first business

would be to get clear of this mischief, and the man go to work with

their knife, feel for the lanyard and hack and cut with a

will. darkness give a peculiar horror to disaster of this kind at sea.

in the daylight you can see what have happen; you can use your eye as

well as your hand and make despatch, and the bad be evident. but the

darkness leave everything to be guess at. you shout for help for some

job too heavy for you, and it do not come. the outline of the sea

grow colossal by the illusion of the faint light throw out from their

break crest; you cannot perceive the fly water so as to duck away

from it, and in a breath you may find yourself overboard. it be all

distraction and uproar, loud and fearful shouting, and blind groping.

when at last the wreck be clear, the vessel seem little well than

a sheer hulk, nothing stand but her mainmast, upon which the

mainyard swing helplessly. that she should have live through that long

and fearful antarctic night, the sea comb over she, iceberg in her

vicinity, and drain in water with every roll, must count among the

miracle of the deep. her people have discover that the mainmast,

have little to support it, have work loose, break away the

mast-combing, and start the planking all around it; so that through

this large aperture the water pour into the hold in torrent. the port

pump have be disable by the fall of the mast, and the only other pump

be man and work with such energy as die man will put into their

arm; but in less than an hour the coal choke it, and now nothing

remain but to lighten the vessel by throw the cargo overboard and

bale with bucket. all through those black and howl hour, amid

freezing fall of water, and in the heart of the rage Cape Horn storm,

this severe labour be pursue, so that when the bleak and melancholy

dawn break upon the desolate ocean it find the brig still afloat, and

the brave heart in her grimly fight death, though faint, famished,

and frozen. Help come shortly before noon. a sail be make out head

dead for the wreck, and by the time she be abreast, the wind and sea

have so far moderate as to enable she to bring all the man safely off.

it be not a moment too soon, for twenty minute after the crew have be

transfer to the ship the brig be observe to give a heavy lurch, and

so lie on her beam-end, never right, but slowly sink in that

position--so slowly that after her hull have vanish her mainmast

remained fork out like the lift arm of a drown man.



when this story be tell I I could not help think of what the Horn

route be in Dana’s time, and the very small chance that brig’s crew

would have have for their life have her name be the _Pilgrim_, and have

she be beat to the westward forty year ago. certain it be, that

however ship may come and go, and change the nature of their material

and the form of their fabric, the weather in the Pacific down there be

very much what it be in Anson’s time, and as it have be, in all

probability, since the creation of the world. other climate may vary in

the lapse of age, and south-easter may in place be find to work

themselves into north-wester. but the Horn remain always the same

harsh, tempestuous, frozen headland, echo at this hour the hurricane

note which reverberate over it century ago, and grimly overlook

the stormy space of water in the world. who, then, do not hope

that the final construction of the Panama Canal may abridge the bleak

and icebound horror of that point of continent which look on the chart

to stretch its league and league of tongue into the very heart of the

southern frozen water? to be sure, the passage of the famous cape have

long since cease to be a wonder; but none the less be it full of peril

to vessel which, like the brig I have write about, be at the mercy

of the monstrous sea and furious gale of that formidable tract of

Pacific water.









_a strange chase._





one be sorry to hear of the growth of the very un-english habit of

sheer off and scuttle away after a collision. the first duty of a

shipmaster who plump into a vessel or be run into be to stand by, if

the condition of his ship will permit he, and render all the assistance

in his power. there be nothing more despicable and cowardly than run

away after a disaster of this kind. we know what come of such conduct in

the case of the _northfleet_; and week after week one read in the

shipping paper how such and such a vessel be run into, and how the

other ship make off, and how so many people be drown in consequence.

Darkness, that be fruitful of collision, be also, unhappily, favourable

to these mean and unmanly escape. at night it mostly happen that the

utmost you can tell of the vessel that comes grind into your ship be

that she be big or little, a steamer or a sailing-vessel, and rig in

such and such a fashion. the letter on her nameboard cannot be

decipher; she will not answer your hail; and her reply to the

melancholy shout of “for God’s sake don’t leave we, we believe we be

sink,” be to shift her helm and vanish in the gloom. the obligation

to record such casualty in the log-book or to depone to they before

receiver of wreck do not, it be to be fear, always imply the sort

of accuracy that would be useful to sufferer. from time to time a buoy

be sink, a lightship run into, and the Trinity Corporation offer a

handsome sum of money for information, but without avail. the absence of

all reference by shipmate to such occurrence must make one hope that

they be mainly the work of foreigner. but whatever the flag under

which a captain sail, his sneak away from a disaster in which he have

have a hand express a species of cowardice that press heavily upon

the humbler order of shipowner. a little coaster be run into by a fine

large vessel, which stop a minute or two and then proceed. the master

of the coaster may be her owner, and all that he have in the world be in

his little ship. she be not sink, but her mast be over the side, and

she look as if she have be for some hour under the gun of a fort.

whether or not the master be to blame for the collision, he be pretty

sure to consider that the fault be not his; and his hardship be, that

whilst he stand a chance of be ruin, he be unable to discover the

name of the ship that run into he, so as to be able to bring her owner

into a court of justice, and take his risk as a litigant.



I be amuse and interest some time since by hear the story of the

resolute behaviour of Mr. John Whitear, master of the schooner _Jehu_, a

vessel of about 150 ton. give chase, if you can, be one way, at

least, of clear up the mystery of the paternity of an offend ship

that sneak off in the darkness in the hope of save her owner’s

pocket. any way, Mr. John Whitear’s conduct illustrate a spirit

pleasant to come across in the homely prosaic of the marine life of

to-day. eighty and a hundred year ago it be man of the stamp of Mr.

Whitear who command british privateer; otherwise how should the

maritime memorial of that kind of vessel be so full as they be of the

unflinche obstinacy and the grim courage which follow the flee

enemy over league and league of ocean, through storm and through

calm, finally overhaul and board the breathless chase in

latitude so remote from the point of departure that the span between

the two place might even now be reckon a long voyage?



not very many day ago, then, the _Jehu_, with 230 ton of coal aboard,

be quietly jog along on her way to her port of destination. the

afternoon have be fine, and the night come down very clear and bright,

with starlight. the water be smooth, though a merry wind be blow,

and the little vessel under easy canvas lie softly lean in the gloom,

with the white water rippling and crisping past her side in a hollow,

brass-like tinkling. Starlight give beauty even to a coalman; and I

have know stump topgallant-mast and sail yawn upon sheet hard

upon a fathom from the point in the yardarm through which they lead,

make as dream-like and dainty a picture in the tender sobering shadow

of the night as the tall and taper rig of the handsome yacht now

afloat.



at all event, the _Jehu_ be Mr. John Whitear’s sea-home, and as he

pace the weather side of the deck, sometimes squint into the

windward darkness where the loom of the land hang low upon the vague

greyish softness of the water that way, or sometimes aloft where the

star, like so many benign and encourage eye, be tip he

cheerful wink through the black square in the shroud and over the

main gaff and among the dim tracery of the stand and run rigging,

whose height seem to bring near the sweeping enfoldment of the

glittering heaven, as though the vast star-laden shadow be revolve

and be weave its circling burden of gloom close and close yet

round the lonely schooner journeying slowly along with a bell-like

resonance of broken water around she, he be no doubt as well satisfy

with his little hooker as the captain of an ocean steamer could be with

his stately ship.



his pipe be smoke out, the weather look as steady as a church,

and all be well in every possible sense of that marine expression,

Mr. John Whitear think that no harm could come of his go below for

a spell to take some rest. accordingly, after exchange a few word

with his mate, and take another good look to windward and then aloft,

he walk to the companion and disappear down the step. but instead

of go to bed like a landsman, he keep on his boot and his coat,

merely remove his cap as a preliminary to turn in, and stretch

himself upon a locker, within easy hearing of the first shout that

should come down through the companion, he close his eye, and be

presently contribute to the other creaking sound raise in the plain

and quaint little cabin by the occasional movement of the _Jehu_.



how it come about he could not say, not having be on deck at the time;

but whilst he lie dream such peaceful dream as should visit a master

mariner whose whole professional life be dedicate to the careful

attention of the three L’s, he be suddenly arouse, and in some measure

startle, by a loud and fearful cry in the companion of “below, there!

here’s a barque run into we.”



fortunately, Mr. Whitear have no occasion to stay to dress himself; in a

breath he be up the ladder and on deck. the first thing he see be a

large barque on the port bow, apparently pay off, having just go

about. fresh as he be from a deep sleep, Mr. Whitear have all his wit

about he in a moment; and he immediately perceive that, let he do

what he like and shout as he would, a collision be unavoidable. the

barque loom up large and massive in the darkness. her light be as

plainly to be see as the star, whilst the _Jehu’s_ burn as brightly.

the wind have freshen somewhat, and both vessel be heel under it.

all be silent aboard the barque--not the least sound could be hear;

and in that thrilling and breathless moment all other noise take a

startling distinctness--the washing of water, the creaking of spar, the

squeak up in the darkness of a sheave upon a rusty pin. there be no

sensation comparable to what be feel in the few minute which elapse

between the approach and shock of two meeting vessel. a railway

collision give you no time. if by chance you look out of the carriage

window and see what be go to happen, before you can sing out the

thing have come and be over. but a collision at sea furnish you with

leisure to think, to anticipate, and to make an agony of the disaster

before it actually befall you. whichever way the helm of the _Jehu_ have

be jammed would have be all the same; the barque be bind to come,

and in a few moment there she be, with her bow tower like a cliff

over the low bulwark of the well-freight _Jehu_, her jibboom and

bowsprit arching across the little schooner’s deck like a great spear in

the hand of a giant.



the _Jehu_ heel over under the blow until the rail of her starboard

bulwark be flush with the water. the man come skurrye, half-naked,

out of the forecastle, think she be sink, and rush aft to be

out of the way of whatever might tumble down from aloft. you hear the

grind noise of crushed wood; the thud of fall gear, the tearing of

canvas. the weight of the barque, that be a big vessel in ballast,

sweep the stern of the little _Jehu_ to windward, round she in such a

manner as to free they both. but by this time there be plenty of noise

and activity to be note aboard the barque. order be rattle out in

plain English, and you could hear the scampering of foot and the song

of the seaman as they run to and fro and pull and haul. she heel

over like a great shadow with her mainyard square and her fore-sheet

flatten in. it be impossible to know what mischief she have do; and,

run to the side, Mr. Whitear shout to she at the top of his voice

to stand by they, as he fear the schooner be sink.



no answer be return.



“they’re leave we!” cry the mate. “look! they’re trim sail;

they’re swinge the mainyard!”



again Mr. Whitear bawl to they not to abandon the schooner; but no

answer be vouchsafed, and in a few moment it be not only see that

she be leave they, but that she mean to get away as fast as she

could, for they loose their fore topgallant-sail and main-royal, and

sheet the canvas home with all expedition.



under such circumstance most man would have content themselves with

bestow a sea-blessing on the stranger, and then turn-to to sound

the well, and, if the schooner be leak fast, get the boat over. but

Mr. John Whitear be make of the old, and, as some people might think,

the right kind of stuff.



“Bill,” say he to William Dart, A.B., who be at the wheel, “keep your

eye upon that old catermerang while I and the mate overhaul the

schooner. follow she without a wink, William; for if there’s a creak

leave in this old bucket, we’ll stick to her skirt and have her name,

though she should go all on sailing till we come to Australey.”



Forthwith he and the mate go to work, sound the well, look over

the side, peer at the damage do aloft; and then, come aft again,

“she’s tight and she’s right, boy,” say Mr. Whitear. “now, bully,

here’s a mess that’s to cost some one pound and pound. that some one’s

not to be John Whitear; so, William, starboard your helm, my lad; and

the rest of ye all turn to and make sail forrard, every stitch ye can

find, and then we’ll repair the main rigging, and get a new mainsail

bent;” for he have discover that the barque’s jibboom have cut through

the centre cloth of the mainsail, rip it open from the head to the

second reef-band as neatly as if a sailmaker’s knife have do the job.



they all go to work with a will, put uncommon agility into their

limb and spirit by call the shadow ahead many hard salt name, and

swear they would catch she if she carry they into the Polar

region. the labour be severe, for there be not many of they to

“turn-to;” nevertheless, they manage, in a time less by three-quarter

than they would have occupy on any other occasion, to repair the

damage shroud, set up preventer backstay, bend a new mainsail, and

cover the little vessel with canvas. the barque be close-haul, three

or four mile ahead, on the port tack, lie over, as a light vessel

will in such a merry breeze as be then blow, under both royal and

gaff topsail; she be trust to her heel and run away, like a big

bully from a little man whom he have accidentally hurt, and be afraid of.

her people would probably ridicule the idea of the deep-freight

schooner chase they; indeed, they have leave she apparently helpless,

her port main rigging hanging in bight over the side, her mainsail in

half, and the whole fabric looking wreck and stun from the shock

of the collision.



meanwhile, Mr. John Whitear stump the quarter-deck of his little

craft, often pause to point an old leather-cover telescope at the

lean shadow out away under the low-shine star just the merest

trifle to leeward of the lee knighthead, and then cock the glass

under his arm afresh, and swinge round with a sharp, obstinate stamp

of the foot to resume his walk.



“boy,” he sing out, “there’s no occasion for the Watch below to remain

on deck.”



“no, no,” be the gruff answer; “there’s no go below till we’ve find

out that wessel’s name.”



the wind come along with a fresh, strong sweep, and a deep moan in the

gust as they blow over the bulwark rail into the hollow glimmer of the

great mainsail; there be a kind of flashful light in the break head

of the little black surge, and a regular rise and fall of fountain-like

sound from forward, where the stem of the drive schooner be hiss

through the dark water, and the wake run away astern like a snow-cover

road, until, look at it, you seem to see the dark water on either

side stand up as if the white vein be the frothing stream of a

cataract rush into darkness betwixt the shadow of hill.



“why, smother I, if she’s not get the scent of we!” suddenly cry Mr.

Whitear with the glass at his eye; “she’s off three point, and there’s

no luff leave in she! boy, do any of you take notice if she have her

stun-sail boom aloft?”



“no,” answer William Dart; “her foreyard be just up yonder”

(point into the air), “an’ I’ll take my oath she’d get no boom on

’em.”



“then we’ll run she down yet: we’ll have she!” cry Mr. Whitear,

fetch his knee a slap that sound like the report of a pistol. “keep

she away a bit; ease off the sheet fore and aft. Hurrah, my lad! the

_Jehu_ know the road! we’ll weather the sneak, boy!” and so he rattle

on, sometimes talk to his man, sometimes to the schooner, and

sometimes address the barque ahead.



shortly after two o’clock in the morning, however, four or five

sailing-vessel hove in sight and bother Mr. Whitear exceedingly, for

there be a chance mistake the chase among they and pursue the wrong

vessel. all hand be implore to keep a bright look-out, and the glass

be now much more often at the skipper’s eye than under his arm. it be

strange enough to think of a little colly with 230 ton of coal in her

bottom pursue a vessel three time her size. it might really pass as a

most satirical travestie of the old maritime business, be it not for

the very strong commercial instinct at work in it. the purse be always

as great a power on sea as on land, and the flight of the big barque

from the little coalman be only another illustration of its supremacy.



to the great satisfaction of Mr. Whitear, the schooner turn out to be

more than a match for the cowardly runaway. it be quite clear that the

barque have no more sail to set; as it be, she be bowl along under a

press of canvas that must have make her deck mighty uncomfortable, to

judge from the sharp angle of her inclination. have she choose to put she

helm up and bring the wind well aft, she would no doubt have walk away

from the schooner, whose fore-and-aft canvas then would not have much

help she. but the barque could not forget that she have to work her way

to windward, and that her port lie N.E. and not S.W.: and though she

might slacken away her lee-brace in the hope of make the obstinate

little schooner give up, it would not answer her purpose to do more

than that.



inch by inch the _Jehu_ crawl up to she. just before daybreak the wind

breeze up like a squall, though the sky be clear, and Mr. Whitear, who

all through the night have watch the chase with the intentness of an

old british commodore follow a squadron of fly Frenchmen, shout

out that she have take in her royal and gaff topsail, and that, as it

be, she be nearly out of water to windward. but not so much as a

rope-yarn be touch aboard the _Jehu_; she have never be so pressed

since the hour that she be launch. she hove up the foam as high as

the head-board; every bone of she tremble; the wind boom away from

under the foot of her sail in a thunder-note, and the sheet and

weather stand rigging stand like bar of iron. there seem as much

eagerness in her shivering, rush frame as in her skipper, whose

excitement deepen as the square and lean shadow ahead loom big

and big. earnestly be it to be hope that the port main rigging

would stand all this straining; and yet such be the temper of the

captain and the man of the brave little _Jehu_, that, I believe, have the

mainmast go overboard, they would have hold on after the barque with a

single spar, just as I once see a man with one arm and a wooden leg give

chase to a rogue who have sneer at his misfortune.



the faint grey of the dawn be in the sky when the barque be bring to

the wind again, and, after hold on for a short while with a close

luff, go about. before she have her foreyard brace round, the

schooner have stay and be on the starboard tack, savagely break the

quick sea which be roll in the wake of the wind, and find all

the advantage she need in the weathering she have make upon the

barque, who, with the rise of the sun, appear to lose all heart, for

no more sail be make, and when she be brace up she be keep so close

that the weather half of her fore topgallant-sail be aback. the white

sunshine that have fling a deep blue over the star, and transform the

ocean into a tumble green surface full of sparkle and white line,

and a horizon so clear that it be like the sweep of a brush dip in

bright green paint along the enfold azure of the morning sky, give

stout-hearte Mr. John Whitear a good sight of the tall vessel he have

be chase all through the middle and morning watch. she be what he

call “a lump of a barque,” so light that half her metal sheathing be

out of water, with very square yard and a main skysail mast, and she

tumble with such unwieldly motion upon the run sea that it seem

no long wonderful that the _Jehu_ should have be able to weather and

forereach upon she. her way be almost stop by the gripe of her luff,

and within an hour of the time of she go about the schooner be on

her weather quarter.



Mr. Whitear have already decipher her name upon her stern, but he have

some question to ask; so, jump on to the rail and claw a backstay

with one hand, whilst he put the other hand to his mouth, he bawl out,

“barque ahoy!”



“Hallo!” be the answer.



“what’s the name of your vessel?” sing out Mr. “Whitear.



“have you forget how to read, skipper? it’s under your nose,” come

the reply.



“you’re the barque _Juno_, of Maitland, N.S.--that’s clear enough on

your starn,” shout Mr. Whitear, whose temper, inflame by the long

pursuit, be not improve, as may be suppose, by this reception; “and

you’re the vessel that run into we last night, and carry away our

shroud, brace, and run gear, the mainrail, topgallant bulwark,

and split our mainsail.”



“no, we ain’t,” be the reply. “we know nothing of the job you’re

talk about; so sheer off, will ye, and take care to spot the right

party afore let fly.”



without answer, Mr. Whitear shift his helm so as to bring his

vessel to leeward of the barque; and then, run forward when the

schooner have forge abreast of the other vessel, he shout to the man

who have answer his hail to look over the port bow of the barque and

there he would see the mark of the schooner’s chain-plate bolt, whilst

farther evidence of the barque be the culprit lie in particle of she

planking adhering to the _Jehu’s_ chain-plate. this be too decisive to

admit of farth denial; and Mr. John Whitear having obtain all the

information he require, walk aft again, once more shift his helm,

salute the barque with a farewell flourish of his fist, and then give

order to his man to trim sail and head for the port to which they be

bind.









_a salvage JOB._





among the most picturesque and lively incident of the sea be those of

the encountering of abandon vessel, and the struggle of the people

who board they to carry they into port. be it not for the imperative

injunction of owner, and the various obligation impose upon

shipmaster by the term of charter-party, policy, and the like,

there be no doubt that we should hear very much oftener than we now do

of the preservation of derelict and their cargo. the mariner often

stumble upon some substantial prize in this way. a ship be sight, low

in the water, with nothing stand perhaps but the stump of her

foremast. a spell at the pump ease she, she be overhaul, and she

hold see to be full of valuable cargo. she be take in tow, and after

several day, or perhaps week, of manœuvring, she be carry into port

and find to be worth some thousand of pound, a goodly portion of

which go to the man who navigate she into a place of safety. there be

a touch of romance in such finding that never fail to render they

amusing and even exciting reading; and as story they be often rich in

a high kind of marine characteristic.



one of these yarn, I remember, impress I greatly at the time. the

master of a vessel, call the _Fides_, sight a dutch barque

water-log. on approach she, only one man be to be see on board.

he prove to be the skipper, who say that his crew have refuse to

remain by the vessel, and have leave he alone in she. he be bring

aboard the _Fides_, but have not be there ten minute when he beg to

be send to his water-log barque again. his entreaty be so move

that the captain of the _fide_ yield, and he be once more put in

possession of his wreck and leave there. next day a vessel, call the

_Ballater_, take he off, and the wonder be that the poor fellow have

ever manage to keep his life on the deck of the wave-sweep hulk. here,

in the most obscure form in the world, be an exhibition of the sailor’s

loyalty to his ship so great as to make a truly heroical figure of that

dutch captain. narrative which recount the meeting with derelict and

their conveyance to port often reveal some of the good quality of the

sailor--I mean his indifference to peril, his capacity of determine

labour, his triumph over force whose antagonism would leave most

landsman helpless and hopeless. such be the story of the _Caledonia_, a

prize crew from which take charge of the brig _emily_, and, after ten

day of fierce battle with violent gale of wind in a vessel

jury-rig and half full of water, be eventually force to abandon

she. such be the voyage of five man in the derelict barque _Thor_ of

Tvedestrand, laden with scrap-iron and oil-cask; they have to rig a

jury-rudder to get she to sail, and for nearly a fortnight struggle

with heavy weather and baffle wind, eventually be shipwreck near

Youghal, and narrowly escape with their life only to witness the

craft they have desperately labour to save go to piece among the

rock.



not very long since a ship-rig vessel of nine hundred ton be

proceed on her voyage to one of the West India Islands. the weather

have be calm and thick through the night, with a long swell roll up

from the westward, and the morning break with a fiery sun, red as that

luminary be at his setting, and a mountainous heave of the sea that in

the wake of the orb roll in billow of molten gold, give a kind of

dreadful splendour to the hazy morning, with its faint and tarnished sky

and the sickly green of the swelling and foamless deep, and the stubborn

belt of haze that hang like the greyish shadow of rain upon the horizon,

save where the sun loom like a blood-red shield as he float heavily

out of the deep. there be a hundred sign to betoken a gale at hand,

and preparatory measure be accordingly take aboard the ship. all the

light canvas and the mainsail be furl, and single reef tie in the

topsail. never be such rolling. the draught of air have no weight to

steady the vessel; she fall into the hollow of the swell, and from side

to side she sway as each ponderous liquid fold catch and hove she

over, the water burst inboard in smoke through her scupper-hole, the

shroud creak with the tension of the strain as though they would

draw the chain-plate like pliant wire, and every beam, strong

fastening, and bulkhead add their groaning note to the general

clamour of the labour hull and the beat canvas. by nine o’clock

the sun have vanish under an expanse of slate-colour cloud that hung

over the whole surface of the deep; but yet another hour elapse before

the gale burst, and then it come along in a voice of thunder and over a

surface of milk-white water. with the upper topsail halliard let go

and hand by the low topsail sheet, the ship lean down to it until

the foam be up to a man’s shoulder in the lee scupper; but they

manage to get she to pay off, and presently she be speed like an

arrow on the wing of the temp, pile the foam as high as her

figure-head, her main-topsail blow in rag out of the bolt-rope, and

sheet of spray fog her deck like burst of vapour from a boiler.



the next thing to do be to bring she to the wind before the sea rise;

the crew go aloft to stow the topsail and frap what remain of the

main-topsail upon the yard; and after a little there be the ship with

nothing on she but a small storm trysail, bow and shearing at the

huge surge which the storm have lift in cone and pyramid, and which

be now pour and break with a terrible roaring noise. all day and

far into the night the storm blow without intermission, but it break in

the middle watch, and then fine down so rapidly that at eight o’clock

in the morning the ship be pursue her course under whole topsail and

topgallant-sail, and curtsy over the long heave of the sea, whose

green seem to sparkle after the purification of the temp, and whose

beautiful arching coil be brilliant with the diamond-like flashing of

the foam chip out of the emerald acclivity by the keen tooth of the

clear, fresh north-east wind.



shortly after noon the watch on deck have come out of the forecastle

after eat their dinner, when a small brig be make out right ahead,

apparently stand athwart the ship’s hawse. on approach she it be

see that she be drift, and that though there might be people

aboard, she be not under control. aloft she be in a state of great

confusion, her foreyards square, and her after-yard braced as wildly

as the leech of the canvas would allow. the davit fall be

overhaul to the water’s edge, and all the boat be go. here and

there end of her run rigging trail overboard, and as she roll

heavily in the trough of the sea, the sound of her flap canvas throw

a wild and melancholy echo athwart the breeze. the master of the ship

loudly hail she, and all eye be eagerly fasten upon the brig to

observe if there be any indication of life in she. possibly nothing

so heighten the mournful and tragical suggestion of an abandon

vessel as the loud hail of a pass ship and the deathlike stillness

follow, unbroken save by the hollow beating of canvas, the drowning

sob of swell water, and the creak of strain timber.



it be very evident that nothing alive be in the brig, and the master

of the ship, after consult with his mate, decide on send a boat.

accordingly, the second mate and a couple of seaman go over the side,

and, after some hard rowing and careful dodging of the sea, they gain

the brig, and scramble upon her deck. they find that she be damage

to an extent that could not be imagine by inspection of she from the

ship. her galley and cabin skylight be smash in, bulwark stanchion

be start, and, in addition to various other injury, there be

three foot of water in the hold. whether she have drain this water into

she from the deck or whether it be due to a leak could not at once be

ascertain; it be certain at least that her hold be full of cargo,

and that it be of a nature that would not enable she to float should

the water gain upon she. these fact be report by the second mate,

who add that he could find no paper belong to the vessel, and that

she have be strip of all her provision.



“it seem a pity to leave she knock about here,” shout the

captain. “it’ll be another man’s job if we don’t tackle it. do you see

your way to carry she to Fayal?” then distant about four hundred mile.



the second mate converse with the two man who be with he, and, after

a little while, call out, “ay, we’ll risk it.”



on this the two seaman be order to come alongside, when some

provision, water, a sextant, chart, and other needful article be

lower into the boat. with these they put off, receive a loud

encourage cheer from the rest of the ship’s crew; and, reach the

brig’s side, hoist out the provision, and hook on the boat and

drag she up to the davit. the ship stand by for awhile, watch the

plucky fellow, and perhaps suspect that they might repent their

undertaking, for even with a dry bottom the brig might have be

reckon a big navigating job for three man. she roll heavily and

continuously, her canvas strike the mast with loud report, and

make the light spar buckle, and as she lift her shine side out

of the bright green sea the water be see to gush from her bulwark in

a manner to prove the wrenching they have undergo from the recent

tempest. there be no show of misgiving or repentance, however, on the

part of the man. having hoist their boat they turn to and trim

the yard, clap the jigger on to the topsail halliard, and give

everything a good spread. the little vessel take the wind, slightly

heel, and come round to her course for the Western Islands, and the

last thing the ship, as she fill and stand on her voyage, see of the

brig be the second mate at the wheel, the two man toil at the

break-pump amidship, and the little vessel under fore and main

topgallant sail heavily swinge over the long ocean swell, throw the

foam from her deep round bow, and look but the mere toy amid the

vast surface of undulating water which lean away into the furth

reach of the sky.



a crew of three man leave, with one at the wheel, only two to do the

ship’s work. four or five seaman would not have be too many to hand

that brig’s main sail alone, and a gale of wind might therefore oblige

the second mate and his two companion to put their helm up and run for

it, and leave the canvas to blow away with a blessing upon it before

they could bring the vessel to. four hundred mile seem but a short

voyage nowadays; but a head wind might enlarge the period of such a

journey into week, in which case, unless these man meet with

help--which, though very likely, be by no mean certain--they be

bind to perish of starvation, as the quantity of provision supply to

they by the ship could not, however economically use, outlast four or

five day. it be just because a sailor would keenly understand all the

heavy risk and difficulty comprise in such an adventure as these

three man have engage in, that the courage imply in this and many

other attempt of the same kind to save property find at sea deserve a

good place in the annal of naval heroism. a half-hour’s spell at the

pump satisfy they that by regular application the water might be keep

under, though there could be no long any doubt that the vessel be

leak either from a start butt or some puncture below the

water-line. a tarpaulin be find and secure over the broken skylight,

as a provision against dirty weather; the galley fire be light, and

the deck clear up, and there be an old reel-log near the wheel,

along with a sand-glass, they manage among they to heave it--the second

mate at the helm hold the glass--and discover that the brig be

make a little less than four knot. but the weather keep fine, and

this support the man’s courage, as do also their assurance one to

another that they be bind to be well reward for the risk they be

run. they have another spell at the pump, and then fetch a bit of

the ship’s beef that have be put to cook in the galley-copper, and

bring it aft with some biscuit, make out a tolerable meal, the mate

steering with one hand and eat with the other.



the day pass quietly, but the wind be light, and the progress make

be small. the duty of keep the pump go at regular interval grow

exhausting, but it be absolutely necessary that the quantity of water

should be keep under the depth find in the brig when she be board,

and every hour throughout the day the harsh clank of the pump might be

hear, cease after an interval when the man, pale with fatigue, and

with the sweat streaming from their face, fling themselves upon the

deck breathless and spend. the breeze freshen at sunset, and the

topgallant-sail be take in. the night come down very dark, with a

few misty star here and there, and a flavour in the swing of the wind

as it blow in gust over the bulwark that be a promise of bad weather.

the weight of the water in the little vessel, couple with the cargo,

that come flush with the main hatch, sink her deep, and as the sea rise

her behaviour grow wild. the billow tumble against her weather-bow,

and such be her inelasticity that at time she would not rise to they,

but let they roll over her forecastle, bury herself pretty nearly as

far aft as her foremast, and flood her deck to the wheel.

fortunately her upper work be staunch, or she must have be drown

again and again by the sea which tumble in ton’ weight over her head.

the man make shift to stow the upper topsail before it come on hard,

but they could do nothing with the low canvas, which must blow away if

it would not stand. this the fore-topsail do shortly after ten o’clock

in a squall of wind; the weather sheet part, and in a few moment the

sail be in rag, increase the roar noise of the gale and the

crash sound of the sea by the fierce whipping of the tattered cloth.

amidst all this confusion and wild scene of the black heaven and

glimmer height of water, the man betook themselves again and again

to the pump, and the metallic ring of the work brake fling a dismal

note of shipwreck into the harsh uproar of the war element. it be

difficult to realize a sterner picture of struggle, a more furious array

of peril. here be three man as crew of a vessel which want a good

nine hand to work she, exhaust by pumping, and yet oblige regularly

to apply themselves to the pump to keep the vessel afloat--force by

this work, or by have to tend the helm, to remain unsheltered upon the

deck over which the sea be burst in whole ocean; wet through to

the skin, without the mean of obtain a warm drink, and without the

chance of preserve a dry stitch even be an opportunity afford they

to change their clothe; a black and howl void overhead, and below a

huge break sea, in whose thunderous hollow the little vessel labour

like a drown thing, one moment upright and becalm by the tower

coil of a rush surge, the next on her beam-end on the summit of the

liquid height, with the full force of the gale howl through her

rigging, and the spray from the break head of the near comber

sweep over her deck upon the breath of the black and ring wind

like a furious snow-storm.



in the limit assign here it be impossible to do justice to this

struggle. to make it a conceivable thing to the landsman’s intelligence

something of photographic minuteness be want in the reproduction; the

picture of the man leave the pump and crawl along the deck to the

wheel, their talk, their posture as they sit crouch and listen to

the infernal din in the ebony void on high--a hundred such matter,

indeed--together with the outline of the vessel, reveal for a

breathless space, as she swoop into a trough with a headlong shearing

of the bow that make the water boil in whiteness which fling a kind of

twilight round about, in which the ink-like configuration of the

straining and beat fabric be throw up as though a gleam of pallid

moonshine have break through the dense vapour of the storm and fall

for an instant into the swirling and cream hollow in which the brig

lay weltering. that the deeply-laden and half-drown vessel should have

outlive that night be a real miracle. fierce as have be the precede

storm encounter by the ship, this gale have at time an edge in it that

the other want. happily, like its predecessor, it be short-live, and

blow itself out soon after daybreak, though it leave such a tremendous

sea behind that for several hour after the wind have sober down into a

topgallant breeze the brig be in the utmost jeopardy. the rolling be

so frightful that the man could do nothing aloft. the mate refuse to

allow they to leave the deck, expect every instant to see the mast go

over the side. it be almost impossible to stand at the pump; sometimes

the little vessel would literally _dish_ a sea over her rail that sweep

the two seaman off their leg, and force the mate, who grasp the

wheel, to hold on to the spoke for dear life; and it be as much as

their neck be worth to let go for a moment. by noon, however, the

swell have greatly subside, and the man make shift to set the main and

upper fore-topsail and topgallant-sail, and to board the foretack.

the mate also get an observation which enable he to set his course.

but the night that be pass have almost do for they; they could

scarcely stand, and crawl about like sick man; and such be their pass

that when the mate, lay hold of the pump, sing out to one of his

companion to come and lend he a hand, the reply be that if the

pumping be to depend upon _he_, the bloom hooker might as well sink

at once, as there be not strength enough leave in he to kill a flea;

and it be not until the mate and the other man who stand at the wheel

have consume twenty minute in entreaty, curse, and other marine

rhetoric, that the exhausted creature be induce to “tail on.”

fortunately for the poor fellow the wind have shift into a quarter

favourable for their voyage; they dry their clothe, cook some beef,

and manage to snatch sufficient rest between the interval of pump

to give they back something of their strength. everything go on well

until they be about forty mile distant from Fayal, when the wind

back and blow a fresh breeze right ahead. this be madden enough.

they brace the yard hard up, pack all that they could hoist upon the

vessel, and swear that, come what might, they would not slacken a

halliard nor touch a sheet though it should blow fit to prize the old

butter-box out of the water. it be not long after this that a steamer

hove in sight, and, probably suspect a case of distress by the look

of the brig aloft--for the rag of the low fore-topsail still

flutter upon the yard--slow her engine to speak the little vessel.

“what ship be that?” be ask. the name be give and the circumstance

relate. the steamer then offer to give the brig a drag towards Fayal,

but when it be understand that a share in the salvage would be

expect, the second mate sing out no, they want no help, they have

scrape through it all right so far, and be willing to venture the

remain risk. thereupon the steamer proceed, but have not sink she

hull when the wind again shift, and enable the brig to look up for

her port with the breeze full abeam; and within nine hour from the time

of having be speak by the steamer, a pilot have board she, and she

be safely moor at the west end of Fayal Bay. the value of the brig

and cargo prove to be sixteen hundred pound, and when the award come

to be make, four hundred pound be give to the owner of the ship

that have board the brig, one hundred pound to the master and crew of

the ship, and a substantial sum to the second mate and his two man.









_a CHANNEL INCIDENT._





the captain of the steamer which ply as passenger and cargo vessel

between London and the french port be a class of man familiar in a

more or less degree to most of we, and it be probably this familiarity

that prevent we from dwelling, with the emphasis that be deserve, upon

the singular skill they exhibit, day after day, and year after year, in

carry their ship through what may be fairly call the most

dangerous water in the world, with scarcely a misadventure to vary the

chronicle of their little voyage. by night and by day they be

thread the intricacy of the crowd river Thames, grope through

white mist so thick that a buoy must be alongside before it can be

see; struggle against sudden burst of furious Channel weather, which

bring up the most abominable kind of sea that a sailor can tumble about

in--short, roar cross surge which seem to knock the very breath out

of the paddle steamer, slope her funnel like the _bâton_ in the hand

of a band conductor, submerge one paddle-wheel to let the other

revolve like a windmill out of water, and blow up in storm of snow

from the sponson, whilst the worry vessel pitch savagely into the

narrow hollow, fling up her stern like the hind leg of a colt that

take fright at a pass train, her tarpaulin stream with wet, the

escape-pipe blowing as she reel, a few sea-sick passenger wet through

aft, two or three seaman in oilskin dodge the sea forward, and the

skipper on the bridge hold on to the rail with both hand, and

wonder what that confound old “Geordie” right ahead be up to,

come along with square yard and his patch boom-foresail belly

out like a sailor’s shirt dry in the forestay, as if the whole of the

Channel be his private property, and it be his duty to run over

anything that get in his road.



take the trip to Boulogne alone. in fine, clear weather it be all plain

sailing, no doubt. but if a passenger want to appraise the merit of

these captain rightly, let he quit the pitch-dark deck, and a night so

black and thick that it be a positive relief to the eye when a shower of

spark break out of the funnel and blow away into the ebony gloom to

leeward, and go below into the bright, warm cabin, and overhaul a chart

of the mouth of the Thames and the adjacent water as far as the South

Sands Head Light. why, the sight be bewilder by the mere look of that

chart. it be as though a spider have get foul of an ink bottle, and have

be clean its leg on a large sheet of white paper. West and East

Girdlers, Margate Sands, Long Sands, Sunk Sands, Goodwin Sands--it seem

to be all sand; whilst the sounding be more alarming still--eleven

fathom here, and close against it, one fathom--the English of which be

blue light, rocket, hoveller, lifeboat, and Board-of-trade

inquiry. Jones, asleep in his little state cabin, know nothing of the

maze of peril through which he be be steer; he will rise in the

morning and take his seat at the breakfast-table, and in the compose

feature of the brown-face, hearty-look captain who sit modestly

eat a rasher of bacon, he will find no trace or hint of a vigil

which begin at London Bridge and which will not terminate until Boulogne

be reach, though perhaps--the Goodwins be astern, and neither the

Varne nor the Ridge be very much in the road--the hard part of it

may be say to be over.



but the danger of the English Channel be by no mean limit to shoal

and foul weather. if those be all, the captain who safely carry

hundred upon hundred of passenger to and fro in the course of the

year, would have to abate something of the praise to which their

excellent skill and remarkable vigilance entitle they. in truth, a

danger more to be fear than shallow water and tempestuous weather be

collision. I be not speak of the daytime and fine weather; though

even in the daytime and in fine weather collision at sea will happen

through a dozen circumstance more absolutely unavoidable than the most

apparently unavoidable railway collision ever attribute by a coroner’s

jury to pure accident. it be the thick and silent night that be most

haunt by this deadly peril. there be no wind, but a drench drizzle

drop unseen, save in the haze of the cabin skylight, from a black

heaven that seem to rest its ponderous burden on the slender masthead

of the creep steamer. it be the English Channel, the great maritime

highway that lead to all part of the world, and now as ever it be

crowded with shipping; and through this mighty shadow, full of hide

life and hidden danger, those captain I be write of must bring their

vessel, day after day, week after week. they must not lag, for time be

precious to their owner. their unscathed emergence year after year must

surely savour of the miraculous to any man who will but give his mind to

the character of the danger through which these sailor steer their

vessel in safety. as a sample of this particular peril of collision,

let I give an instance--a recent one. it may remove reference from all

risk of misapprehension if I say at once that the steamer be from

Bilbao, bind to a North country port.



she be abreast of Beachy Head when the night fall, and the fresh

southerly wind, suddenly shift to the westward in a little squall,

drop. during the latter portion of the afternoon the weather have be

slowly thicken, but when the wind go the haze roll up all round

like smoke, blacken the moonless night until the very foam breaking

away from under the counter be a scarcely perceptible glimmer upon the

inky surface that melt into the midnight void within a biscuit’s throw

from the vessel’s side. there be a few passenger, who vanish with

the daylight and might be see, by peer through the cloudy skylight

glass, seat at the cabin table, the lamplight bright upon they, and

make the picture of the irradiate interior, by contrast with the

breathless blackness on deck, like a magic-lantern show. there be no

gleam of phosphorus, no pallid streak of foam, to define the presence of

the deep; but the soft seething of the pass froth, resemble the

escape of steam hear thinly and at a long distance, fill the ear with

a permanent note, and the dull vibration of the engine could be lightly

feel. the haze be as wet as rain; and the bullseyes over the

light interior glimmer like emerald in the deck upon which the

mist be crawl as the vessel carry it along. there be a mystery in

the hush blackness of a night like this at sea which may be enjoy in

the open ocean, where the imagination let itself loose upon the hide

league of water, and find a kind of life in death in the mere

capacity of sentience amidst such a universe of shadow; but it come

with an element of fear in a narrow sea stud with quicksand and

alive with vessel. the eye struggle with the darkness in vain. every

instinct sympathize with the blindness that have fall upon you; but

the strained ear catch no more than the sob and fret of pass water

and the chafing of gear as the vessel sway upon the indistinguishable

fold of the swell. a man come up out of the cabin of that steamer

might have reckon the vessel desert and leave to her own guidance.

the wheel be amidship, and there be no familiar binnacle-lamp to

relieve with its soft mist of light the eye that strove to pierce the

darkness aft. to know where the captain be, or whether there be any

hand on the look-out, it would have be necessary to sing out or go

about the deck and upon the bridge groping.



presently, what look to be a composant--a small trembling point of

light--hover in the blackness on the starboard bow, and a moment after

there creep out under it a dull green smudge, as faint and baffle in

the thickness as the waver flame of spirit of wine. a steamer’s

light; but all that be visible of she be a deep darkness in the air

where she loom, a row of illuminate scuttle like the beach-lamp of

a little town see afar, and fibre of radiance strike into the foggy

air from the bright light on the fore-mast. a deep fold of darkness

seem to overlap the night as the invisible steamship sweep by; the

pulsing of her engine thin down, and the wash of the bow-wave melt

into the vague, haunt undertone of chafing water--a sound come you

know not from where. on a sudden the deck rang with a loud and fearful

cry, “there’s a vessel right ahead! hard-a-port! hard-a-port! mind, or

we shall be into she!” crash! you could hear the sound of splinter

wood, follow by a whole chorus of shriek, whilst a dozen order be

volley out in hoarse note on the steamer’s deck. “what be it?”

“where be she?” “get some light along, in God’s name!” a bright red

flame throw out a wild radiance over the steamer’s side: there be a

rush of man to see what it be, and there, glide past the steamer,

every outline distort by the crimson, flickering, streaming fire of a

flare-tin hold on high by one of her man, be a french three-masted

smack, her deck apparently full of people, shriek altogether, and in

every conceivable posture of entreaty and terror--a dreadful picture

indeed, stand out with terrible distinctness in the red light of the

flare against the liquid pitch of the sea and the sky. their shout and

cry be in the rude _patois_; it be impossible to distinguish

their meaning amidst the hubbub on the maim and broken hull, as it

veer swiftly astern, the mainmast over the side, the wild light

flash up the crowd of white face as the flame from the tin break out

in a blood-red fork of radiance, and the whole fearful picture vanishing

as the light suddenly expire, and the night roll its inky tide over

it. the steamer’s engine be instantly reverse and the iron fabric

stop. the passenger came rush up out of the cabin, increase the

distraction of the darkness by their eager, terrified inquiry to know

what have happen. the chorus of shrieks astern be silence, and only

faint, single, most melancholy shout break the terrible silence upon

the sea, prove but too conclusively that the vessel have founder, and

that these cry come from swimmer.



meanwhile every lamp and lantern aboard the steamer that could be

collect have be bring on deck, and you could see the dark figure

of seaman struggle to get the boat overboard, rush aft, and

vociferate promise of speedy help into the blackness astern, some

bend on lantern to rope’ end, and let they drop over the side,

and fling end of line overboard for the clutch of such swimmer as

should reach the steamer; whilst the cry of the captain and mate and

the shout of the crew be make deafen by the pouring and hiss of

steam up in the blackness overhead. it always seem an eternity at time

like this before the boat be overboard; something get foul; the oar

have be take forward to be scrape, and cannot be find; a kink in

the fall have jam in the davit-block; there be no plug, and a dozen

voice be shout all at once for something to take its place. but two

boat at last be launch, after an interval of about five minute,

and pull slowly away for the spot where the smack have founder, a

hand in each bow hold a lantern and keep a bright look-out for

those black spot which should denote the head of swimmer and drown

man. a silence as of death fall upon the steamship as her boat leave

she. a crowd of people stand in the stern watch the two spot of

light upon the water, breathlessly listen for any sound that should

indicate the rescue of even one man. the lantern over the side fling a

short space of radiance upon the sea, and man be post along the rail

to watch for any approach swimmer who should have be miss by the

boat.



“be you find any of they?” bawl the captain of the steamer,

send his voice in a roar through the hollow of his hand.



“ay, ay, we’re pick they up,” come back the answer in the merest

thread of sound.



ten minute go by, and then suddenly there arise a shout from one of

the man station at the port bulwark.



“here’s a man swim here!” and in a breath there be a rush to the

side.



“get another light over!”



“fle he this life-buoy!”



“pitch a coil of rope to he, but mind you don’t hit his head, or you’ll

sink he!”



half a dozen splash tell that these various order have be execute.

“he’s get hold of my line!” sing out a voice, and as the rope be gently

haul in, a seaman, jump into the bight of a rope, spring overboard,

and in a few moment both man be drag over the side.



the half-drown french smacksman fall down in a heap the instant he

touch the deck. he be dress in heavy sea-boot and oilskin

legging, and how he have manage to swim the distance from where his

vessel have founder to the steamer be a miracle not to be explain by

any known law of specific gravity. he be carry into the forecastle,

unable to articulate; but another quarter of an hour go by before the

boat return.



“how many have you?” shout the captain, as they approach.



“we have four, and the other boat have five. there be woman among ’em,”

be the answer.



they come alongside, and one by one the poor creature be hand up.

there be three woman, dress in the picturesque costume of the

Boulogne fishwife, but draggle, streaming, with closed eye, and a

quick, suffocating breathing, half dead. Most of the other be in the

last stage of exhaustion; but one be able to speak, and as he stand a

moment in the lantern-light answer the captain’s question, a more

move object could not be imagine. the water drain from his finger,

his hat be go, and his iron-grey hair--for he be an old man--lie in

a tangled mass over his eye; and there be a most heartrending

expression of horror and despair in his face.



he say his vessel have leave Boulogne early that morning. there be four

woman and ten man and boy on board. he own that they have have no

light burn. he tremble like a freezing man, and be then lead below,

with his hand to his face, sob as if his heart would break, and

moaning in his rude French that amongst the drowned be his wife and

boy.



“be you sure there be no other afloat when you come away?” ask the

captain of the mate, who have charge of the boat. “one man swam to we, I

must tell you, and we have he aboard.”



“sure, sir,” be the answer. “we pull round and round, but there be

nothing to be see. the people be save by the mainmast that be leave

afloat when the smack go down. those who be drown miss it,

otherwise it be big enough to keep all of they up.”



for another twenty minute the captain linger, peer into the

darkness, and keep one boat overboard ready for the first sound. but

the deep be as silent as the tomb, and nothing disturb the deathlike

stillness, unless it be the murmur of the man forward talk over the

tragical incident, and the quick, passionate whisper of the passenger,

as one would suddenly say, “hush! what be that?” and another, “see! be

not that something move out yonder?” nothing more could be do. very

reluctantly the captain quit the stern of his vessel and give order

to get the boat on board, and in a little while the steamer be slowly

move again through the blackness, her deck wrap in darkness and

silence, whilst the haze float like steam round the masthead light,

and the water gurgle like the cry of a drown man as it eddy round

under the counter and go away in a pale glimmer of froth into the

midnight gloom astern.



this little incident will, I believe, fairly set before the reader one

of the peril against which those particular captain to whom I refer

in the beginning of this article have to contend. here be a

fishing-smack, lie becalm, without a light showing, on a night make

pitch dark by a drizzling haze. how could such a collision be avert,

short of the captain of the steamer bring up?--a remedy which his

owner most assuredly would not think the well of he for adopt. I

repeat that having regard to the difficult navigation of the mouth of

the Thames, as far south as the southern limb of the Goodwins, to the

mass of shipping of all kind that be always crowd these water, to

the perilous weather to be find there, and to the negligence,

foolhardiness, and indifference which be characteristic of the

seamanship of score of the man--English as well as foreigner--who have

charge of small craft navigate that sea, the manner in which the

master I be speak of carry their steamer from port to port, year

after year, show always the same clean bill of health, imply an

amount of skill and vigilance which any one acquaint with the

navigation and danger of the English Channel from the Nore to the

Bullock Bank will own cannot be too highly praised.









_loss of a smack’S CREW._





I fall once into conversation with a smack-boy--a yorkshire lad--who

tell I a story which I privately decline to believe until I see the

print report of the inqu, and have confirmation of his narrative

from other hand. man who go to sea meet with strange accident, and

perish through cause which landsman would ridicule as impossibility

in marine novel; but seldom do a vessel’s crew encounter such a

disaster as that which befell the people of the smack _Apostle_, of

Hull. I wish I could tell the story as the fishing apprentice give it

I. no painter could imagine a fine study than the figure of the lad in

his blue knit overall, his big boot, his sou’wester, the hinder

thatch of which fork out from the back of his head like the tail of a

gull on the wing, his young face as he talk warm up into a kind of

passionate awe and fear, as it might in his sleep when the dreadful

circumstance stand out in the sharp configuration of a dream; whilst now

and again he would pass the back of his rough hand across his forehead

to rub off the gout of sweat which gather there. however, I can do no

more than translate the lad’s yarn, and make it complete, in its way, by

fact I get from other. the _Apostle_, then, be a smack, belong to

Hull. will Stevenson be her master, and John Butler her mate. besides

these she carry two other man and a boy--the lad who tell I the

story--make in all five soul. she leave Hull, however, with only four

man, for the boy do not join she until she have be out cruise a

week, when he be send to she in a steamer.



Life on board a smack be but a dull affair, and such excitement as it

have be all against the fisherman. it be tedious work drift for hour

with the trawl overboard; but what be to be make of it when, as

sometimes happen, the trawl be get aboard and the net find tear to

piece by a piece of sunken wreck or something of that kind, and all the

fish go? or take a gale of wind blow for a week, keep the

fisherman wait and wait for a spell of moderate weather to fetch

his ground. to be hove-to in a smack in the North Sea be such a dance as

you must endure--not for a day, but for several day together--to

understand. who that have roll in a big steamer across the tempestuous

stretch of water which wash our eastern coast have not watch from the

reeling, spray-sweep deck the spectacle of some dandy or cutter-rig

boat, jump as if by magic into the arena of the green, pelting, and

foaming amphitheatre, with her storm jib-sheet to windward or well

amidship, a slender band of dark, close-reef mainsail tearing at the

quivering gaff, whilst she toss the high spring of her bow at the

rush snow of the surge, chop sharply down into the livid vortex

and make it flash up in white spume that smother she like the smoking

spray of a great waterfall, vanish until her gaff be hide, and

nothing show but the jerk vane at the masthead behind the glitter

ridge of the sea that run at she with the roar of a good train

sweep through a tunnel; and then spring afresh to the height of

the thunderous surge until some fathom of her keel forward be

expose, and lean down upon the slope of the mountainous wave, and

under the giant pressure of the ring gale, until her mast seem

parallel with the water and her dark shred of canvas a mere black patch

upon the snow-storm under she?



one wonder, look at such a sight, how the big-boot fellow aboard

of her hold on; how they manage to cook their food; by what

inconceivable art they contrive to “fetch” their bunk, or sleep

closet, without numerous ineffectual struggle, first of all, to hit

the hole. but, in truth, no class of sailor make less trouble of dirty

weather than fisherman. with his tiller securely lash, the storm jib

slat a moment or two as the reefed mainsail swing the little craft

into the wind, then shove her nose round again as the sea run hiss

away under she, the air forward dark with fly foam and the water

drain overboard in bucketful with every send, the smacksman sit

cosily in the companion, pipe in mouth, keep one eye on the look-out

and the other eye on the time when one of his mate shall come and take

his place, and send he below to toast his hand at the little stove,

whose ruddy glow pleasantly tinge the darksome twilight of the cabin,

and enable he to find, without groping, another pipe of tobacco before

he lie down.



Daybreak on Friday, the _Apostle_ be then very nearly five week out

from Hull, find the smack with her trawl over the weather quarter and

near the north-east end of the Dogger Bank. there be a fresh breeze

blowing and a middling sea running, and the smack, surge to leeward

with the trend of the wave, rise and fall with the regularity of a

pendulum. many mile distant to windward be another smack, apparently

head for the same ground over which the _Apostle_ be drag she

trawl; otherwise the sea be vacant, and the greenish dawn, fling a

sickly tint into the sky, but leave the water dark by contrast, and

throw up the great circle of the horizon until the ocean resemble a

black and solid disc centre the huge concavity of the heaven, make

the immediate aspect of the deep indescribably wild and melancholy.

indeed, there be not a more desolate scene in the world than daybreak at

sea. the shadow of the night still hang in fold upon the water, and

the dim illumination in the east only serve to accentuate the chilly

sullenness and grim bleakness bequeath by the black hour, the last of

which be draw away in gloom into the west. but the sun be a noble

magician, and one stroke of his flash wand convert the mystery of

the dawn’s vague hint into a glorious revelation of blue height and

sparkling water. the _Apostle’s_ trawl have be over all night, but a

further short spell of drift could do no harm, and might furnish out

another trunk of fish, and the interval would give they time to get

breakfast. so the little fire in the stove be stir into a good

blaze, the coffee boil, and the two man at rest in their bunk route

out for the meal. fisherman be usually well fed, and that be one

reason, I suppose, why they appear to relish their food in a manner you

shall not find in any forecastle. they have generally a good freight of

fish to pick from, and they be not slow to boil a cod or cook a big

sole when fancy and appetite prompt they. somehow or other, to I, the

smoke that comes blow away out of the little chimney which pierce

their deck always savour of good cheer, and I be not at all

surprised, on look over some victualling account show I by a

smack-owner, to discover that the fisherman’s sea-larder--many of they,

certainly--be stock with a liberality that must make owner very

anxious indeed to know how much fish there be aboard, when their

vessel’s number or burgee come within reach of their telescope.



Breakfast do, the master give order for the trawl to be get in, and

all hand tumble up on deck to help at one of the few heavy job which

happen aboard fishing-smack. I have already say there be a fresh

breeze blowing, and the vessel, though hove-to with her jib-sheet to

windward, lean down freely under the weight of the reefed mainsail.

the sea be regular, but run quickly, and every lift of the surge

help the wind to lay the little craft along, until at time her lee

gunwale be flush with the water; but, like all boat of her class, she

would right with great vehemence, jump to windward like a goad

creature of instinct, and make the deck, slippery with wet, extremely

dangerous even to practise foot. they say that a fisherman’s walk be

two step and overboard, and any one would have think the say a

true one who have see this jumping bit of a fabric--spark like a

shrimp in and out of the hollow of the tumbling water--and watch

those big-boot, clumsily-move, powerfully-build man stride about

the deck and make ready to drag the great trawl in.



the process be very simple. the dandy-wink be man, the beam secure,

and the net be then drag in over the side. the _Apostle’s_ man have

succeed in get in the net to the cod-end, as it be call. all

five hand be employ on this job, as it be one that demand the

united strength of such little company as smack carry. they lean

over the rail to grasp the net, but the vessel at that moment bury

her lee side through the lift of an unusually heavy sea, one of the man

lose his balance and go overboard, and the net belly out and

send away as the vessel roll to windward, in the twinkling of an

eye the other three man whose hand grasp the mesh be tear clean

over the beam and bury in the sea alongside, leave only the boy on

deck. it be do in a breath. there be no time even to raise a shriek.

one moment there be all four man lean over the side, the net

securely inwreathe about their finger and wait for the signal from

the master to drag together; the next they be flounder in the water

alongside, struggle, desperately clutch at the sink net, and

drown. there be a portion of the net on deck, and to this the

boy--who preserve an heroical presence of mind in the midst of this

appallingly sudden and dreadful disaster--clung, that the man might not

drag it all overboard (and so have nothing to hold by) in their wild and

overhand grasp at the deadly, deceptive mesh which float and sink

under they, and clog the free action of their limb, and clung to

they like masse of seaweed, settle they lower and low as new fold

of it be sweep by the water around they. the net be to leeward, the

tendency of every sea be to belly it out and increase its weight,

whilst also set the whole mass of it far and far away from

the vessel’s side; but this weight be beyond description increase by

the man who battle with the fury of strong die creature in the

deadly envelopment of the trawl. every now and again a sea would break

under the vessel and bury the poor fellow in foam; and then, as the

smack sweep down into the hollow and lean heavily to windward, the

drag of the hull upon the net would strike it up again, and the four

smacksman would reappear with dusky despairing face, their eye

protrude as they strain for breath. robust as the boy be, here be

a conflict it be impossible for he long to engage in. he hold to the

net with as manly and resolute a heart as ever an english lad bring to

a struggle for life; but the weight of the belly net and of the man

cling to it, increase as it be tenfold at time by the swing and

rush of the smack upon the sea, must have tax and presently exhaust

the strength of a dozen such as he; gradually as he fail the net be

tear foot by foot away from he, though every time it be wrench from

his hand he grab at it again, and hold on with clench tooth until

another swoop would unlock his finger as you might snap a clay

pipe-stem.



suddenly turn his head--for hitherto he have be engross by the

dreadful struggle in the water just a fathom or two away beyond he--he

spy the smack that have be sight at dawn, about half a mile to

windward. she be manifestly head for the _Apostle_, and the boy

shout to the miserable drown man that help be come, and urge

they to hold on. but it be doubtful whether they hear the lad’s voice.

close upon the water the seething and hiss of foam would be

deafen; moreover, their eye be glaze--death have his hand on

their throat; they present a row of asphyxiated face, now and again

revolve in the eddy amid the trawl gear, sometimes throw up

until their body as high as the waist be out of the water, in which

posture they would remain poised with uplifted arm that give they a

horrible appearance of entreaty, then vanish utterly, to emerge a few

second after as the roll of the vessel swing they up and out. the boy’s

strength be now completely exhausted, and also he have to let go in

order to signal to the approach smack. the whole of the net then go

overboard.



about an hour have pass since the man have fall into the sea, during

all which time this most shocking tragedy be be enact, whilst the

boy with magnificent courage protract his shipmate’ live by

maintain his hold of the net. but the moment he let go the net veer

out to its full sweep, and an instant after one of the man sink and rise

no more. the smack be now within hail. the boy rush to the weather

side, and shout out the dreadful story with such strength as remain

in he, at the same pointing frantically at the water where the drowning

man be. the dreadful scene be by this time visible to the crew of the

vessel, which prove to be a Yarmouth smack call the _Esther_. they

tumble their boat over the side; a couple of hand jump into she and

row at once for the perish fisherman. the boy run back to the lee

side of his vessel to encourage the poor creature, but, look, he

discover that the third man be go; the master and mate only be to

be see, both cling to the gear and scarcely live. the little

boat--hardly well than a walnutshell in such a sea--come along fast;

but before she could come up to the master, he let go his hold and

float away, face down and arm hang lifeless, upon a run wave.

a few stroke of the oar, however, bring the rescuer abreast of he,

and he be seize and lift into the boat, which then return and take

off the mate from the gear, to which he clung like a mass of black

seaweed tear from the rock. call out to the boy that they would see

to he presently, the Yarmouth fisherman row back to the _Esther_ with

their dreadful freight, but when they come to hand the man up over the

side they find that the master be dead. the mate be carry below,

strip and dry before the cabin stove, then wrap in rug and

lay in a bunk. but he be little more than a corpse when rescue, and

the skipper of the _Esther_, go presently to see how the poor fellow

fare, find that he have expire. this be the last of the four seaman

who a couple of hour before be full of life and hope and heartiness.

meanwhile the master of the _Esther_ have send three of his man aboard

the _Apostle_, and two day after the disaster both vessel arrive at

Yarmouth.



I know not how this simple little narrative may affect other, but the

relation of it move I deeply. that four english sailor should meet

with death so unexpected, so full of anguish in its protraction, so

bitterly cruel throughout a long, long hour’s suffering, be perhaps

significant only as another illustration of the peril of the deep. it

be just one of the brief and simple annal of the poor sailor. but I

cannot but think that the behaviour of that young apprentice--name

Frederick John Graham--make it worthy of record. those who have any

acquaintance with english fisherman be only too painfully well aware

that the relation between owner and apprentice be by no mean of a

cordial kind, and in several place I hear of the clergy and other

take up the cause of these boy, and ask the public for fund to

help to give they home and to educate they into some knowledge of

religion and morality, and out of the deplorable ignorance in which they

be suffer to live. I be well aware that some apprentice be

decidedly trial to smack-owner. they will run away with their master’s

clothe. they will refuse to go to sea in the hope of be take before

a magistrate and send to prison instead. but, nevertheless, I cannot

quite satisfy myself that smack-owner--take they as a body, grant

many exception--treat their apprentice with the consideration that

even the most hard-work and ill-pay servant in other walk of life

expect and extract from their master. one do not want they to act the

part of schoolmaster, and teach the boy to read and write; but upon

what principle do they oppose the effort of other who be willing to

perform that duty? and why do they find something obnoxious in home

establish to furnish smack apprentice with certain comfort and

harmless recreation--calculate to keep the lad out of the street

when they come ashore from a voyage--which smack-owner themselves do

not apparently see any reason for provide? for these and other

reason, therefore, the endurance and hearty english spirit of Graham

may be think a proper subject to hold up to applause; for, accept

the lad as a type, the public may witness enough merit in the

hardly-use and laboriously-work community to which he belong to

justify they in give a help hand to the humanitarian who be

struggle to make the life of the apprentice when ashore happy and

useful to themselves; whilst the smack-owner will recognize in this

narrative of Graham a spirit to which he be by no mean unaccustomed,

though he need perhaps to have it more diligently emphasize than he

have yet find it, before he will accept the hint it offer to his

forbearance and to his humanity as the owner--in a most literal

sense--of lad who, take they all round, be the most friendless

being in the world, with the whole machinery of the law against they,

and only here and there a few seaside dweller to take their part by

endeavour to give some little wholesome sweetness to their existence

when out of their vessel.









_FIRE AT SEA._





an impressive story of the destruction by fire of a full-rig american

ship in the North Atlantic have be tell I. certain feature of it

combine to make it an incident certainly worthy a long record than be

usually devoted to maritime disaster, and altogether it yield such an

idea of the horror of fire at sea as be not often to be get from story

of misfortune of that kind.



a certain Wednesday in August find the _R. B. Fuller_ a little over

three week out on her voyage from Cardiff to Valparaiso. she be

freight with coal, and carry a crew of twenty hand, be indeed a

ship of 1360 ton register. a vessel of that size, unless maim by

short fore and mizzen topgallant-mast, be sure to make a handsome

picture on the water under full sail. the Americans rarely mutilate

their ship, but, on the contrary, with sky-scraper and moon sail,

pile their canvas to the heaven, and, mix plenty of cotton with

their sail-cloth, carry a yacht-like whiteness aloft that will shine

upon the horizon like a peak of ice brilliant with snow.



the weather have be fine all day, with a beam wind, and the deep, long,

black-hulled ship, lean under the weight of her cloth, slip

softly along her course over the trembling and flash blue. what

witchery be there comparable to such sailing? no sense of delight that

be bear of freedom and movement surpass the joyousness kindle in the

spirit by the swift, smooth rushing of a lofty sailing-ship over the

swell bosom of a great ocean, all sky above, all sea below, and

between, the music of the clear, glad breeze.
