the sun sink and the night gather, the wind fine down, and the

american ship, with spar erect, float over the dark water, in which

the starlight seem to flake away in small coil of quicksilver. over

the side nothing could be hear but the tinkling of the ripple at the

stem; aloft there be not a stir, unless it be now and again the

muffle chafing of the foot of a sail upon a stay or the rattle of a

reef-point upon the canvas. Forward all be in shadow, with the figure

of a man on the look-out; whilst aft the mate on duty pace the deck,

pause sometimes to take a peep at the compass-card, where the

binnacle-lamp glisten in the brass centre-bit of the wheel, and shine

upon the face of the officer as he stoop to observe the indication of

the card.



the captain, Mr. Thomas Peabody, have leave the deck about three-quarter

of an hour. he be asleep in his cabin, when, shortly after ten o’clock,

he be awaken by a feeling of suffocation, and perceive that the

cabin be full of smoke. moreover, the atmosphere be charge with a

deadly, nauseating, gaseous smell that give an iron tightness to his

throat and fill his body with an unendurable prickly sensation, as

though strong mustard have be rub into his skin. he rush on deck,

where the fresh air at once revive he, and not immediately perceive

anybody about, shout for the officer of the watch. the chief mate come

run out of the darkness forward, and before Captain Peabody could

address he, cry out that the ship be on fire. the news spread as if

by magic, and in a few moment the deck be alive with the crew

hurry out of the forecastle.



of all cry, none thrill through the heart of a sailor like that of

fire. human helplessness be never so feel as at such a time. the ship be

a burn volcano, from whose cabin the red flame may soar presently,

make a wide circumference of air scorch hot with a furious play of

wither flame. the mate say that he believe the fire be in the hold

under the cabin. forthwith there be a rush to the hatch, which be

immediately close; calk-iron be fetch, and the air be busy

with the hammering of mallet. it be a sight to see the man. there be

no lack of determined courage among they, but the cry of “Fire!” be

ring in their ear; they toil in quick impulsive rush, with

feverish haste, glance to right and leave, know not in what part of

the ship the fire would first show itself in flame. every ventilator be

closed, and the cabin shut up, in the hope of stifle the fire, and the

crew then gather in a group in the waist to watch and wait and see

what their work would do for they.



presently somebody call out that the smoke be still break through.



“look there--and there, sir!”



it be hard to guess how it could escape; the hatch be closed and

calk, every aperture securely block, and yet there be the smoke

break out from all part of the vessel as steam rise from the

compact earth. on this the carpenter’s chest be overhaul, and by

order of the captain the man fall to work to bore hole in the deck. as

the solid plank be pierce the smoke belch forth in puff, mingle

with a pestilential exhalation of gas that force the seaman to work

with avert face. the pump be then man, the hose get along,

bucket drop over the side, and all hand turn-to to drown the fire

by discharge water into the glow cargo. cloud of steam come up

through the hole, regularly follow, as the white vapour thin, by

spiral column of black smoke which wound round and round to the height

of the maintop, where the light breeze catch and arch they over. no

flame be as yet visible, but the man know that the ship be full of

fire, that at any instant the hatch might be rive and shrivel up

by a discharge of flame, and therefore when the captain give order to

lower the boat there be a rush to the davit.



when the boat be in the water alongside, the captain, desire to

save certain article, call the mate and four seaman to accompany he

to the cabin; but they have not be there a minute when they suddenly

run out, some of they vomit blood, and all of they complain that

their head be swell so that they be like to burst. indeed, but

for their speedy flight, they must have drop dead in an atmosphere

that be render virulently poisonous by the combine gas and smoke. a

short spell of rest and fresh air recover the poor man, and the crew

then proceed to victual the boat with such provision as they could

come at. the mainyard be brace aback, and the man enter the boat

and row to a distance of about half a mile from the vessel, where they

remain.



it be a fine night, very calm, and the ship, with her mainyard aback,

lie steady. hour after hour go by, but no flame show itself, though

there be a gradual thickening of the smoke from the deck, and the

seaman could observe it hang in a shadow over the masthead of the

vessel and to leeward of she. gaze at she as she stand like a marble

carving upon the dark sea, it be difficult for the man to realize that

her hold be a conceal furnace; that by take off one of the hatch

and look down they could have behold an incandescent interior, a

red-hot surface like a lake of fire, with blue and green flame crawl

over it, and masse of smoke, repel or consume by the intense heat

of the central space. but for the shadow overhang her glimmering

height, there be no sign that anything be amiss with the ship.

survey from the low level of the boat, she look a majestic fabric

out there, a brave sight in the faint, fine starlight. it be a long,

weary, and bitter vigil for the poor fellow to keep. they would not

leave the neighbourhood of the vessel while she remain afloat. they

could not tell what might happen. if she burst into flame the light she

make might bring they help. or the fire might die out and so give they

their home to return to. whilst she be there, she be, in a manner,

something to hold on to; for it be a fearful thing to look away from

she into the mystery of the darkness around, and to think of be leave

to struggle amid that black and fathomless desert of water in open

boat, which bring the mighty deep within reach of their hand.



slowly the long hour go by, and then the dawn come, and the sun

uprose. with the first of the grey light every eye be turn upon the

ship. they could see the shroud of smoke that overhang she, yet not a

spark of fire have be visible throughout the night, and this, now that

the sunshine be on the sea, begot a hope in the man that, though to be

sure the smoke crawl thickly from the ship, the fire be not so bad as

they have fear, and that a long and resolute struggle might enable

they to conquer it. accordingly the oar be throw over, and the boat

head for the vessel. the boat occupy by the captain be the first to

get alongside. he jump on board, and be follow by other; but the

heat of the deck strike through his boot make he put his hand to

the plank. it be like touch hot iron. he walk to the cabin, but

on feel the door he withdraw his finger with a groan. the whole

fabric be full of fiery heat; whatever touch the flesh give it pain;

the very rope which lie coil over the belay pin be too hot to

handle; the pitch be bubble in the seam; the air between the

bulwark resemble the atmosphere of a furnace; in the haze of the heat

every object seem to revolve like a corkscrew; and the man in the

boat say that feel her side, even to the level of the water-line,

be as bad as put the hand upon a boiler full of steam.



a cry from one of the seaman who have come over the side in bare foot,

raise a kind of panic among those already aboard. “over with you,” be

the shout, “before she burst into a blaze!” and in mad haste the poor

fellow drop over the bulwark, seize their oar, and resume the

same distance from the ship that they have occupy all night.



soon after this a small breeze of wind arise. it seem to penetrate the

vessel, for with the draught there soar up a thick body of smoke. her

passage to leeward be perceptible in the short, oil-smooth wake to

windward of she; but the drift of the boat be the same as her, so

that the man have no need to use their oar to maintain their distance.

there be now weight enough in the wind to blow the smoke clear of the

deck before it rise a foot above the bulwark, so that the picture of

that full-rig ship remain there in its completeness. as the time

pass the man would see a fountain of spark hove up occasionally in

the smoke. it be dismal work sit and watch that fine ship

smouldering. all that the man possess be leave aboard of she; they have

come away, most of they, in their shirt and trouser, many without

shoe, and there in those three boat they sit look at the burning

vessel, silent in the main, often glance around they on the look-out

for a sail, and hold on to the thwart or gunwale as the boat

jerk and topple sharply about on the bit of a sea that the wind have

raise. a little before noon those who have their eye on the ship

perceive the mizzenmast to sway to and fro a moment; then suddenly it

fall with a crash; a rush of smoke, like a monstrous balloon, hover

over the quarter-deck and conceal the ruin; but it soar into the

air, and sail away on the wind under a sudden furious discharge of

spark, which resemble the explosion of a mass of rocket, and when the

vapour have settle down it be see that the mizzenmast be over the

side, the vessel a wreck aft, whilst forward the sail be dusky and

red, as though iron-stain, with the blowing of the sooty coil and the

fire of the glowing spark.



until the night come down no further alteration take place in the

appearance of the vessel. during all those long hour the man sat

crouch in their boat, watch their burn ship and search the

sea for the help that do not come. the second night roll down dark,

with windy cloud drift across the sky. here and there the

phosphorus shine in the curl of a break surge. the half-clothe man

shiver under the fresh night wind; but the ship whilst she stay

there be a beacon. if they quit she, what be there to do? she be a

dreadful signal upon the dark sea, and might yet bring succour, and so

they stay. but the darkness have not gather an hour when a tongue of

red flame dart out of the deck abaft the mainmast. it throw out a

great light, like the flash of a big gun, and the man could see one

another’s face in it. it sink and seem to expire, and then there

rush up a body of crimson spark which clearly define the dense and

swell volume of smoke that blot out the heaven in the south-east;

but speedily the flame sweep aloft again like a serpent, wreathe

itself around the mainmast; then forward and apparently out of the fore

hatch spring up another pillar of fire, and presently there be tongue

and lance of flame crawl and hiss all over the doom vessel,

glide in serpentine convolution along her bulwark, over her stern,

around her bow, limn the configuration of her hull with burning

pencil, fill whole league of the darkness with light. the stay,

the shroud, all the gear connect with the bowsprit and jibboom,

catch fire; the yard be kindle; the whole outline of the vessel be

score in fire upon the night; every detail of the standing mast and

yard and sail, the crosstree, outrigger, and top--all the furniture

of the ship’s deck, the boat-davit, the cathead, the martingale, the

spritsail yard, be express in flame. it be like the picture of a

ship draw in fire upon a black curtain. not a sound come from the man

in the boat. they watch breathless, full of amazement, thought of

their serious position be overwhelm by the dreadful but magnificent

sight of that noble ship. when suddenly the burn vessel open, a

flame such as might go up from Vesuvius soar into the air, make a

roar noise upon the wind; there be a sound of the fall of the

burning mast and yard; and then, in a breath, the whole terrific

picture vanish; it disappear as you might blow out a candle; the

boom of an explosion come dully up against the wind, and there be

nothing but the star and the black sea and a dense shadow in the

south-east where the smoke from the foundered ship be heavily sail

away.



if ever loneliness be feel at sea it be feel by those man when that

great light go out, and leave they in darkness and dread and

uncertainty. but enough if I say that after toss about for two day

and night, they sight a sail to the westward, which they chase until

they be sufficiently near for her people to see they. she prove to be

the London barque _paracca_, whose captain gladly receive the poor

fellow and treat they with the utmost humanity.









_SEA-SICKNESS._





many will remember the terrible description of Mr. Aaron Bang’s pang of

sea-sickness in “Tom Cringle.” it be fortunate that everybody whilst

suffer from nausea be not so demonstrative as the West Indian

planter. the horror of a rough passage between Calais and Dover would

be fearfully increase be the prostrate passenger to bewail amid

their throe the wine and dish which old Neptune exact from they.

and yet one have only to consider what kind of heaving sea it be that

set the West Indian howl for brandy-and-water to commiserate the poor

old epicure’s noisy anguish. sailor will appreciate the affect upon a

passenger’s stomach of a heavy gale of wind drop as if by magic and

leave the sailing vessel--for Tom Cringle flourish before the day

of steam--roll upon a tremendous swell. a steamer whose screw or

paddle be revolve and drive the hull through the water will not,

amidst the heavy sea, give you the same sensation you get from a

vessel tumble about on a strong, fine-weather swell, not a breath of

air to steady she or give her way. the steamer in a measure escape the

bad of the sea by slide out of they; her bow be lift clear of

the washing coil whilst her lee sponson be bury, and she half jump

the intervene hollow as her paddle thrust she from the summit of the

surge. often have I watch this behaviour in swift steamer, and see

they take a bow or beam sea as a horse take a hurdle.



but the motion of a vessel becalm amid a heavy swell be one of the

most uncomfortable of all sea-experience. let the mere relic of

nausea linger in the human breast, and this movement shall make a

full-blow anguish of it. I have hear of steward, man who have make a

dozen voyage round the world--whose stomach be as immovable in a

gale of wind as the ship’s figurehead;--I have hear of such man, I say,

in a heavy breathless swell, tumble down among their dish too sick

to stand, roll about among the crockery and echo with their groan

the spasmodic gurgling of the water as it sob in the scupper-hole or

wash up full, green, and sicken over the glass of the scuttle or

the cabin window.



this sort of tumblefication be fast become a thing of the past among

passenger, very few of whom nowadays make their voyage in sailing

ship, although it be by no mean yet an extinct feature of the

emigrant’s progress from the old world to Australia and New Zealand. at

such time as this the ship be as sea-sick as any of the yellow and

haggard sufferer who moan in her cabin; squeak and cry and the

rumbling of a disorder internal organization resound in her hold. over

she lean like a fainting creature, and the bubble wash of water

alongside deliver a note full of nauseating suggestion; the beating of

the canvas against the mast send a shiver through the hull; down drop

her counter amid a swirl of gurgle eddy, the stern-post complain,

the rudder jar, the wheel chain harshly strain; and then up, slowly

and giddily, mount the after end of the staggering fabric, make the

pale and helpless holder-on there feel that his brain be descend

into his boot, and that his bowel be rise to fill the emptiness of

his skull, whilst sharp report of crash crockery break out through

the skylight, the cask that have break adrift on the main-deck roll to

and fro and defy the pursuit of the three or four seaman who dodge

about after it and go sprawl over one another into the scupper, the

pig under the long-boat scuffle and snort, chest and box fetch away

in the cabin, the sailor flounder over the cable range as they stagger

out of the galley with hook pot of tea in their hand, and the

sea-blessing shower out by the cook as he chase his dish and pan

and burn his finger in his effort to save the cuddy dinner, can be

hear by the man at the wheel and the youngster who be shift the

dog-vane at the main-royal masthead.



this, I say, be an old experience; but it be a time to try the stomach

whilst it last. think of three or four day and three or four night

of it! in these day if you be sea-sick you at least have the

satisfaction of know that the ship be always go ahead, and that

the day, if not the hour, when your nausea will have terminate may be

pretty accurately fix. and yet what man hang over the side or

prostrate on his back and execrate existence can get satisfaction out

of the thought that, bad as his suffering be, they might be bad by

be protract? I believe there be some people who, when once their

head be fairly over the rail, or when what Thackeray call the

“expectaroon” be between their knee, be inspire by such a loathing

for life that they be not to be move by the wild threat of

destruction. once, in cross from Calais to Dover, I notice a vast

pile of luggage, unsecured by a single lashing, heap up on the

fore-deck. all be well until we get clear of the french coast, when a

small beam sea set the vessel rolling. in a few minute the bulwark,

from the sponson to the eye, be crowd with people of both sex

and various nationality, all engage in raise their voice in the

most dismal manner, wipe their cheek, and cast bloodshot glance

around they, only to direct their gaze again with hideous rapidity upon

the giddy white water that rush in a spin dance aft while they

explode in loud roar. I look with alarm at the nodding pile of

luggage, feel sure that an extra lurch would tumble the whole over

and seriously injure the unhappy sick people on one side or other of the

vessel. I speak to a french sailor--they be all Frenchmen aboard that

steamer--and advise he to secure the luggage. he merely shrug his

shoulder and make off. I address another, who could not or would not

understand I. thereupon I go up to the sea-sick people, and touch

first one and then another, I point to the tower of luggage and

advise they to go far aft, out of the way of the box, lest they

should tumble upon they. they must have see their danger as plainly as

I, but not one of they offer to move. they keep a tight hold of the

rail, merely turn their lacklustre eye upon I with an expression in

they, half implore, half savage, as much as to say, “let the box

come! let we be crush! what stop the box from fall?” fortunately

the second sailor I accost perceive by this time that if the luggage

be not secure the top box bid fair to go overboard when the

strong sea of the mid-channel be reach; and so among they the

Frenchmen bind the box to the deck by rope, and by so do, in my

humble opinion, save several valuable sea-sick life.



in this same journey I be amuse by an aspect of sea-sickness, or let

I say a condition of it, that will be familiar to many who make short

passage by water. go forward of the funnel, where smoking be not

prohibit, I take notice of a gentleman wear an eye-glass. he be

clothe in a yachting coat, embellish with brass button, and he be

smoke a large cigar. a very stout Frenchman be ask he some

question in broken English--I hear the gentleman with the eye-glass

say that he believe there be a pretty middling sea on outside, but “if

you’re afraid of be sick, mounseer, you should smoke, sir. you should

do as I do. nothing like tobacco for settle the stomach;” and he give

a horribly confident laugh. the corpulent Frenchman withdraw with a

groan, and lodge himself in the gloom under the bridge near the

engine, the vibration of which cause his immense body to quiver like a

jelly on a supper-table when people be dance overhead, and there he

lie so clamorously ill that the fireman drop their shovel below to

come up and look at he.



meanwhile I keep my eye on the gentleman who believe in tobacco, and

when the steamer take the first of the sea I see he seize hold of a

shroud or a funnel stay and set his leg wide apart. he continue

puff at his cigar for some time but the interval between remove

and lift it to his mouth grow long and long; presently it go

out, but he take no notice. he have his glass in his eye and his face

look forward; he be deplorably pale, and I never could have believe

that such a trifling thing as a brass button and so prosaic an object as

a nautically-cut coat could become, on occasion, more cuttingly ironical

than anything a man’s friend could say of he. the eye-glass give this

gentleman an unusually glaring expression; he never shift his gaze--I

should say that he never wink. there he stand with his leg wide

apart, the extinguished cigar in one hand and the other support he

with a death-grip, stare with horrible intensity at nothing. I know

perfectly well that if that man be make to shift his posture or speak

he would rush to the rail.



it be a brave fight; but it could not last. a young coloured gentleman,

the ashiness of nausea visible in his dark skin, suddenly jump up from

under the pile of luggage, where he have be screen himself from the

wind, and, bolt to the side, expend himself in a howl full of the

deep throaty noise that be peculiar to negro. it be irresistible; the

man with the eye-glass let go, and stagger away, with his cigar go

and his hand extend. I fear that he would find no room, for the

bulwark be line with sufferer; but, with the selfishness of acute

suffering, he plump with all his might between a couple of Frenchmen,

squeeze the aperture between they open with his elbow, and fix

himself there; and there he remain until the water grow smooth near

the english cliff, and the steamer go forward on a steady keel.



it be difficult to understand why people should find anything divert

in sea-sickness, than which surely nothing can cause more suffering. of

course, if a man will give himself air ashore or on smooth water, use

nautical word, and deride the misgiving other be honest enough to

confess to, then, indeed, if we find that marine gentleman with his head

in a basin, or with his face over the side and his hat go, we have

some excuse to laugh at he. there be people who never will own that

they be sick at sea, just as there be people who deny with indignation

that they snore in their sleep. such folk deserve our ridicule. for

what be there to be ashamed of? I have know old sea-captain quit ship

newly arrive from around the world and be ill on a voyage from London

Bridge to Hull. if such man can shout for the steward without blush,

it be hard to know why Jones, of the Middle Temple, or Smith, of the

Stock Exchange, or Snooks, the celebrate novelist, should sneak to the

side and feel humble if his fellow-sufferer see he blue in the face

with his pocket-handkerchief half-way down his throat. it may be that

people laugh at sea-sick sufferer because of the enormously and by

consequence absurdly level character of the malady. one might be the

most compassionate creature live and yet find it impossible to stop

laugh at the debasement of the high and mighty personage who, when he

come aboard, people whisper be the right honourable so-and-so, or the

acute and famous Mr. Justice Somebody else. he sit aloof, he be full of

dignity, he scarcely raise even a condescend eye from the book or

paper in his hand to glance at the other passenger, who sit doggedly,

if humbly, wait for the wheel to go round, inside and outside. I say

that a man must be more than human if he can help laugh when the high

and mighty personage change colour, when he put his paper down and

roll his eye about, when nothing seem to keep his head on but his

shirt-collar, and when an invincible horror of life gleam in that gaze

which have grow hollow with surprising rapidity. alas! no amount of

reputation, no social importance, no eloquence, which in other place

might affect the heart and even improve the understanding, can save he.

yonder in the bow be a poor little cockney, a second-class passenger,

in a shabby coat and his trouser half-way up his leg, sick beyond the

power of description; there be no bench long enough on that vessel to

furnish room for he and the great man at once if the water be smooth;

but Nausea have wave her wand, and the humble little cockney and the

high and mighty personage be brother and equal, fellow-sufferer,

with all distinction vanish between they as, with yellow face, the

cockney forward, the great man aft, they overhang the rush foam with

open mouth, the tear pour from their eye, and anguish inimitably

express in the curve of their back and the occasional kick-up

deliver by their leg.



more pathetic, perhaps, be the newly-married couple, though many a cruel

laugh and jeer have be direct even at they. but nothing be sacred at

sea. sentiment that be full of poetry in drawing-room, among flower,

under the moonshine, among hedge, take another character among rough

water.



I remember once cross fifty mile of sea in company with a young

gentleman and his bride. they be return, I take it, from their

honeymoon. they sit together upon a small, uncomfortable bench fix

against the inside of the paddle-box, whence they command a fine view

of the action of the engine, and where the smell of the oil-can hung

steadily in the wind. they both know they be go to be sick, and sit

with hand lock, two devoted heart bent on suffer together. the

steward--a pale, large, sandy-haired man--considerately anticipate

their want by place a couple of basin at their foot. the dismal

implement make but a melancholy foreground for the impassione pair,

and I wonder how they would like to have have their photograph take

in that posture. a quarter of an hour suffice to make the picture

tragical. the wife lean across the husband and the husband hold on to

she. his heroic devotion be immense; I could hear he in guttural

accent pour consolation into her deaf ear amid the interval of his

own convulsion, and when an unusually heavy roll to leeward cause both

basin to slide away out of sight under the bench, I never behold

anything more touching than his struggle to replace they without

let go of his wife.



happily, however, the heart be occasionally steel against such object

of misery as this by spectacle of selfishness and fear in the last

degree contemptible. I particularly recall a gaunt Frenchman with a

spiked moustache, who, long before nausea afflict he, refuse to stir

from his seat to help his miserable, prostrate wife, and who answer

her murmur to Emile to put something under her head and something over

her foot, by fierce command to she to hold her tongue. this wretched

man be himself seize with nausea, and so great be his fear--either

excite by the somewhat heavy sea that wash alongside the vessel or by

his suffering, which to judge from the noise he make, must have lead he

to suppose that, bit by bit, the whole of he be go overboard--that

after every explosion I could hear he shriek, “Maman! maman!” like a

girl.



what be the remedy for sea-sickness? I wish I know--most cheerfully

would I impart the secret. there be many prescription, from the ice of

Dr. Chapman to Jack’s lump of fat pork attach to a ropeyarn; but

nothing seem to answer the end design. nor be it very remarkable that

the wonderful vessel which be to put an end to nausea should still

leave the “expectaroon,” even on their own deck, the useful piece of

furniture passenger have for generation find it; for whilst clever

gentleman have show we how the effect of the rolling and pitch

movement of a ship upon the head or stomach may be overcome by pivot

saloon and swinge accommodation, they have entirely fail to

produce any kind of mechanism to obviate the consequence of those

movement of a vessel in a seaway which be alone responsible for

sickness; I mean the heave up and the swoop down. if a ship oscillate

on an immutable basis, a cot or a balanced chair would effectually stop

nausea; like a wineglass on a swinge tray, the passenger could always

maintain a posture perpendicular with the horizon. but what be to

qualify the sensation which follow the swoop down into the hollow and

the roaring heave up on to the summit of the sea? everything in the

ship must accompany she in her fall and in her rising; and it be this

motion which send people rush to the side, which set they roar

for the steward, which cause they to loathe life and to lie with their

head anywhere and their foot anyhow.



I cannot help think, however that imagination contribute something,

and often a very great deal, to sea-sickness; otherwise how be we to

account for people suffer from nausea actually before they step on

board the vessel that be to carry they? if a sea-sick man could be send

to sleep his suffering would cease; yet the vessel go on roll, and

if it be this movement, affect the stomach, that cause nausea, I

cannot quite see why the stomach should not be as sympathetic in sleep

as in wake. any way, I believe that a person could be make to forget

to be sea-sick by have his imagination intensely occupy or his fear

excited. let a vessel full of sea-sick people drive ashore, or catch

fire, or be in collision; let the captain bawl out, “we be all lose;”

it would be interesting to conjecture how much sickness would remain

aboard that ship. a good prescription might be a profoundly exciting

novel: some hideous mystery so distractingly complicate as to make one

sink all thought of wave and steward in the eagerness to discover

whether the figure Sir Jasper see be really a ghost or his first wife,

and whether it be her ladyship or the groom she run away with who shoot

Signor Squallini in the throat and do the fine art a real service. but

it be well to be sea-sick than in danger; and, if the novelist can do

nothing for we, I be afraid there be no alternative but to go on fee

the steward and build swift vessel.









_a log extract._





the follow entry be make in the official log-book of a ship name

the _Oxford_:--“Fifth November, 1882, Sunday, 4.0 p.m., lat. 35° 39´ S.,

long. 18° 53´ E., W. Waters, A.B., while furl the mizzen-topsail fall

from the yard into the sea, strike the half round of the poop in his

fall. a lifebuoy be promptly throw he, the ship bring to the

wind--it blow a fresh gale from the S.W., with thick weather and a

heavy sea at the time. the port lifeboat be at once lower, and

proceed under the charge of Mr. A. Bowling, second mate, to pick up

the man. owing, however, to the shock sustain by he in strike the

ship, and his be encumber with oilskin, etc., he sink before the

boat could reach he. after an unsuccessful search, the boat return to

the ship and be with difficulty hoist up, owe to the heavy sea

which half fill she. everything be do that could be do to save

the poor fellow. (sign) J. BRADDICK, Master.”



now, here be the whole story, as who would not suppose? the sailor

drop overboard, a boat unsuccessfully search for he, and then the

ship brace her mainyard round and sail away. but extract from

log-book, I have take notice, be like the little box which the

fisherman in the “Arabian Nights” find upon the sea-shore; when it be

open a wonderful creature shape itself out, and its figure fill the

sky. I particularly realize this when Mr. Bowling give I a sketch of

the yarn of which Captain Braddick’s log be the brief hint. why, what

a world of adventure, of heroism, of peril grow up out of these marine

entry! be it four line about a ship rescue a crew from a sinking

vessel; or about a captain come across a smack’s boat in the middle of

the North Sea, with nothing in she but a little crouch, starve boy;

or about a brig find drift helplessly, with her crew, dead of frost,

lie upon her deck? assuredly in those four line there be the making

of a thrilling volume to any man who shall faithfully put his hand to

the work and, exaggerate nothing, relate merely the adventure as it

befell, and how it come about and end, and what the actor in it say,

and do, and think. here, in very brief form, be Mr. Bowling’s own

yarn, tell with my pen, of an incident as common pretty nearly in its

way at sea as the sight of froth blow into a hollow, or of the curve

of the bow-wave flash green and glass-smooth from the shearing

cutwater.



“we leave Calcutta on Sept. 4, 1882, with a full cargo, bind for the

port of London. all go well--if by well you’ll understand nothing

extraordinary outside spell of bothersome head wind, dead calm, and

now and again a twister over the quarter to give we leg--until come

Sunday, Nov. 5, on which date you’ll see by the extract from the

log-book where we be; the glass stand low, and in the morning there

be a kind of wild wet light in the sun when he spring up from behind

the dull-coloured sea, and the lustre that come along with he seem to

roll on the top of the swell as if it be burn oil lie there

instead of be the up and down flash of fair weather, when the

light sound the very bottom of the ocean with its silver lead-line, as

you may see for yourself if you’ll watch the break of day under a pure

sky and over clean blue water. we be under topgallant-sail, on the

starboard tack, the wind about west, with weight enough in it to swear

by, and a slow gathering of haze all along the horizon over the port

quarter--south-west the bearing would be about--and a thick,

deep-breathing swell come out of it, tumble by the wind into a bit of

a sea that wash with a stormy noise along the bend, and make the ship

as uncomfortable as an old cab on a road full of stone.



“I have charge of the deck, and not like the look of the weather, I

go below to tell the captain about it. he have be up pretty near all

the night that be go, and be in his cabin take some rest. but

there’s very little rest for shipmaster, who need to have as many eye

as you find in a peacock’s tail, that they might close two or three of

they at a time, if ever they’re to get the amount of sleep that all

other kind of people, bar nautical man, find needful to keep

themselves alive on. well, sir, I call the captain and tell he that

the weather look threaten, and straightway he come on deck and take

a squint around. the wind be freshen slowly and surely, and the

topsail and topgallant-sail, out of whose cloth the wet of last

night’s squall of rain be not yet dry, be stretch as if they

would burst under it; and the water to leeward wash like boil milk

all along the scupper as the ship be rush by the pressure, take

the sea with a float jump, and make they roar as she split they

with her sharp stem and send they seethe in white smother on either

hand. there be cloud crawl up out of the thickness in the west and

south, and pass like smoke over the masthead, and there be a look

of race about the whole ocean with the sailing of those bit of

vapour, and the pelting of the ship, and the wild hurrying roll of

the sea, along which there be sea-bird screech as they skim in

their low flight through the drive spray in pursuit of we.



“well, sir, the fore topgallant-sail be furl and the watch lie aft to

roll up the mainsail; but not for long do we hold on with the main

topgallant-sail; that be clew up soon, and the wind freshen as sail

be diminish; so that, although half strip of canvas, the ship be

heel to it as before, whilst there be the hard look of a gale of

wind in the sky that you see grey between the scud; and the thickness

be blow up near and nearer, make a mere biscuit’s-throw of the

horizon, so that the sea look lump thing as they roll, all of a

sudden like, out of the haze, and be under the ship and stand up on

either hand of she almost as fast as they seem to be form. we be

now under topsail and foresail only--of the square canvas--when on a

sudden there come a bit of a lull, and a sort of silence aloft that

sound strange after the roaring, and a great noise of washing water

all around; and then plump sweep up the wind in a wild out-fly out of

the south-west, drive the ship forwards until the foam of the cutwater

look to be smother her head. all hand be call to shorten sail,

the three upper topsail halliard be let go, the starboard brace

round in, and the helm shift to bring the ship to her course. four

able seaman and four boy go aloft to furl the upper mizzen-topsail.

you know the old story: the light hand well out, the old hand in the

sling and quarter, and the sail swell up like a sheet of iron to

the wind that blow fair into it in a storm betwixt the two yard. I have

my eye on those man I be speak of, when a blast like a squall sweep

the canvas out of their fist, and in a breath one of they fall with a

twirl and a toss of his clench hand off the yard, strike the

half-round of the poop a blow that come along with the yell of the wind

in a frightful thud; and with that, rebound as a ball might, over he

go into the yeast and froth alongside. it be a horrible thing to

happen; it will stop the breathing of the strong for a minute. the

fellow on the yard roar out, ‘man overboard!’ I spring aft, and have a

life-buoy in my hand in an instant, which I throw fair, as I pray and

believe, to the yellow patch of sou’wester that I see dark on the foam

of the side of a sea; but the wind blow the light thing, like a feather,

to leeward of he. but he be swim--there be life in he, though,

man, you should have hear the thump of his fall, and then think of

he struggle there with his great sea-boot full of water, and his

heavy oilskin drag he down, and a rushing of froth over his head

every time that a sea sweep he up into the snow of its break crest.

well, sir, we go to work smartly; the hand came tumble down from

aloft, and the ship be bring to with her main-topsail aback, whilst

half a dozen of we be obey with mad haste the order to clear away

the quarter-boat ready for lower.



“meanwhile a hand remain in the mizzen-topsail yard to keep the poor

fellow in sight, and he be shout that the man be swim, and

swimming strong; that he don’t seem to see the life-buoy, but that he

be struggle bravely; and I, see this too, and drive half mad by

the pitiful sight of that sailor and shipmate fight the whole ocean,

as I may put it, and battle it with an english seaman’s courage, sing

out, ‘who’s go to volunteer for the boat?’ there be no hanging back;

it be just a leap to see who should be first. as fast as they could

tumble in, there they be, six of they, the pick of the crew--merchant

seaman, sir, whom we’re be teach to despise; there they be, I say,

with the other handle the fall, and every one look as if the

saving of the life of the man astern be his business and nobody’s else;

for he be a shipmate, and that mean a brother at sea, sir, when the

forecastle hold real sailor.



“it be four o’clock in the afternoon, and the mist be drive between

the mast. I be in charge of the boat, but try my dead good I could not

help she be badly stove before we get away, and the water come in

fast as we head for the spot where the man be last see. you must go

through it to realize the difference between the deck of a ship pitching

and roll, no matter how heavily, and the feel of an open boat

release from her side in the same sea. the solid deck you’re fresh from

make the contrast fearfully sharp, and I can well believe what I

remember read in your yarn of the wreck of the _Indian Chief_, that

the survivor of her crew when in the lifeboat own to be more

frightened by the fearful tossing and jumping of the buoyant craft than

they be when in their foretop, with the hull of the ship go to

piece under they. we could only pull four oar, for two man have all

their work in bale the boat, one with a sou’wester and the other with

a sea-boot, those be our baler. my duty lie at the helm, in watch

for the man and look out for the sea. bitterly cold it be, the sun

go down, the haze thick around, and the ship a mere heaving darkness

upon it when we have measure but a few length from she. I look

narrowly about I, but could see nothing of the man. sometimes a lump of

green water tumble over the foam would show like his head, and my

heart would leap: but the next moment the clear sea would roll away from

the blow froth and explain what the deception be. we pull to the

buoy, but it be empty.



“then one of the man say, ‘suppose even Bill have not be hurt by the

fall, surely he couldn’t live in such a sea as this.’ and another say,

‘think of his wrap and oilskin, sir. the good swimmer in the world

couldn’t hold up all these minute under such drag.’ but they speak not

as if they wish to give up, but as if prepare themselves for the

disappointment. have he hurt himself more than we could know? be he

break and die when he touch the water, and be his struggle there

the despairing effort of a broken and die man? we strain our eye,

but could see nothing save the boil head of the sea which came

roar down upon we and threaten with every desperate swing to fill

the boat. still we keep up heart. ‘another pull, boy! impossible to go

back without he!’ I would cry, whilst the two fellow in the bottom

be chuck the water out over the side, and the thickness stand like

a wall around.



“well, for three-quarter of an hour do we hang about, pull in all

direction, and think only of find and save he; and then we

give up and look round for the ship. I could not see she. I sing out

to the man, ‘do you see the ship?’ and they turn their head upon

their shoulder to look; and the chap in the bow cry out, when we be

stand nearly end on up the side of a sea, ‘there she be, I think.’



“well, to be sure, I could see she, but it might as well have be the

thickening of the mist that way as the ship, for she make a shadow

scarcely noticeable, and I look with dismay at the distance that lay

before we to row over, and at the water that be come into the boat as

fast as the two man could bale it out, and at the terrible sea around

we. we have get into such a situation that the sea run right abeam, and

every send drive we to leeward, and sometimes the mist sweep down so

thick that there be never a man of we all who could see the ship,

though, thank be to Heaven, it do not come to our lose sight of she

for good. it be a bad job for we that the heaving and straining of the

boat cause she to leak bad and bad. but for she leak I could

have put the two man who be bale she to the oar, and they would

have be just the sort of help we want; instead of which they be

scarcely able to prevent the boat from fill. it would, however, have

be destruction to we to have set more man than those two at the job

they be on. every moment be precious; the afternoon be fast wane;

in a short while the night would be upon we, and I know quite surely

that if it come before we fetch the ship we be doom man. oh, sir,

it be a fierce bit of labour. in the midst of our struggle a squall of

sleet blow down and hide the whole surface of the ocean to within our own

length of we; but it clear off, and when it be go the mist thin

somewhat, and give we a well view of the ship, at whose peak we could

see a colour streaming as a signal of recall. never be any man of we

near to death in his life than he be during the time we occupy in

reach the vessel. that we do reach she you may reckon, or I should

not be here to tell you this story. but by the hour we have pull across

her head and drop along the port side of she, the water in the boat

be up to the thwart, we show scarce more than our gunwale, it be

almost dark, the sea have increase in volume, and the wind be blow

half a hurricane. we be fairly exhausted when we gain the deck, but

humbly grateful as we be for the preservation of our life, ne’er a

one of we could cast a look over the quarter in the place where our

shipmate have go down, and where the darkness of the evening now lay,

with the white foam show with startling clearness upon the side of

those black rush hill, without feel that our thankfulness would

have be deep have we be allow to rescue the man whom we have be

very near to lose our life to save.”









_in an open BOAT._





Loneliness have many form. it be Selkirk, imprison in an island, with

nothing but the wash of the surf to break the shocking stillness; it be

the mountain-climber miss his way, and pass the long night amid

the tremendous silence of tower hill and black valley; or it be the

loneliness describe by Byron, that of a man solitary in crowd. but

what sense of solitude can equal that feel by shipwreck man in a small

open boat, surround by a universe of water, with no other chance for

their life than such as a pass ship may bring? it be not the first

hour, nor yet the first day; the agony of such a trial lie in the slow

maddening of the mind by fruitless expectation; the deception of the

white shoulder of cloud, which look like ship as they seem to linger

a moment upon the horizon before sail above it; the straining of the

aching sight against the pitiless, vacant sea-line; the sense that death

be close at hand, though a hundred death may have be suffer before

the skeleton’s clutch be upon the sufferer.



no kind of human anguish be more terrible, and no story catch a

tight hold of the imagination than those which relate it. generation

have shudder, and generation will yet shudder over the grand and

soul-move description in “Don Juan.” the raft of the _Medusa_ be an

immortal horror. the narrative which be at once the most fascinating

and depress in the marine record be always those which concern the

suffering of human being adrift in an open boat in the midst of a

great ocean. the deep be unchanging in the misery it work. our ship

be of iron; they be propel through the calm sea by an irresistible

power fast than a gale of wind would drive they; they be of

proportion so colossal that many of they could sle the “tall

schippe” of our forefather over their side, and stow they on skid as

they stow their boat; and yet just the same sort of suffering be

endure now by mariner as be experience by they in the day when a

vessel of thirty ton be reckon big enough not only to seek the

North-West Passage but to hunt the unnavigated ocean after continent.



I hear once a story that seem fitter for the lip of an ancient

mariner, like Coleridge’s, than the mouth of a seaman who live in an

age in which the Atlantic be cross in eight day, and in which the

Cape of Good Hope have be pretty nearly extinguish by a narrow

water-way across a hundred mile of sand. the hearing it take I back in

imagination to the day of the ship _Thomas_ of Liverpool, the _Lady

Hobart_ packet, the Yankee ship _Peggy_, the French East India Company’s

_Prince_, and I know not how many more old craft which age since become

phantom vessel, to be wreck again and again upon the dark and

noiseless ocean of tradition.



“my name,” begin my informant, “be William Pearce. I have use the sea

for above eight and twenty year, have sail in all kind of ship in

all sort of capacity--boy, ordinary seaman, sailmaker, bo’sun’s mate;

cross the Atlantic seventeen time, and be round the world eight;

be shipwreck thrice; likewise overboard during seven hour of

darkness, and pick up at daybreak with my head in a lifebuoy; know

pretty nigh the good and the bad of the weather that’s to be find at

sea; and be, therefore, capable of take my oath to this, that of all

the bad job that ever I be in or that ever I hear of any other sailor

be in, there’s nothing to beat the suffering we man of the schooner

_Richard Warbrick_ have to endure when the foundering of that vessel

oblige we to take to the boat.



“the schooner sail from Runcorn with a cargo of coal for Plymouth.

she be twenty year old, and a trifle over a hundred ton burden. there

be five of a crew, and nothing particular happen until we be

abreast of the Bristol Channel, when there blow up a heavy gale of wind

from the east’ard. there’s no call to describe it; it be of the regular

kind, full of wet, and raise a sea a sight too big for a vessel of one

hundred ton pretty nigh chock-a-block with coal and with twenty year

of hard use in her hull. however, we scrape through the gale and two or

three more that follow fast, until one morning we be somewhere

betwixt the Scilly Isles and the cornish coast. it be dark, thick

weather, blow and rain hard, the sea rough, bitter cold--as you

may calculate it be, the month be January--and everything invisible

that be more than half a mile off. the wind be east and north, and we

be ratche along under very small canvas, when, be turn in, as

it be my watch below, and the land o’ Nod close aboard, I be rouse up

by a loud cry on deck and a tremendous crash. I tumble up as fast as

ever I could pelt, and find the schooner go down and the man get

the only boat we carry overboard. it be no time for question. you

could feel the vessel settle under your foot, just like stand on

soft mud and sink in it. the sea be wash over the deck, and

grow heavy as her bulwark sink lower. there be nothing but white

water to be see on the starboard bow--no rock, nothing show above

the froth; but I don’t want any one to tell I that we have run foul of

the Seven Stones. there be no time to do more than launch the boat and

roll into she. Daly be the last man in, and scarce have he jump when

the schooner plump clean out of sight, go down like a deep-sea

lead, so suddenly that it take my breath away.



“there’s no sensation bad than that a man feel when he look for the

ship he’s be force to abandon and find she vanish under the sea.

the ocean never seem so wide as then. the whole world appear to be

make of water. sailor be a class of man little give to talk, and

when they come clear of such job as this they say next to nothing about

it, and so people think that either they’re man without the capacity of

feeling, or else their suffering be not equal to what might be

suppose. have people who take these view be in that boat along with

we, they’d look sharp in alter their opinion. the suddenness of the

disaster--our be one moment safe, and the next tossing on the sea in

a small boat, with the schooner go, nothing save but what we stand

in, not a morsel of food nor a drop of drink of any kind, the wind

blow fit to freeze the eye out of our head, every mother’s son of

we soak to the skin, and drift fast away towards the Atlantic--take

our sense away for a spell. we sit hold on and stare like daft

man. the captain be the first to rally.



“he call out, ‘a bad job; it’s a bad job, lad!’ several time, and

then say, ‘no use let she drive too fast. we mustn’t let she blow

away into the ocean;’ and with that we lash the two oar to the

painter and fling ’em overboard.



“this bring her head to wind and slow her drift; but, for all that,

every hour be carry we far and far towards the open sea, and

away from the Scilly Isles and the cornish coast, which be our good

chance, so that all the hope that be leave we be be pick up by a

pass vessel. yet there could be no bad month in the year than

January for that likelihood. how long be the gale and the frost go

to let we last? we be far to the nor’ard of the fairway, in a part of

the sea that every vessel be bind to give a wide berth to. the

weather, as I have say, be so thick that you couldn’t see half a mile

off, and though of course it be sure to clear in time and open out the

horizon, so that vessel could have a view around they, the question be

where should we be when it come on fine?



“unlike a good many other who have go through such dreadful mess as

this, our suffering begin the moment we tumble into the boat. in the

low latitude that ever I be in I never feel such cold. have the

water be fresh our clothe would have freeze into covering of ice. the

air be full of spray, and squall of sleet came roll up. we sit in

the bottom of the boat in a lump, to keep she steady, and for the

shelter of one another’s body, and those who be to windward--that

be, in the fore part--would shift from time to time, and other take

their place. we have no mast nor sail, nothing but the two oar we ride

to. it be a Monday, and all through the daylight we sit lift our

eye above the gunwale, and try to pierce the haze for a vessel. it

be blow about half a gale of wind, and it keep steady. now and then

we’d ship a dose of water, and bale it out with our cap; but it keep

our foot soak, and I reckon it be bad than be without boot at

all. the boat do well, and the oar be a kind of breakwater, and

help she. after four in the afternoon the night draw on. we never

could get use to the darkness. the daytime be bad enough, but the

night make our suffering madden. the wind, when the sea be black,

would take the feel of solid ice; we couldn’t see one another, and that

make talk a kind of foolishness, and so we never speak, which cause

every one to feel himself a lonely man upon the sea. likewise the noise

of the water would sound strong. in the daytime I take no notice, but

at night I’d find myself listen to the crying of the wind up in the

dark, and the hissing that rise all over the ocean from the breaking of

the wave.



“I don’t know what my mate do; but that first night I never close my

eye, never try to shut they, never think of sleep. I see the dawn

come, but the haze be too thick to let the light show on the horizon;

it be overhead as well as around, when the morning break; there be no

darkness that you’ll find hang in the west at daybreak. indeed, I

believe the sun be up above the sea before any light come, so thick it

be. all the man be awake, and dreadful they look, as of course I

do. one of they be name Burke. I notice he at once, and think he

be die. he lie athwartship with his back against the starboard side

of the boat, and there be a strange working in his finger, like the

movement of a woman’s hand open a skein of thread.



“the captain say, ‘for God’s sake look around, lad, and see if there’s

anything in sight.’



“the sea run high, and make it dangerous for any of we to stand up, for

fear of capsize the boat; so we hang over the gunwale with our chin

on a level with it, and stare into the drive smother with all our

might: but there be nothing to be see but the break sea when we

be hove up, and the water stand like wall on either hand when we

drop into the trough. all at once Burke sit up and begin to sing out

for a drink of water. he talk as if he believe we have it and wouldn’t

give it, which be the first sign of his insanity. the captain try to

pacify he, speak very kindly, and seek to cheer he.



“‘we have outlive a day and a night,’ say he. ‘keep up your heart,

mate; we may have a thousand-ton ship under we before it come dark

again.’



“but Burke keep on cry for water, say that he be die for it,

and point to his throat; and then, fall on all four, he put his

face to the salt water wash about in the bottom of the boat and

suck up several mouthful. well, it seem to do he no hurt, and he

lie quiet. soon after this I spy something knock about in the sea a

few fathom astern, and call the skipper’s attention to it. he say it

be one of some keg of butter that have be aboard the schooner, so we

pull the oar in and drop down to it and pick it up. we break it

open and eat the butter in fistful, be mad with hunger; but it be

as salt as brine, and the effect of it be to make our thirst rage.

the knife we have use to open the keg lie in the bottom of the boat, and

Burke, on a sudden turning over, seize hold of it, jump up, and fall

upon the captain. he hit he once, but the knife don’t pierce through

the thick jacket the skipper have on, and, before he could raise his hand

again, we drag he down and kneel upon he.



“there be no bad part in all that dreadful time than this. the

madman’s face be a terrible sight; almost black it be. he snap

about he with his tooth, and his cry and curse be thing it bring

the sweat upon my face to talk about. think of our situation, mad with

thirst ourselves and struggle with a madman, a kill north-easter

blow like knife through our frozen body, the sea leap and

roar around we, and nothing between we and the bottom but the little

old boat we be in. we be too weak, and in too much suffering

ourselves, to remain hold the madman down, and find he quiet we

let go, and squat one close to another for warmth; but scarcely have

we haul off from the poor wretch when he jump up and throw himself

overboard. ‘mind!’ shout the skipper, ‘one’s enough!’ fear that if

we all get to the side Burke have leap from we should upset the boat. I

be the near, and as he come up close I lean over, and get he by

the hair, and drag he into the boat. he be pretty nigh dead, and

give we no more trouble.



“well, sir, the night come down a second time, find we live, but

without the look of live man. I make sure I should never see another

daybreak. my thirst be not so sharp as it have be; but I don’t know

whether the dull throbbing in my throat, the kind of lockjaw feeling in

my mouth, the burning in my tongue as though it be a lump of hot iron,

be not more torturing than when the craving be fiercer. all night long

it blow a strong wind, with now and then a squall of sleet and rain, and

hour after hour two of the man, Parsons and Daly, be groan in the

bottom of the boat. when the light come, I look to see who be alive,

and my eye fall on Burke, I call out, ‘dead!’ the captain lean

down and feel he, and say, ‘yes, he’s go. he’s the first. God have

mercy upon we!’ and catch hold of my shoulder he stand up to search

the sea, but the haze be as thick as it have be all the time, and he

throw himself down with his hand over his face. presently, look at

the body, he say, ‘we must bury he; but first, my lad, let we say a

prayer for he and for ourselves.’ we all knelt while the captain

pray, and when he have do we lift the body and let it go overboard.



“the madness that thirst create break out strong in Daly and Parsons

when the body be go, and down they drop as Burke have, and lap

up the salt water in the bottom of the boat like dog would. the captain

implore they not to drink, but they never heed he nor I, who

likewise entreat they. however, no harm seem to come of it. well,

sir, there’s no need for I to describe that Wednesday nor our third

night in that open boat. Thursday morning come, make the fourth day,

and to our joy the weather clear, the wind shift and moderated, and

the sea go down. we get the oar in, rig up one as a mast, and two

of we have oilskin coat on, we join they so as to form a sail, make

a yard of the other oar, and put the boat before the wind, which be

blow a light breeze from the south’ard, head, as the captain

judge, for the irish coast. all the day long we keep a wild look-out,

as you may reckon, for any pass ship; but never once, not in the

furth distance, do such an object heave in sight. we might have be

sailing in the middle of the Pacific. nature in we be almost numb. we

have come to such a pass that we be too faint and exhausted to feel the

craving of hunger and thirst. at least I can speak for myself, and it’s

in that way I account for my suffering less at the end than I do at

the beginning of the dreadful time we go through. it be still cold,

but nothing like the bitter cold of the gale and the heavy sea and

squall. we reckon by the sun that the wind hang steady, and we let

the boat slip before it; that be all that could be do. if we be to

sail at all we be bind to keep the breeze over our starn, see

there be nothing to draw but a couple of oilskin secure to the oar.



“but the come on of Thursday night be like the bitterness of death

itself, sir. indeed it be. all day long we have reckon upon sight

something before the sun go. every hour we have hope and pray and

believe would heave up some sort of vessel to come to our rescue; and

therefore, when it draw up black, only a few star among the slow

cloud, and we be bring face to face with another long winter’s

night, my heart fail I altogether; I feel that there be a curse upon

we, and that we be doom man, single out to die of famine, the most

cruel of death, because the long. think of ninety-six hour in an

open boat, in January, in the Chops, a north-east gale blow most of

the time, with never a morsel of food except the salt butter, and no

drink but the salt water washing in the boat! and yet when the Friday

morning come we be still alive, the captain steering, double up with

faintness and the cold, his knee against his mouth, and his head

loll for want of strength in his neck; Daly and Parsons lie still

as dead man under the thwart, and I in the bow, too weak and

break-hearte even to cast my eye around the sea to notice if there

be a vessel in sight.



“the morning pass; the afternoon pass. be we to go through another

night? the sun be within half of an hour of his setting when Parsons,

who be lean his breast on the gunwale, stand upright and point.

his mouth be full of froth, and as he try to speak the foam fly out

of his lip, but no word he speak; it be naught but a kind of

death-rattle in his throat. we all look in the direction he point

to, and see a large sailing-vessel head right down for we. how we

watch she! all of we stand up, never speak, and only move with

the roll and toss of the boat. it take she an hour to approach we, and

then she hove we a line; but her people have to sle we aboard. none of

we could move. nothing but the excitement of see she have allow we

to stand. the moment the line be in the boat and we be alongside, we

all become as helpless as baby.



“the vessel’s name, sir? she be the austrian barque _Grad Karlovak_,

command by so humane a man that I feel fit to cry when I think of he

and his kindness to we poor miserable shipwreck english sailor.

that’s the story, sir, or as much of it as there be any call to relate.

five day and four night in the month of January, in an open boat, most

of the time blow heavily! the tale’s know at Plymouth--it’s know at

Runcorn--it’s know to Mr. Hopkins, the agent of the Shipwrecked

Mariner’s Society at Plymouth. and I’ll tell you somebody else it’s

know to, sir--some one as’ll swear to every word of it; and that’s

I.”









_wait for a ship._





the Shipping Office in Tower Hill be a place where seaman, fireman,

stoker, and other assemble in the hope that captain in want of crew

will come and pick out the good man among they to “sign on,” as it be

call. I be induce to visit it the other day by hear a sailor

complain bitterly of the filthy state of it. “neglect,” say he, “be our

lot; but the condition of that shipping office beat my time. it’s all

dirt and Dutchmen, and if ye want to see something to make you

reflective, just trot down the step and take a turn round the yard the

next time you’re pass that way.” when finally I do trot down the

step I find myself in a kind of courtyard, flank on the one hand by

the shipping office--grimy door, lead into gloomy interior--and on

the other hand by a species of shed, partition into stone room, with

hard and painful seat against the wall, and unwholesome draught of

dampish wind eddy about they. it be a gloomy day--rain have fall,

and pool of muddy water gleam here and there in the yard; the brown

and stoop London sky threaten more wet, and fling a shadow that

make the shipping office and its yard and its condemn-cell-like room

under the shed an unspeakably cheerless, depressing, and miserable

picture. some sixty or seventy man stand or move about in group in

the yard, or be seat in the cell under the shed. I be hardly

prepared to witness so large an assembly, and remain near the step

for a little while survey they. a few of they be decently

attire--one or two respectably and comfortably dress in good clothe

and clean linen; but a large proportion of they be, so far as their

costume go, little well than scarecrow. some be clothe merely in

shirt and trouser, with their naked foot thrust into old shoe or

boot; here and there be a red or blue shirt, or a figure button up

in such a manner as to suggest that under the ragged old coat there be

no shirt at all. “and be this,” think I, “the british sailor of the

nineteenth century?--is this the original of those rubicund feature,

those flow breech, that tarpaulin hat on nine hair, those

well-polish shoe twinkle in the light-hearted measure of the

hornpipe, which be offer by novelist, dramatist, and theatre

lessee as accurate representation of the jolly tar we be so fond of

join in chorus about, and whom we gaze at with such patriotic

enthusiasm as he hitch up his breech, turn his quid, and smite his

timber?” every crowd of human face be full of variety, but no crowd

that ever I look at have the variety submit by the countenance of

these sixty or seventy man who be “wait for a ship.” the negro’s

face--flat, bland, and open-mouthed--be, of course, not want. square

cheek, hollow cheek, high cheek; complexion black, brown, and

yellow; eye of every pattern and shade--from the small, twinkle blue

of the north-country to the filmy and red-web optic of the

gin-soak Cockney--combine, with the different build and shape of the

man, the appearance of their clothe, the various head-covering, to

make up a truly singular scene. I step forward and get among a

little bunch of man, of whom, address myself to one, I ask what

sort of shelter that dirty and wretched shed and those bleak and stony

cell offer in the winter, when the wind blow with an edge and the

sleet and rain fall. no notice have be take of I before, but on my

make this inquiry the eye of the whole group be fix upon I, and

half a dozen voice answer at once. the meaning of the reply be

lose in the confusion, but the noise be like a signal; for I can truly

say that within a few second of my having ask that question every man

in that yard and every man that have be lounge in the cell have

gather about I, so that before I very well know what be happen I

find myself--pretty tightly squeeze--in the centre of a mass of man,

the outer portion of whom press eagerly upon the inner to hear and

see what be go forward. it be like a mutiny on a large scale, and

when I look around at the mass of face, and taste the tobacco-laden

breath of the near people blow hot against my cheek, I feel that

nothing be want to complete the suggestion of revolt but the gleam of

a score of sheath-knife flourish in the air. “give I a little room,

my lad,” say I, work with my elbow; and, having free myself

somewhat, I say, “there seem no lack of man here; captain ought to

find no difficulty in man their ship.”



“they don’t want Englishmen; it’s dutchman they take,” shout two or

three voice.



“here’s a man,” call out some one, point into the left of the

crowd, “who’s be walk this yard for five month.”



“five month, as true as the word I use be English,” bawl a hoarse

voice. “but they willn’t have I because my name’s Johnson. if it be

unk von dunk I’d ha’ be woyage o’er and o’er again in the time

I’ve be kick my heel about starve here.”



“Scoffen von Romp would do as well,” say a man near I. “don’t matter

what the name be so long as it sound Dutch.”



“by Dutch I suppose you mean foreigner of all kind?” say I.



“ay, they’re all Dutchmen!” be the shout.



“but why be it that dutchman be prefer to Englishmen?” I ask.



the hubbub raise by this oblige I to hold up my hand and entreat

silence; but it would not do. every man’s mind be full of the

grievance, and, amid the chorus of reply, I barely succeed in

catch such answer as--“Dutchmen’ll ship for two pound a month!”

“Dutchmen’ll eat anything!” “englishman willn’t put up with the mess

Dutchmen’ll swallow!” “skipper can rope’s-end Dutchmen, but they

durs’n’t serve Englishmen so!” “it’s the dutch crimp as do it!” and

so forth.



it be difficult to hear these cry and watch the sea of surge head

and face around I with unmoved gravity. there be something to touch

the very dull capacity of appreciate the ridiculous in the

astonishing contrast of physiognomy, and in the multifarious

expression which adorn the poor fellow’ countenance; but I be not

sure that the appeal make to my laughter do not owe much of its force

to the sorrowful element in it--to a quality of pathos lie close to

humour. many of these face have a pinched look, that be painfully

expressive of want, if not of positive starvation; and sad indeed, it

seem to I, be the sight of it in man who carry the manner of real

seaman, and who appear to I to be fit for any forecastle afloat, and

for any duty that a sailor be expect to understand.



“I suppose you all come here with certificate of conduct in your

pocket?” say I, when the hubbub have cease.



instantly a crowd of fist be thrust under my nose, fill with

document, and “here’s mine!” and “here’s mine!” “V. G. every one of

’em!” be roar out in twenty or thirty voice. I look at some of

these certificate, and find the letter “V. G.” (very good) endorse

on the back of all that I examine.



“D’ye want to ship, sir?” sing out a fellow whilst I be glance over

these paper. “I’ve get two V. G. certificate in my pocket, and as I’ve

not have anything to eat to-day you shall have ’em both for a couple of

shilling.”



“be certificate often sell in this fashion?” say I, of a

quiet-look man stand alongside of I.



“sell!” he exclaim indignantly; “what’s to hinder ’em? if a man stick

to the name that’s on the certificate, who’s to know? and so ye get man

ship themselves with false character, no more fit for sailor’ work

than if they wos greengrocer.”



“perhaps that’s one reason why skipper and owner prefer Dutchmen to

englishman,” say I. but this raise another storm; they shout that

more rascality go on in that way among Dutchmen than british sailor;

that the reason be not that, but because, as I have hear, Dutchmen

ship for wage no Englishman would look at, and put up with food,

accommodation, and treatment which no Englishman would endure, and

likewise because there be a deal of underhand crimping work go on

between the foreign boarding-house runner and mate and captain, and

so on.



here the emotion of these sixty or seventy man bring they press

so heavily around I, that my anxiety to hear their statement be

swamp in the labour of breathing and the struggle to liberate myself.

I bawl to they to make way, as I want to have a look at the room

under the shed; on which they draw back and let I out, though they

follow at my heel as I pass from one room to another, talk and

argue hotly, call marine blessing down on the head of all

Dutchmen, and wonder what good it be nowadays be bear an

Englishman, when even a Finn, whom, in the olden time, no sailor like

to be shipmate with, be think a well man? the room be

middle-sized, damp, dark, and dirty compartment, and be mean to

serve as waiting-room for the unhappy creature who throng the bleak

and frowsy yard in the hope of be engage by captain. it be like

be in the dungeon in the Tower of London--which, by the way, stand

close at hand--to pass through these death-cold apartment and view the

legend, dictate by hopeless waiting, roughly scrawl in pencil upon

the wall. dirt and soot everywhere!--on the ceiling, on the floor, on

the wall, on the bench, in the very atmosphere that fill the

cheerless haunt. a strip of grating run through the floor, disclose

the outline of a hot-water pipe; but it look, in that grave, the very

corpse of a heating apparatus; and when I ask if ever these stone

room be make warm by that old, mouldy, dirt and soot cover

contrivance, the only answer I get be a loud growl laugh, as if,

exquisite as be the joke, it be likewise very offensive. and this,

think I, as I stand gaze with mingled astonishment and disgust at

the picture of grime, neglect, and dirt, be the great London shipping

office, the medium for the vast and ever-grow port of London for the

transaction of business between the master and crew of ship! who be

these man who come here in the hope of obtain employment by man

the fleet we be never weary of extoll as the source of Great

Britain’s wealth and power, that they should be use in this

manner--furnish for their long, weary, and often hopeless wait with

accommodation fouler, unwholesomer, cold, more soul-depressing than

the bad prison that ever excite the horror and provoke the

denunciation of the philanthropist?



“have this place,” I ask, “be long in this condition?”



“it use to be keep a little more decent,” be the reply; “but it’s be

fall from bad to bad for many a month go. consider the fees[A]

we sailor have to pay, it’s a shame that we should have to put up with

a place which no farmer who value the life of his hog would stow ’em

in. I’ve be day after day down here, from the opening hour till the

closing at four o’clock, for six week, hope to be engage; and I tell

you, sir, that a man need be to be bear a gutter-snipe, use to sleep

all his life under railway arch and the like of they place, not to

feel the effect of such a slum as this upon his spirit, when day after

day go by and he have to keep on wait here for a captain to single

he out. you be see it now in summer, when the air’s warm; think of

it in winter, sir, with the slush a foot thick, and the wind blow

into those waiting-room fit to turn your marrow into ice.”



the Board of Trade be responsible for the conduct and keeping of this

office. have the official of that great department any conception of

the state of the place? be it ever visit by they? do they know

anything more about it than that it be situate somewhere in the

neighbourhood of Tower Hill? nothing more disgraceful be to be find in

London.



by degree the man leave I, to resume their weary trudging up and down

or to draw together in group; on which, find that I should be able

to converse without the risk of suffocation, I go up to a

well-look, decently-dress sailor-man, on whom I have have my eye for

some time previously, and ask how long he have be wait for a ship.



“it will be three week to-morrow,” he reply.



“what be your certificate?”



“I have four in my pocket,” he answer, produce they; and I find

that he have serve in the several capacity of boatswain, sailmaker,

and able seaman aboard sailing-vessel belong to some of the good

firm in London.



“how long have you be at sea?”



“thirty year,” he reply.



“and be it possible,” say I, run my eye over his neat suit of pilot

cloth, his clean blue shirt, and silk handkerchief, and admire his

unmistakably sailorly appearance, and the frank expression in his tanned

face, “that in this age, when one hear so many complaint of the

difficulty of procure good seaman, that a man who have be thirty

year at sea, fill responsible post, and hold honest voucher to his

efficiency and good conduct, cannot get a ship! what countryman be

you?”



“a Scotchman--an Aberdeen man.”



“wouldn’t the last ship you be in take you again?”



“she discharge at Cardiff, and be now for sale. my wife live just out

of the Commercial Road, and that’s why I’m in London,” he answer. “I

have only be home a week when I try to get a ship again, for I’m a

poor man.”



“what be you willing to ship as?”



“as anything; I’m too poor to choose, sir. I’ll go as A. B. if I can get

the berth. but this hang about be eat up all our little saving.”



“why cann’t you get a berth?” say I.



“because the captain willn’t take Englishmen,” he say.



“what be their objection?”



“oh,” say he, “objection be easily make if they’re want. captain

say that english crew desert, that they’re loafer, bad seaman, expect

more wage than they’re worth, and that the good of we be no well

than vagrant, turnpike sailor, who’ll never work so long as there’s a

police-magistrate within hail, and who’ll soger[B] when they’re at sea.

that may be true of some, but it’s false if say of the rest; and,

depend upon it, sir, it don’t account for eighty per cent. of the man

employ in the mercantile marine be ‘dutchman.’ our argument--the

English sailor’s argument--be this: there be a lot of foreign

boarding-house keeper in London. we’ll take one of ’em. he have, say,

twenty Dutchmen in his house, who pay he, each of ’em, sixteen

shilling a week. well, sir, most of these man have no mean to last

their expense much beyond a fortnight; so the boarding-house keeper or

runner say, ‘look here, my lad, you cann’t stay here. I must get you a

ship, and you’ll pay I five shilling apiece out of your allotment

note for do it.’ to this they’re agreeable. the runner then go

down to a ship with his pocket full of his man’s certificate, hand

they, along with a bribe, to the mate or master, who bring ’em to this

office, and the Dutchmen, who’ve be tell by the runner to come to

Tower Hill, be call in to sign article. it pay the runner, who get

five shilling a man for ship they, besides his other expense out

of the allotment note, which he discount at about fifty per cent.; and

it answer the purpose of the skipper, who pocket the bribe, and come

down to find a crew all ready cut and dry for he; but it leave we

englishman out in the cold, kick our heel about, starve many of

we, and stand no shadow of a chance against the underhand roguery

that go on.”



“this be a grave charge to bring against captain,” say I.



“Grave or not,” he reply, “go and ask the opinion of british seaman

all round the coast, and see whether or not this crimping swindle be

understand by they and take as the evil that’s fill english ship

with foreigner.”



“but this kind of rascality be provide against, for the Act say that

any person who receive any remuneration whatever other than the

authorize fee for provide a seaman with employment incur a penalty

of twenty pound.”



“Act or no Act,” he answer contemptuously, “it’s do every day; it’s

do every hour.”



“cann’t you englishman catch one of these ‘dutch’ crimp and make an

example of he?”



“it’s carry on so that it’s hard to prove,” he reply. “Dutchmen

willn’t give evidence against one another; besides, the man sail away and

be lose sight of, and there’s no see how to get at the runner.”



“what be the remedy, then; what be it you want?” I ask.



“we english sailor want this,” he say; “we ask that captain shall

come to the Shipping Office and pick crew out of the crowd; not go and

take certificate from crimp, and come down to find a crew ready

beforehand, to step in as they’re call in. give we a fair chance along

with the Dutchmen. if already eighty per cent. of the crew in English

ship be foreigner, what’s to happen later on when there’ll not be an

Englishman find in the forecastle of a ship that fly the red ensign?

why, the whole breed of sailors’ll die out. talk of Jack be a

skulker, a scaramouch, a no-sailor! what’s the good of abuse he if

you don’t give he a chance? it be say not long ago that owner mean

to ship black crew, so hard do they find it to get Englishmen to act

honestly by their employer. but look at this,” say he, pull a
