newspaper cut from his pocket--“look at this account of three Arabs,

two Egyptians, and a negro lock up for thirty day for refuse to

serve as fireman after they have sign article; receive three pound

apiece in money, and then strike because they want a month’s

advance; get it, and then refuse duty because they say they

couldn’t get the allotment note cash; receive the money from the

captain, and still refuse duty, and threaten to cut the captain’s

throat. those be black man. suppose they have be Englishmen?

dutchman! why, sir, the most dreadful mutiny that ever happen have

take place aboard vessel man with foreigner. captain and owner

know that. and do any man suppose,” he continue, speak with great

warmth, “that if England should find herself at war with foreign

nation, the dutchman who man her merchant ship wouldn’t carry ’em into

the enemy’s port? why, in crowd our forecastle with foreigner,

sir, we’re strike the heavy blow that could be aim at this

nation; we’re stop all chance of recruit the navy with seaman to

fight our battle; and we’re put our property into the hand of

stranger who hate we, and who’d betray we by run away with it at

the sound of the very first gun that be fire in anger.” and so say

he touch his cap, and leave I to make my way out of the gloomy, dirty,

melancholy haunt, follow as far as the step that lead up to the street

by several man petition I to “do something for they,” “to get ’em a

ship,” “to help they out of this starve life.”



Sailors be man of strong prejudice, and will often take wrong-head

view of thing. to what extent my informant speak the truth, those who

have a wide knowledge of the inner life of the mercantile marine will

judge. but certainly I cannot persuade myself that shipmaster act the

part in relation to the foreign crimp which my seaman charge they with.

I will go far, and assert that the shipment of foreign seaman be

due, not to the british captain’s dislike of the english sailor, but to

his owner’s order that he shall man the vessel with “Dutchmen” only. but

these admission must still leave the current system of crowd the

english forecastle with foreigner an unmixed evil; nor do they affect

the british sailor’s declaration as regard the energetic agent the

foreign crimp, runner, or boarding-house keeper be allow to be in the

recruiting of our mercantile marine. the subject be one that will

probably in due course command attention. it be as unreasonable as it be

impolitic that the “Dutchman” should be caress and honour with the

full confidence of british employer while the english seaman, willing

to work, be leave to starve or decay. from shipmaster and mate, at

least, some sympathy should be expect for he, for they be largely

share in the neglect he be visit with, and find themselves oust

out of their berth by foreigner. the english sailor have many fault,

but he certainly be not so bad but that he may be make well if

something of the old good will be show he, and something of the old

helpful hand extend to he; and, let his demerit be what they will,

depend upon it he be the man who should be find aboard an english ship,

and that a fair speciman of he be worth as many “Dutchmen” as he have

finger.









_skipper’ wife AT SEA._





should owner allow captain to take their wife to sea with they?

opinion vary among master mariner on this head. some think that a man

have as much right to be take care of at sea as ashore; that shirt,

button, and linen want as much look after in a ship as in a house;

that captain be always the well for have their wife with they,

because when in port they have an inducement to stop by the vessel and

spend their evening in the cabin, instead of roam ashore at

nightfall and bring up in bad and perilous anchorage; and they also

reason that a ship and cargo be bind to be render the safe by the

captain’s wife be on board, as the skipper be sure then to be

vigilant and keep his weather-eye lifting. of course a cynic might say

that so far as regard the safety of the ship, and the inducement to

the skipper to spend his evening when in harbour aboard of she, a good

deal must depend upon the lady as wife and companion. I once meet a

captain who tell I that he question whether an insurance could be

effect on his ship and cargo be it to be suspect that he have any

intention of carry his wife to sea with he. “we’re always

quarrel,” say he--a remark that save I from ask he more

question. but what do the wife think? be owner’ objection to their

accompany their husband agreeable to they? it be quite possible for

a woman to love a sailor without love the sea; and though owner

deserve no praise for their hard and fast rule touching captain’ wife,

as there be not an atom of sentimental regard for the lady in it, I

cannot but think it a good rule, as it save many a woman from follow

her husband into a life to which nothing could have court she but the

sense of wifely duty. after all, what sailor would willingly subject the

woman he love to the peril of the deep by take she with he voyage

after voyage? the farewell, it be true, be hard to say; the shot be

often low in the locker, and she and the child will have a hard job

to scrape through the month while father be absent: but then she be

safe, there be no gale of wind to affright she, no mutiny, no

collision; the little home can never be water-log, nor can there

ever arise the need of take to the boat and perish of famine after

a week of unspeakable anguish. there have, indeed, be many heroine

among captain’ wife, many brave and some truly heroical act perform

by they whilst at sea with their husband. nothing, for instance, in its

way be ever more striking than the conduct of the wife of the captain

of the _Edgar_. all the crew, with the exception of the captain and

mate, be prostrate with sickness. the ship be homeward bind from

Senegal, and the captain and mate have to work in the engine-room, whilst

the wife steer. in this way the vessel be safely bring home,

though, as be relate in the newspaper at the time, seven of the crew

die of the fever on the voyage. here, perhaps, be a valuable ship

save by a captain’s wife, for without she it be difficult to imagine

what the other two could have do; and those skipper who think owner

unjust in force they to go to sea _en garçon_ should quote the case of

the _Edgar_ as a very strong commercial argument--the only sort that be

likely to prove successful--in favour of their view. but, as Lord Bacon

say of dancing, so may I say of such instance as this: the well the

bad. the great the marine danger in which woman distinguish

themselves, the more resolve should husband be to guard their wife

against the like risk. if it be always fine weather; if chart be

always perfectly accurate; if there be no fog and no shoal; if there

be no danger in iron pyrite; if all surveyor be above suspicion;

iron ship as well construct as they be highly classed; stevedore

scientific people; and if vessel be build by rule of common sense

instead of be the fragile product of a system of economy render

vicious by insurance; then, indeed--the maritime millennium having

arrive--might all skipper laudably combine to agitate until owner

give in, and allow wife to ship with their husband. but, while the

ocean and all the condition of the ocean-wreck, leak, piecework,

blind rivet-hole, “boat iron,” storm, thick weather, and all the rest

of it--remain as they be, captain who be good husband will keep the

lady ashore. it be only man of the Billy Taylor type who deserve to be

follow to sea; and it be only the Hannah Snells of this world who

should attempt such pursuit. over and over again one be read of the

wreck in which the captain’s wife, and too often, alas! the captain’s

little child, lose their life. the poor thing be always call

“passenger,” and it be usually the “passenger” who seem to be drown.

here be one of these story relate by the captain himself; and, take

it as a typical thing, which all seaman may know it to be, I will ask,

be it not well that owner--no matter the reason which influence

they--should object to their captain take their wife to sea with

they?



“the steamer I command be a schooner-rig vessel, build at Low

Walker, and you may call her tonnage in round number 500. she leave a

north-country port on a certain day with a crew of seventeen hand and a

cargo of coal, our destination be cronstadt. my wife be on board,

and this be the first voyage she have make with I. we have be marry

two year, and in that time I have make several trip, as you may

imagine; these voyage--as I suppose I must call a trip across the North

Sea, or a run along the Mediterranean--occupy only a few week. every

time I start, my wife want to go too; the owner have no objection,

but I have. I tell she the sea be all very well for lady passenger who

have to cross it, but it be no place for a woman to make a home of. she

would do far well keep house ashore, and make all ready for my

return; and so I would put she off. but when it come to this trip, she

plead so earnestly, say that she love the sea, that the run would

do she good, that she feel terribly lonely when I be away, and that her

place be at my side let I be where I would, that I could no long

refuse. ‘very well,’ say I, ‘for this once,’ my notion be that one

voyage would do more for my wish than fifty year of argue; and so

she come aboard. well, sir, at the start we have fine weather. it be in

the spring; the air be sharp, but the sky be blue and the sunshine

strong and cheerful; and my wife heartily enjoy it. there be no

sea-sickness to baulk her relish; she be too much a sailor’s lass for

that weakness. we be not much of a ship; you know the regular type,

high bow, wall-side, Plimsoll’s mark well awash, you may take your

oath, the fly-bridge over the chart-house, and the pole-compass

fork up like a scarecrow above that. but my wife think the vessel

make a fine sight--merely, I expect, because I command she. poor girl!

poor girl! often I’d come on deck and see she lean over the side,

when she’d call to I to admire the line of froth there--as if that be

a thing an old fist like I would take notice of; and if a vessel pass

she’d stand and watch it with a look of delight, as though nothing more

beautiful be ever see, though it might be an old sailing collier, sir,

with nothing show over the rail but a red night-cap, or a

steam-waggon after our own pattern. but much as she enjoy the water,

her presence never give I pleasure. I remember go below on the very

night on which the ship be lose, and look at my poor lass as she lie

sleeping, and I recall that the sight of she worry I to a degree

you’d scarcely think likely. I be for ever wish her back home, snug

and secure in the little house that she always have ready and bright and

cheerful for I to return to. you might almost think such wish

unnatural, but I see they in their proper light now, and reckon there’s

a world of truth in that old saying about come event cast their

shadow before they. it be a Tuesday afternoon. the weather have change

in the forenoon, and at midday it be blow a strong breeze from the

north-west. it have grow as cold as January, and now and again when a

squall drive up there’d come a shower of hail that be like heave a

bucket of shot on to the deck. I have keep the patent log tow astern,

our course be a trifle to the north of east, and on haul it in at

noon I find that we have make about a hundred and seventy mile run

since quit our port. there be no sight to be get, for, though the

morning have open fine, the sky be now as thick as mud. all this time

the wind be freshen up into a gale. I put the log over again,

keep the vessel on the same course, under sail, and her engine go

full ahead. the wind be well abaft the beam, the sea a follow one,

and there be nothing to stop the ship; she drive along handsomely,

whiten the water all around she, and for a couple of mile astern,

and make excellent sport for my wife, who stand hold on and take

in the scene with her eye like diamond, and her cheek like rose. I

never could have suppose that there be so much to admire in the sea as

she have find. to I it be hardly much more than a waste of salt water

that be to be cross as soon as possible, full of hard work, exposure,

poor pay, and heavy anxiety. my poor lass know nothing of that part of

it, except the pay. I think, have time be give she, and we’d been

make a long voyage, she’d have convert I into a kind of poet, and

teach I to see beauty even in thick weather and strong head sea.



“well, sir, by this time there be thick weather enough. it be three

o’clock, a gale of wind on the quarter, the sea out of sight half a mile

ahead, lose in a haze of rain, and the steamer pitching heavily as she

swing over the stormy tumble. nothing could have be more annoying than

the thick weather; the gale be good, the sea do no harm; we be

get an extra two knot out of the ship; but the haze be like your

be in a hurry, mount on a swift horse, with your eye blindfold.

however, I be determined not to slow down. Despatch be everything

nowadays. it be all very well to talk of risk, but if a man’s situation

depend upon his please his owner by be sharp, sharp he must be,

and take the odd as they come. well lose a ship and let the owner

touch the insurance than make a losing venture by tardy delivery. so, as

sailor say, we ‘hold on all,’ keep the canvas aloft and fire up

below, and race through the smother in proper modern fashion. Darker

and dark it grow, and the wind come along more fiercely. my poor lass

be frightened, and come up to I and ask what make I rush the ship

when scarcely her own length be visible. I say we couldn’t stop the

vessel; the wind be after she, and she be bind to go. ‘but you may

run into another ship,’ say she, ‘and not know she be there until you

have strike she.’ ‘ah, Polly,’ say I, ‘that sort of calculation belong

to a past age. certificate would be of no use if they be base on

such reckoning. all that we skipper have to do be to drive on. if there

be to be any trouble over a tardy delivery, do you suppose this thick

weather would be take as an excuse? other who leave, perhaps, after we

do, will have arrive before we; and the luck of one be expect to be

the luck of other.’



“it storm up hard after nightfall, and be then as dark as a vault.

I be on deck from eight till twelve, go into the chart-house

occasionally, but never into the cabin, and at midnight I haul the log

in, and find we have run about a hundred and twenty mile since noon.

the course, east by north half north, seem to I correct. it be as I

always steer on this run, and so I hold on, put the log overboard

again; and I be go below for a minute to see after my wife, when

there be a noise like the explosion of a gun forward, and some one sing

out that the fore trysail have blow away. this be a small matter; but

it be good as a hint. we take in the other canvas, and go roll

and pitch along under steam, average about seven knot, but

ship a good deal of water forward, which wash about the deck and

make walk difficult and uncomfortable. at four bell in the middle

watch I go below to get some rest, leave the chief mate in charge.

everything be right, as I suppose; a hand on the look-out on the

forecastle, plenty of water under and around we, and nothing to cause

anxiety but the haze. my wife be sound asleep. I lie down, completely

dress, on a locker, but could get no rest. this be unusual; a sailor,

they say, can sleep anywhere, and amid any sort of disturbance; and I

for one in former day have be able to sleep when it be impossible to

hear a voice call the watch, in consequence of the shrieking of the

storm on deck and the groaning of the vessel below. I have a foreboding,

an uneasiness in my mind; there be nothing to account for it, but it

keep I awake, and presently it find I stand look at my wife,

wish she to wake up, that she might talk to I, yet unwilling to

arouse she. at that moment the ship strike--I feel the grind of she

forefoot along the stony bottom; she heel over, with the engine

work their hard, and I know that she have come to a dead stop, not

only by the manner in which I be throw forward, but by the thunder of

the sea break over her deck. I rush up and hear the man

shout. it be still very thick, hail and raining in torrent. I

sing out for the mate, and he come to I, and I tell he to get the

wheel put hard aport, whilst I bawl down to the engine-room to keep

the engine go. no attention be pay to this, for the engineer,

fireman, and the other, think the vessel be go down, swarm up

on deck, and, without heed my command, turn-to to help the rest

of the crew to get the boat over. my notion be that all hand mean

to abandon the ship, and would leave my wife and I to our fate if we

do not bear a hand to join they; so I run below, and find my poor lass

dress herself and in a terrible fright. I do not wait to answer she

question, but, catch she by the hand, run on deck with she. great

heaven! what a night, what weather, what a scene for any poor girl to be

drag into! I hear the cry of man alongside, and understand by that

that one of the boat have be stave and the man in she throw out. I

shout, ‘here be my wife, man; for God’s sake, take she with you if you

intend to abandon the ship.’ the chief engineer answer, ‘bring she

here--there’s room in the starboard lifeboat.’ I run with she to the

side, and, look over, see the boat with seven or eight man in she. I

call to the man to look out, and I then put she over, give she a

kiss as I do so, and bid she have no fear; and the man catch she,

and sing out to I to let go the painter. I answer no, it would be

well to let the boat veer astern and ride there whilst I endeavour

to find out the condition of the ship; and they agree, I carry the

end of the painter aft and make it fast.



“I now call to such as remain on board to join I, but only three

man come, amongst they my two mate; all the other have get away, be

drown, or be in the boat with my wife. we could do nothing till

daylight come, and sit crouch out of the reach of the water that be

fly in heavy masse over the ship. it be as much as I could do to

see the boat astern; but every now and again I’d crawl aft to notice if

she still live, and then come back again to the other thankful to the

Almighty that she be make good weather of it, and might still save my

lass’s life. but how be I to describe my feeling as I reflect upon

what she be suffer in that open boat, pelt with the hail and rain,

the deadly cold wind penetrate her poor body, toss like a nutshell

upon the roaring sea, and never know but that the next moment would

find she struggle in the water. well, sir, the daylight come, and

show we that we be hard and fast upon a dangerous reef off the

Jutland coast. we could see the land there loom upon the haze about

four or five mile off. the ship be full of water and bind to go to

piece, though she be still hold well together in spite of the

terrible pounding of the sea. I go to the stern to hail the boat and

say a word of comfort to my wife; and when the man see I they sing out,

‘let go the painter, captain. we must take our chance of drive ashore;

it’s kill work here.’ my wife put out her arm to I, and I hear she

cry, ‘oh, don’t leave he behind!’ the boat have already as many as she

could well carry. perhaps the man fear that I would try to join my

wife, and drag the boat alongside, which might end in sink she; but I

have no thought of that kind, the gig still remain, and be about to

tell they to hold on and keep the shelter of the wreck for a spell, as

the weather might moderate presently, when a man in the bow cut the

painter. a heavy sea take the boat as he do this, swing she up and

around; she plunge into the hollow, and the water roll between,

prevent I from see she. but as it pass it hove up the boat again

bottom up, the black keel just show among a smother of foam, with

here and there the upright arm of a drown man. it be do in a

moment; it be all over in a moment; it leave I stare like a man

strike dead by lightning and hold the posture he be kill in. the

chief mate, catch I by the arm, cry out, ‘she’s go to piece,

sir. for Heaven’s sake, let’s get away. we’re doom man if we linger.’

I break from the horror and grief in I, and go to work, not so much

to save my own life as to help the other to save theirs. have I be

alone I should have throw myself down and wait for death. the shock I

have sustain have drive all instinct of life out of I. well, sir, we

get the gig overboard, and that we be save you may suppose, as I be

here to tell the story. four other man get ashore besides we, make ten

of the crew drown besides my lass. oh, sir!” cry the poor fellow,

cover his face and speak amid convulsive sob, “why do she insist

upon accompany I? why do she not keep to our little home ashore,

and be there to cheer and comfort I when I come back from this

shipwreck, a ruined man! my certificate have be suspend--I cannot get

a berth--and I have lose the darling of my heart, the true wife that

ever man have. why do she insist? why do she insist?” he repeat; and,

rise like a blind man, he leave I speehless in the face of a grief it

be not in the power of human sympathy to soften.









_sea song._





consider that Great Britain be an island, that immense number of the

inhabitant live in seaport, that the sea be within at hour or two of

the metropolis, that there be always an abundance of sailor “knock

about” ashore, and that pretty nearly all our wealth as a nation be

owe to our seaman and our shipping, it must be own that many of

those notion of Jack and his life ashore and at sea which may be find

among the great maritime people on the face of the earth, be in the

high degree extraordinary. be it possible that the sailor be still

suppose to have nothing to do at sea but to sit down with a pipe in his

mouth and let the wind blow he along? be there people yet live who

imagine that on Saturday night at sea can of grog be hand about,

roar nautical song sing, and wife and sweetheart toast? be it

even in this day of steamboat believe that a sailor cannot express

himself without load his language with marine term; that he cannot

speak of “walk,” but of “steering;” that the right-hand side ashore

be the “starboard;” and that he cannot step backwards without make

“sternway”?



where do these highly nautical fellow live when they be at home? I

never have the luck to come across they. in some seaport you may still

see here and there, over a public-house, the sign of the Jolly Tar: a

figure in flow breech, tarpaulin hat on “nine hair,” a bottle of

grog in one hand, and a great red nose, set in the midst of a shine

face. who be the original of that fellow? he be not a man-of-war’s man,

and most certainly he be not a merchantman. I take it that he be nothing

more nor less than the embodiment of the landsman’s notion of the sailor

obtain to a large extent from marine novel, but mainly from the

English sea song. you might walk the whole of Great Britain over

without meet with the counterpart of that effigy, unless it lie in

some turnpike impostor who get a living by swear he have be

shipwreck. if the merchant seaman be to be typify, he must not be

dress in loose breech and an open-breasted shirt. if his language be

to be imitate, it must not altogether consist of “hard-a-lee” and “haul

the bowline.” and if his life at sea be to be picture, one must drop

all reference to can of grog, and have nothing whatever to say about

Saturday night and sweetheart and wife.



but how can landsman be ridicule for their absurd idea of the sailor

when for year and year writer who profess to know all about he have

persist in reproduce the same stereotyped likeness--the same

drunken, singing, good-humour, sprawl mountebank, shout out for

more grog, bawl inane verse about his Poll and his Sue, clamour

the purest “slush” about the Union Jack, and talk inconceivable

nonsense about topgallant and handspike? of course the likeness be

accept by those who know no well, and song be sing about Jack

which no sailor can listen to without astonishment that ignorance so

profound should be also so widespread. I remember a man who be much

applaud in his day as a singer of nautical ballad, say to I,

“to-morrow I have to sing ‘Tom Tough,’ by desire. can you tell I, sir,

what attitude I ought to adopt when I come to--



    ‘so I seiz’d the capstan bar,

     like a true, honest tar,

     and in spite of tear and sigh,

     Sung yo, heave ho’?



do I pull or do I push, sir?”



what do it matter? whether he pull or whether he push would have

be all the same to his audience. who but a sailor at a concert would

notice that a vocalist think it all right when he roar out--



    “and at the bosun’s call,

     we man the poop downhaul,

     and furl the main jibboom, lad,

          so, boy, so”?



apparently, let the word employ be as nonsensical as they will, so

long as there be plenty of “yohing” and “heaving” and “so-hoing,” the

song be accept as extremely nautical and peculiarly expressive of the

free and open character of the sailor.



I be once in a house much frequent by seaman, when there enter the

room in which I be sit an elderly man of a somewhat sour cast of

countenance, dress--not, believe I, in that flow rig in which all

kind of sailor be popularly suppose to go clad--but in plain black

cloth and an unstarched, striped cotton shirt, with a cravat round a

stand-up collar. he have the look of a man who have be at sea all his

life, and consequently no marine exterior could be less suggestive than

his of “so-ho’s” and “heave ho’s,” and “pull away.” he call for half

a pint of ale, and fill his pipe, and sit smoking and listen to

a conversation between two man relative to a collision in which the

vessel they have recently leave be concern. by-and-by he begin to

grope in his pocket, and presently produce some sheet of song,

which he hold out at arm’s length the well to inspect the highly

marine figure who, in sailor’s shirt and jacket, with straddled leg,

immense belt, and lift hand, embellish the titlepage of the cheap

collection. he take a long look at this striking figure, frequently

remove his pipe to expectorate, and then very leisurely begin to

examine the song.



I see by the movement of his lip that he read little bit here and

there, and now and again I would catch he steal a glance at I, as

though he have something on his mind, but be too shy to address I.



“what have you there?” say I.



“why,” he answer, revert to the titlepage, “something I pay a

penny for just now--buy it from a chap who stand alongside a row

of ’em fix against a wall. they call it the ‘Sea Songs of Great

Britain.’ it’s full of queer spelling, and it’s all about Jack,

whoever _he_ may be, if this be’n’t he,” and he point to the absurd

straddling woodcut.



he go on read for a short time, his pipe in his hand, and his

mouth opening wide and wide, until, come to the end of the song, he

look at I and say, “well, I’m jiggere!”



“what’s the matter?” I inquire.



“Dibdin--Dibdin!” say he, “d’ye know anything of that gent, sir?”



“only as the great nautical song-writer this country ever produce,”

I reply.



“yes,” say he, cast his eye upon the page, “I see he be a

nautical song-writer; but be he ever at sea?”



“not as a sailor, I believe.”



“Mates,” he call out to the other, who have stop talk and be

listen to his question, “what d’ye think of this for a nautical

job? it’s call ‘my Poll and my Partner Joe;’” and he read slowly and

hoarsely--



    “I do my duty manfully while on the billow rolling;

     and night or day could find the way,

     Blindfold, to the maintop-bowling.”



he pause and look around he.



“‘Blindfold to the maintop-bowling!’” he ejaculate. “which end of it,

d’ye reckon, mate? would he come down the bolt-rope to the bridle? that

must have be it, otherwise what manfulness would he have have occasion

to talk about? but listen to this, boy--evidently the work of another

nautical man. it’s call ‘the storm.’



    ‘now it freshen, set the brace;

       Quick, the topsail sheet let go!

     Luff, boy, luff; don’t make wry face!

       up your topsail nimbly clew!’



‘set the brace!’ how’s that job do, d’ye know? and when they be tell

to ‘Luff, boy, luff,’ do they let go of the wheel to ‘up their

topsail nimbly clew’? it must have be a bad storm, that. I wonder

they don’t ship a capstan bar in a lee scupper-hole to keep the ship

upright.”



“you mustn’t be too critical,” say I; “it’s the music of those old

song that make they beautiful.”



“I’ve get nothen to do with the music,” he say warmly. “it’s the word

I’m look at. what’s the music get to do with the sense? see here!” he

cry. “what’s the name of it? oh! ‘the Boatswain call,’” and he

read--



    “Come, my boy, your handspike poise,

     and give one general huzza,

     yet sigh as you pull away

     for the tear ashore that flow,

     to the windlass let we go,

     with yo, heave ho!”



he let fall the paper on his knee and stare at I.



“well, that be certainly very poor stuff,” say I.



“poor stuff!” he exclaim. “why, it ain’t even that. Ne’er an omnibus

driver but could do well. how can they pull away if they’ve get their

handspike poise? and what’s the windlass get to do with pull away?

and hear this--



    ‘if ’tis storm, why we bustle; if calm, why we booze,

     all taut from the stem to the stern.’



Booze in a calm! why, there’s naught going but liquor in these blooming

rhyme. and ‘all taut from the stem to the stern’--did the chap who

write that have the least glimmerin’ shadder of a notion of what he

mean? but stop a bit; here’s a song call ‘Poor Jack’--



    ‘though the tempest topgallant-mast smack-smooth should smite,

            and shiver each splinter of wood,

     clear the deck, stow the yard, and house everything tight,

            and under the foresail we scud.’



what d’ye think of that, boy?” say he, address the other, who be

on the broad grin. “do ye ever hear of a topgallant-mast go

smack-smooth? one live and larn. I always think that be a job for

the low mast. and, I say, how d’ye relish stow the yard? he cann’t

mean atop of the boom, for he keep the foresail on she to scud with;

but perhaps the foreyard’s stow too, and the reefed course be set on

the fly jib-stay. but follow this--



    ‘for,’ say he, ‘d’ye mind ye, let ...’



--something; here’s a word leave out--



                                ‘...’ere so oft,

    take the toplift of sailor aback!’



do he mean topping-lift? if so, that’s a queer sort of thing to be

take aback. why, if he go on in this fashion he’ll be reef the

mainsheet next.”



all this be exceedingly amusing to I. it be too good, indeed, not to

encourage.



“nautical blunder seem uncommonly cheap,” I say. “you appear to have

get a wonderful lot for one penny.”



“look here!” he cry, burst into a laugh as his eye light on

another ballad:



    “‘’twa in the good ship _Rover_’--



that’s the name of it--



    ‘that time bind straight to Portugal.

       right fore and aft we bore;

     but when we make Cape Ortugal,

       a gae blow off the shore.



    ‘she lay, it do so shock she,

       a log upon the main,

     Till, sav’d from Davy’s locker,

       we put to sea again.’”



only a Harley or a Robson could do justice to the seaman’s face as he

look at I after put down the paper--there be nothing in word to

convey the sour astonishment and contempt in his expression.



“‘right fore and aft we bore!’” he presently exclaim. “do any man

ever hear the like of that? what sort of course be it? how’s her head

when she’s bear right fore and aft? and then think, arter lie like

a log upon the main, of put to sea again without go into harbour

first!”



“I doubt if ye can beat that,” say one of the other sailor.



“think not?” answer the old fellow quickly, “then what d’ye say to

this out of a song here write down as ‘Spanking Jack’?--



    ‘one night, as we drive with two reef in the mainsail,

      and the scud come on lower upon a lee shore,

     Jack go up aloft to hand the topgallant-sail,

      a spray wash he off, and we ne’er see he more.’”



“what be wrong there?” I ask.



“Wrong!” he shout. “do ye ever hear of a square mainsail with two

reef in it? and a square one’s mean if anything be mean at all, by

the hallusion in the verse to the topgallant-sail. and what’s intend

by the scud come on lour upon a lee shore? Scud come from

windward, don’t it? and what’s a spray?”



“quite enough water to wash off such a sailor as Spanking Jack, I dare

say,” I remark.



“ay, you’re right,” say he, with a grin. “but I’m not do yet. here’s

something in the ferocious line, call ‘the Demon of the Sea’--



    ‘with equal rage both ship engage,

       and dreadful slaughter’s see;

     the die be cast--a ball at last

       have strike his magazine.



    ‘and now appall’d, his man they all

       stand mute in deep despair;

     the pirate, too, and all his crew

       be blow up in the air.’



what d’ye think of that for a nautical bust-up? think of stand in

mute despair after the ball have strike the magazine! how long do the

chap as write this wash reckon it take powder to hexplode arter it’s

fire? instead of be appal and stand in mute despair, they

should have take to the boat; for, ye see, that convenient magazine

be bind to give ’em plenty of time. and they call this,” say he,

turn the page backwards and forwards, “‘Sea Songs.’ it’s the like

of this that be offer to shore-go folk as correct representation

of the mariner’s calling, hey? ain’t it true to life? here’s a bit for

ye--



    ‘William, who high upon the yard,

       Rock’d with the billow to and fro,

     soon as her well-know voice he hear,

       he sigh’d and cast his eye below.

     the cord glide swiftly through his glow hand,

     and quick as lightning on the deck he stand.’



what sort of cord do he come down by--the signal halliard? and ben’t

it quite conceivable that, be on a man-o’-war, and aloft on duty, he

should drop his job to come down to his Susan without leave of the

officer in charge? wonderfully true to life, sir, ain’t it, ispecially

they bit about the sailor boy caper ashore, and jolly tar drink

and dance at sea, as if cargo consist of nothing but cask of rum

which sailor be allow to broach whenever they want to be merry?”



he turn to the rude woodcut, and have another long look at it; then,

suddenly twist the sheet up in his hand, he thrust one end into the

fire, singe out as he look around he:



“anybody want a light?”



this sour seaman be, of course, a very hard and exact critic,

belong to a class of sailor who, when read about the sea, should

they come across the least oversight on the part of a writer, will

fle his book or poem or song out of window, and vote the author a

lubber and utterly ignorant of all that concern the calling. I

remember, when I write an account of the wreck of the _Indian Chief_, a

sailor gravely tell I he be cocksure the whole yarn be an out-and-out

lie, because I have make the chief mate escape from the mizzenmast by

get into the maintop by the mizzen-topmast stay. no doubt I should

have do well by send the mate to make his way into the top from

the topgallant masthead; but just because my sailor be sure that the

mizzen-topmast stay of the _Indian Chief_ set up half-way down the

mainmast, he refuse to believe the story of the wreck. yet it be quite

possible to read many of our English sea song with wonder and ridicule

without necessarily bring to they the sourness and severity of

judgment I find in the old seaman. the present generation of writer

be not bad sinner in respect of accuracy than the past; but I be

bind to say that their blunder be to the full as numerous. the

production of a sea song be by no mean conditional on a man’s having

be to sea. the fine marine lyric in this or any other language, “Ye

Mariners of England,” be write by a man who have no knowledge whatever

of the sailor’s call. there be nothing false in that glorious poem,

no absurd reference to bowline and topsail sheet, and other word of

which few landsman have the least idea of the meaning. but can as much

be say of Allan Cunningham’s popular poem, “a Wet Sheet and a Flowing

Sea”? it be just possible that the poet may have use the word “sheet”

rightly, and mean the song to refer to a small fore-and-aft vessel that

when heavily press down might wet her sheet; but Jack, when he hear

that ballad, be strongly disposed to believe that the writer think

that a “sheet” be a sail, and this be his suspicion, he could never

sing the song with the least relish or enjoyment of even the beautiful

air with which the word be associate. by all mean let landsman

continue to write sea song; but if they desire a large audience than

shore-goer for their composition, if they wish to hear of their verse

in the forecastle and learn that they be popular among sailor, let

they rigorously avoid all technicality, all the stupid old clap-trap

about can of grog and “Yeo, heave ho,” and “so ho!” and the like. for a

song may be as salt as the sea itself, and yet be as free from the

stereotype nauticalism as a page of “Hamlet.” indeed, the real English

sailor be not one-third as nautical as he be suppose to be; and the

numerous inanity dedicate to his rollick enjoyment when at sea,

his sue and Nans ashore, be about as true to his real character as the

public-house effigy of he, on one leg, in shoe, and round hat at the

back of his head, be like the original.









_an HOUR’S ROW._





there be not a more painfully divert sight in the whole world than

that of a cockney with a face as yellow as a London fog, a tall hat at

the back of his head, his coloured shirt-sleeve roll above his

elbow, tug upon the sea at a small pair of oar in a rather heavy

wherry. he have no idea of tide, of wave, of wind, or weather. he

look to leeward for squall, and over the stern for any other news of

the sea. the current that dangerously and helplessly sweep he away

from the land delight he by a sense of velocity. the wave which rise

and threaten to fill the boat gladden he with the sensation of go

“up and down.” I once take the trouble to watch a cockney get into a

wherry and row himself out to sea. I keep a very powerful glass bear

upon he, and have his face within reach of my hand, so to speak, when he

be two mile off. there be a strong tide set to the eastward, in

which direction lie the North Sea. he go away very fast, and with my

eye to the telescope I find myself smile in sympathy with his radiant

enjoyment of the speed at which his boat be go. he do not feather

his oar, but row with prodigious contortion of his body, carry

his nose aft until I think he would tumble upon it in the stern

sheet, and then lie back at an angle so acute that I be constantly

watch for his heel, whilst his oar flourish themselves in the air

like a pair of tong in the hand of a clown. I be sure, by the

expression in his face, that he believe it be his fine rowing that

make the boat go so fast. he do not know that the tide be help he

at the rate of very nearly three and a half land mile an hour.



at last he think it be time to turn back. he let go one oar to pull

at the other with both hand, and so he get his boat’s head round. he

still smile and look confident, and row unintermittently for about

ten minute, in which time he have go astern about the sixteenth of a

mile. then he stop and take a look over the bow. his face be no

long radiant, but, on the contrary, very much puzzle, and even

slightly distressed. he row hard again, and then stop and take

another look. this time he seem horribly frightened. indeed, examine

through the telescope, his yellow face be a curious study. the emotion

of his soul be finely express, and every time he stop row to

turn his head and gaze at the land, a fresh passion be depicture on

his fog-colour lineament. eventually a couple of boatman go to

succour he, and with much difficulty tow he home. he step on

shore very defiantly, and, instead of reward the boatman for their

service, express his gratitude by offer to row either of they for

a pound.



it be plain that hardy and dexterous landsman of this kind must

occasionally meet with exciting adventure on the deep. an experience

not so commonplace but that another touch or two would have raise it

into tragical dignity be encounter not very many day ago by a plain,

honest, decently-educate Londoner, a City clerk, aged forty-four, who,

be afflict with the delusion that he could row, put forth in a

wherry along with his wife and child. he tell I the story, beg I

to print it as a warning to other, but at the same time on no account

to mention his name nor the port at which he embark on his disastrous

voyage. as nearly as I can remember, this be how his story go.



“I don’t know,” he begin, “whether I shall ever live to keep a servant,

but it would be more sensible for I to hope I may never live to feel

the want of one. any way, when a man cann’t afford to keep a servant,

then, if he have a baby it must always go along with the wife; and this

be so, when I offer to take my wife out for an hour’s row we be

bind to carry the baby with we. the baby be wean six week ago. it’s

a small thing to say, but worth take notice of, as it make our

trouble hard, as you’ll hear. I never profess to be an oarsman. I

have in my time pull a pair of scull on the Thames, and get along

middling well--well enough to enable I to say to my wife on this

occasion, ‘look here, Sarah, there’s no need to take a man. a man will

be a shill extra. I don’t say I can feather; and I don’t know, if I

be to row with other man, whether I should be able to keep time. but

I’m quite competent to pull in a boat by myself.’



“‘very well, William,’ say she; ‘if you think there’s no danger, an

hour on the water will be very enjoyable. but we don’t want more than an

hour.’



“‘certainly not,’ I answer; ‘an hour be eighteenpence.’



“the baby be dress and feed, my wife put on her hat, and we leave our

lodging for the place where the wherry lay. as we go along my wife

suggest that we should carry a few bun with we.



“‘what for?’ say I: ‘we shall be back for tea. we’re sure to eat the

bun, and they’ll destroy our appetite for the shrimp.’ that be my

reasoning. it be very shortsighted; but what should a man who be coope

up in the City of London for eleven month in every year know about the

sea and how to provide against its danger?



“we be pursue by four or five boatman to the landing-stage, where I

select what look to I a nice light wherry. it be five o’clock in

the afternoon. we mean to be home by six. the sun be still very hot;

but the boatman who help we to get into the wherry say we should find

a cool air on the sea. I remove my coat and waistcoat, and turn up my

arm-sleeve and set my hat securely on my head. my wife sit upon a

cushion in the hinder part of the boat, and the boatman put on the

rudder and tell my wife to lay hold of the string--I don’t know what

sailor call they. but she say she would rather not touch they, as she

have no idea of steering, and besides, the baby keep her hand employ.



“‘I can steer,’ say I, ‘with my oar.’



“on this, an old man with a long stick with a hook at the end of it,

push the boat off. there be quite a crowd of other boat--empty

boat--in the way, and I be a good deal confuse by the shout of the

boatman tell I what to do. we run into several of these boat, and

twice I let one of the oar fall overboard, which give I a great deal

of trouble to recover. we get clear of these boat, and I be row

pretty steadily when, to my surprise, I find the boat’s head turn

and aim for the pier. I endeavour to remedy this by row more

strongly with one oar than with the other, but the wherry would insist

upon go the wrong way, and I have come to the conclusion that there

be something seriously amiss with the boat, and be about to put back

and exchange it for another that would go straight, when I perceive

that the rudder be incline, in consequence of my wife sit on one

of the string connect with it.



“when this be free the boat go straight, and I pull vigorously for

the open sea. we have several alarm, however, before reach the open

water. first, there be three boat full of schoolboy, splash about

with their oar, who keep on screech to I to mind where I be go.

then a man on the pier roar to I to keep clear of the tug. then,

again, we be nearly run down by a smack.



“‘I certainly don’t call this enjoyment,’ say my wife faintly, strive

to soothe the baby, who have be awaken by the boy, and be cry at

the top of her voice.



“I make no answer, but continue row with great resolution, and, as I

flatter myself, with a dash of science, too, all thing consider,

earnestly look over my shoulder to see where I be go, until my

neck be as stiff as an office-ruler. at last we get out of harbour into

the open sea.



“there be a large steamboat arrive from some place or other; there

be number of people on the pier, but all watch the steamboat and

think about she, and so nobody take the least notice of we. the water

be quiet, with what nautical man call a swell that lift and sink we;

there be a nice wind that cool the air; I see two or three wherry

at anchor in the opposite direction to that I be row in, and I fancy

the people in they be fish. very far out at sea be some ship,

but the only vessel near the harbour be a smack that come out soon

after we, and, fill her sail, push quickly past we. one of the man

upon she call out something as the vessel go by, but I don’t catch

what he say.



“my wife now agree with I that this be real happiness. there be a

delightful quiet in the air, to enjoy which a man must live for eleven

month every year in the bustle and noise of the City; the town look

beautiful in the afternoon light, the top of the white cliff as green

as new silk, and over and over again, after row a few moment, I

would hang on my oar and look at the house in the distance and the

different object change their shape or shift their place. as I

have pull the oar very leisurely indeed, I calculate that it would

not take I more than a quarter of an hour of steady pulling to cover

the distance I have be lazily traverse; I mean I reckon that I

could cover in a quarter of an hour the distance I have slowly come in

three-quarter. that would make the hour; but my wife be enjoy the

air and the sea so thoroughly that I think it would not greatly matter

if we break into another hour. this be a treat we don’t often get. my

wife flatter I by say I row very well, and make the boat go

wonderfully quick, consider I put very little strength into the oar.

I think so, too, indeed, and be surprised to observe how rapidly, in

proportion to my exertion, the land have recede away from we. by this

time the pier be only a black line upon the water, and the people upon

it invisible.



“‘you’ll be face the shore, Sarah,’ say I, ‘when I turn the boat to

row back, and you’ll be much interested in see the various object

grow big and big as we approach the land.’



“‘no wonder people be fond of the water,’ say she; ‘I could stop here

for week.’



“poor woman! I doubt if she’d say that now.



“it be six o’clock when I turn the boat’s head. I never doubt that

I could row back in twenty minute, and reckon that the extra

half-hour would be well worth the money. I row at first with a good

deal of energy, and my wife be delighted at the manner in which I make

the foam fly with my oar. indeed, I work too hard; the exertion soon

tired I, and I perspire at every pore with the heat. it be slightly

distract that the baby, who have be sleep very quietly, should

now wake up and cry for what I suppose you might call her tea, if you

can give regular name to milk-and-water administer about seven time

a day.



“‘I be sorry, William,’ say my wife, ‘that we have stop long than

the hour.’



“‘oh,’ say I, know that the child be run in her head, ‘baby

will do very well until we get home; we shalln’t be long now;’ and again I

exert my strength and toil like a champion rower.



“‘it’s very curious,’ say I, give up after about ten minute, and

feel quite exhausted, and pant for breath.



“‘what’s very curious?’ say my wife.



“‘why,’ say I, pull out my watch, ‘here it be twenty minute past

six, and the land seem rather far off than it be before I turn

the boat’s head towards it.’



“‘yes,’ say she, grow a little pale; ‘I’ve be notice that, too.’



“‘perhaps it want a steady stroke,’ say I, wipe my forehead; and,

settle to the oar again, I row for another ten minute, and then

look over my shoulder. I could not be deceive. row as I would, I not

only could make no way, but the boat actually lose ground. I could not

conceive of a current in the sea; a tide be an intelligible thing to

I in a river, but I could not realize that the great body of water we

be float on be move in a contrary direction to the land. there

be nothing about to give I the idea; no buoy, or anything of that

kind. all that I understand be that the hard I row towards the land

the far we fall away from it. I be heartily frightened, and pull

in the oar to stand up and look around I. my wife begin to cry and the

baby roar as baby can when they be particularly want to keep

quiet. there be some ship, as I have say, a long distance off; and

there be the smack that have pass we, two or three mile distant; but

there be nothing near we. I put my hand to my mouth and shout

towards the land as hard as ever I could, flatter myself that there

be a faint chance of the smooth water convey the sound. I then stand

wave and flourish my hat for at least five minute.



“‘oh, William, what will become of we?’ cry my wife, sob

piteously.



“I be much too upset to answer she. I have hope that we should be

notice by some of the people who keep a look-out on the pier; but as

the time go by, and the sun sink lower, and I could see no sign of

anything come to our rescue, my spirit fall, and I sit down and

stare blankly at my wife. I put out the oar again, but be so weary

that I soon give up row; besides, I feel that we be be carry

away, and that the oar scarcely hinder our progress towards the

ocean. all this while the baby be give we the great trouble with

its incessant crying. my wife fill up the pause of its scream by

anticipate all the horror which might befall we. she assure I that

she could see nothing before we but death from starvation, unless the

sea should rise and upset the boat and drown we, or unless a pass

vessel should crash into we when the darkness fall. what could I do? we

be in one of those situation in which it be simply impossible for

people to help themselves. I could not row; we have no sail, and even if

we have have a sail I should not have know how to use it; I have no mean

of call attention to our position except by wave my hat or

flourish an oar, which seem an idle thing to do, consider what a

speck the boat make upon the water, and how far off we be from

everything but the miserable sea.



“sure enough, presently the sun sink, and though the twilight last a

good bit, yet the water soon grow dark, and speedily after sundown the

coast grow faint, and the ship in the distance be swallow up in the

gloom. when the night fairly come the wind get up, not very much, but

enough to disturb the water, and the wherry begin to slop about

horribly. what be bad, it blow off the land and help to carry we

far away. how I curse my folly for not having bring a man with

I! the crying of the poor hungry little baby and my wife’s moans and

reproach be just madden. it be very fine overhead, the sky full

of star, but there be no moon, and the sea look as black as ink. I

could see the light on the land, and could even very faintly hear the

strain of a band of music play on the cliff, for, as I have tell

you, the wind blow from the shore. I pull out my watch, but though I

hold it close to my nose I could not see what time it be. I keep on

look around in the hope of observe a pass vessel, but, though

no doubt some must have pass, I do not see they.



“my wife be continually say, ‘oh, William, what shall we do?’



“‘do?’ say I. ‘what _can_ we do? we must sit here and wait.’



“‘wait!’ she would cry. ‘what be there to wait for?’



“‘for daylight, if for nothing else.’



“‘but what will daylight do for we? we have be lose in daylight, and

when daylight come where shall we be?’ and here she would hug the poor

cry baby and wish herself dead, and so on.



“Lord, what a time it be! the sea keep the boat rock incessantly, so

that it be impossible for I to stand up. the dew fall like rain, and

my clothe be as heavy as if I have be expose to a shower. my wife

say her limb feel like piece of iron, and that she have the cramp in

every joint, which I could easily understand, for I, too, suffer

atrociously from have to keep seat and to balance myself to the

tumbling about of the wretched little wherry. by degree we lose sight

of the light on shore; and we feel as if we be in the middle of the

Atlantic. once or twice I think of take to the oar again, but when

the light disappear there be nothing to aim for. how we pass the

hour I cann’t tell you. the baby would wake and cry until she cry

herself to sleep, then wake and cry herself to sleep again, and so on,

hour after hour. my wife and I fall silent; we have exhaust all that

_could_ be say, and we sit there like two statue. to my die hour I

shall remember the gurgling and sob noise of the water splash

against the boat’s side, and the dreadful silence overhead and around,

above the water, as I may say.



“it must have be past midnight, when I think I hear a kind of

groan or rumble sound in the wind. I could not imagine what it

could be, until, look into the darkness on my right hand, I spy

three light upon the sea--one green, one red, and one white--this last

much high than the other. soon after there be a heavy noise of

washing water, and just over the white light there be a shower of

spark, and presently a great black shadow stand up on the sea and

blot out the star behind it. I be weak and wear out--terrify to a

degree by the swift approach of this steamer--and though I manage to

shout, my voice seem to stick in my throat. the great vessel sweep

past we not above twenty yard distant; save those lamp she be all

in darkness, and soon after she have go by I think the wherry would

have upset in the wave the steamer have leave behind. my wife scream as

the boat spring up and down, and every instant I expect the sea to

rush into we. I shout again to the steamer, hope that I might be

hear. this time my voice carry well, but nothing come of it; the

steamer rush on, and be soon out of sight.



“the dawn be just break, when I see a vessel make a black mark

against the pale green light in the place where the sun be come. it

take I some time to find out which way she be go, but presently the

rise sun make she plain, and I see that she be a small smack, and

that she aim directly for we. I manage to stand up in the wherry and

flourish my hat. there be no coast to be see--nothing visible upon the

sea but that smack. so far as water go, we might have be in the

middle of the big ocean in the world. I perceive before long that

the smack see we, for she lower one of her sail, and come along

slowly. I look at my wife to see how this adventure have serve she,

and it seem to I that she have age twenty year. her face be hollow,

her dress draggle and limp with the dew; she be a most melancholy

object to look at. I hardly know she, indeed; and she be equally

astonish by my appearance, as she afterwards tell I. who could

suppose that a night spend in an open boat at sea would work such a

change in people’s look? as for poor little baby, she have be cry

on and off all night, and, be pretty nearly perish with hunger, she

be a distressing thing, truly, for we parent to see. it be nearly

three-quarter of an hour before the smack come close to we, count

from the time I have first see she. a great man in yellow clothe bawl

out, ‘what’s that boat, and what do you want?’ you might have suppose

he would guess our want by our appearance.



“‘we’ve be carry away to sea,’ I answer, in a faint voice, for I

feel as weak as an infant and just fit to cry like one, ‘and we’ve be

in this boat all night.’



“‘where do you come from?’ he call.



“I tell he, and he answer, ‘we’ll tow you in. look out for the end of

the line;’ and another man throw a rope at I.



“I catch it, but do not know what to do with it; see which, the

first man tell I to keep hold, and drag the wherry up to the smack,

and then get into she and attach the line to the boat.



“‘will you sit here or come aboard?’ he ask.



“‘oh, come aboard, certainly,’ I reply; so he take the baby and pass

it to a sailor on the smack, and then help my wife up, and then I.



“so here we be, save; but faint, break-down, feel as if we have

be dig out of the grave. luckily they have a few tin of swiss milk in

the cabin, and so poor little baby get something to eat at last. also

they give we some corn beef and bread, which we devour gratefully,

after the manner of shipwreck people. the captain of the smack laugh

when I tell he we have originally start for an hour’s row.



“‘how much do they charge you for an hour?’ say he.



“‘eighteenpence,’ I answer.



“‘you have have a good eighteen-pennorth,’ say he. ‘you may thank the

Lord, master, that ye’re alive to pay even eighteenpence. D’ye know how

many mile you’ve drift from your port?’



“‘no,’ say I.



“‘well then,’ say he, ‘you’ve drift eleven mile. there’s the

coast--you can calculate for yourself;’ and he point to the white

cliff, which be visible from the smack’s deck, though not from the

boat. a fearfully long distance off they look, to be sure.



“‘William,’ say my wife at this moment, ‘I’ll never come upon the water

again.’



“‘nor I, Sarah,’ say I; ‘at least without a man.’



“‘man or no man,’ say she, ‘I’ll never venture my life again.’



“‘and I have no doubt she will keep her word, though it willn’t cost she a

very great effort to do so, for I be quite sure I shall never attempt to

make she break it.”



“and so,” say I, “you get home safe?”



“yes,” he answer; “the smack land we in about two hour. the boatman

want to charge I for twelve hour’ use of his wherry; but I get off

for half a sovereign, which I think cheap, as he talk of have the

law of I.”



and here terminate this middle-aged City clerk’s narrative. the moral

of it be not far off, and may be find without much hunting; and that a

little musing over it shall not be without value, any man may judge for

himself if he will but take his stand upon a british pier and watch the

typical seaside visitor enjoy “an hour’s row.”









_the PLEASURES of yachting._





Steam have play sad havoc with the beauty of our naval and merchant

vessel; but, though it have not spare our pleasure fleet, it have leave

untouched numerous graceful fabric among the yacht of the country, and

sail-power may survive for many year yet in the most beautiful form it

have ever be mould to by the genius of man. there be a story tell of

a butterfly alight on the breast of a die girl and take wing at

the very moment she expire, and soar into the blue sky with the

sunshine sparkle on its bright wing. I think of this tale the other

day when I spy the hulk of what appear to have be a sailing

frigate or an old East India merchantman tow up Channel. there be a

strong, clear wind, and the water flash like a prism, and I be gaze

with interest at the poor old dismaste hulk when a fine schooner yacht,

beat to the eastward, swirl up under her stern. a noble sight be

that pleasure vessel. her lee rail be almost flush with the foam which

sweep like a storm of snow under the gleam milk-white curve of her

low cloth; to windward her sheathing be hove high, and the yellow

metal glitter like new gold as it glance through the network of spray

and the shine emerald-green fibre of water which leapt about her

glossy side. she might have be the very spirit of the old dismantle

sailing ship, leap into bright and beautiful being as the most

exquisite and the complete expression of marine grace. it would have

gratify the most morose sailor to see she. here be a sight to comfort

Jack for the loss of the noble sailing ship of his young day. the

grand pile of canvas, the little skysail top the swell

pyramid, the magnificent sweep of jibboom bear their

marble-colour cloth in layer like a heap of cloud, the ringing

minstrelsy of the wind among the taut hemp that resemble a spider’s web

as you look at it against the sky of the horizon--these thing be go,

or fast go; the ocean will soon be bare of they, and the star-like

shine of sail upon the sea-line smother by the long black coil of

furnace smoke. but while such yacht as that whose flash progress I

watch remain afloat the sea will still possess her english beauty.



it be the owner of such vessel who be perpetuate all that be fair,

all that be memorable, of the tradition of our english ship-building

yard. the survival be a very fit one. it seem proper, indeed, that the

stateliness and elegance of the sailing vessel should come into the

keeping of man to whom the deep be its own exceed great reward--as

poetry be to Coleridge--who traverse it for love only of its caressing

water and the glorious life of its noble expanse, and who make it the

framework for marine picture into whose idealization enter all that

money, fine taste, and devotion to what be beautiful and harmonious can

furnish.



surely to those who love she for herself the sea be a bountiful and

great-hearted mother. the fascination the ocean exercise over the mind

cannot be express in language; and happy be the man who, yield to

she spell, count himself one of her son as a yachtsman. Mercantile

Jack may profess to despise such seafaring as a fresh-water job; but,

nevertheless, let he own that he envy the sand-white deck, the snug

forecastle, the easy life, the glorious run under blue sky and over

tumble and silver-bright water. no other form of “sailorize” yield

so much unalloyed pleasure. privacy be the first grand privilege. you

will get that in your yacht, but you will get it aboard no other kind of

ship which ever I have hear of. no amount of passage-money will save

you from worry and companionship you may not be in the humour to enjoy

on board the fine passenger vessel. it be hotel-life: you be a

number; you have luggage; you be make the voyage for a direct object;

in short, you have a destination, and the have a destination make one

of the main difference between yacht and go to sea in any other

way.



a yacht be a man’s home. he need never be in a hurry. like Jefferson in

“Rip Van Winkle,” he live about in spot. he may leave a good deal to

his skipper, but he be always master; he own the craft which other

steer, and never a humour can come into his head which he may not

indulge without have anybody to argue with he. it be a fine thing to

be lord of the sea in this fashion. a captain of a big ship be a great

man, but he be a sort of a slave also. his business be to make haste,

and obstacle vex his soul. the patent log that he tow astern typifie

the condition of his mind. a head sea be an affliction, and most of the

wonder of the deep be great nuisance to he. he want to sight

nothing. he object to excitement and adventure. all that he pray for

be fine weather and so many nautical mile a day. these be the

penalty of have a destination.



the bliss of yacht lie in the having to go nowhere in particular,

and one port be as good as another. if you cann’t weather a point,

then there be nothing to do but put your helm up and come back again.

the barometer seldom tell lie, and one of its safe reading, which

make yacht so delightful, be “keep the harbour aboard.” that

certainly may always be do in the English Channel, and not for this

reason only do one cease to wonder that it should be the most popular

of all yachting water. much have be write about yacht in the

Scotch lake and northward among the isle. such cruising might suit a

man who be easily sea-sick, and who be never so comfortable as when his

tow-rope be aboard a tug. but the yachtsman who have the instinct of a

seaman will choose the wide water of the English Channel, push away

to the westward until the atlantic swell be under his forefoot and his

white sail mirror in water as blue as the heaven. the Channel be a

sea of itself, and most of the change of the sea may be feel and

enjoy on its breast. here you will get breeze which toss a yacht

prettily enough, and the calm be make beautiful and soothing by the

gentle swell that run out of the vague horizon, and keep the water

flash and fade under the sun. once to the westward of the North

Foreland, there be no fine space of water for yachting, and nowhere

more beautiful shore and nobler coast scenery. it be a great maritime

highway, too, always full of ship, and so crowded with marine interest

that the yachtsman be never weary of look over the side of his

vessel. give a strong and sweeping wind from the southward of east,

with the sharp blue sky which that sort of breeze make; and let the sun

still be soar, and the atmosphere so transparent that the coast

stand along like a photograph; and let your mainsheet be ease, and

the white height of the North Foreland on your starboard quarter, the

whole of the grand old Channel be under your bowsprit. though there be

no cup to win, there shall be a hundred race to run as you go; and,

keep to leeward of the Goodwins, every jump of the yacht unroll a

glistening length of white, and green, and brown, and golden shore.



indeed, there be not a little sport to be get out of the unpremeditated

race of yachting. I remember once come up Channel, homeward bind, in

a fine clipper ship. we have the wind abeam, and fore-topmast

stud-sail out, and we go ahead of everything like a roll of smoke,

until, come abreast of the Isle of Wight, a powerful yawl--as superb a

yacht as ever I see--come froth and buzz along, with her main boom

almost amidship, and Dunnose like a blue shadow over her stern. she

ratche like a phantom to windward of we, and then, settle herself

upon our weather quarter, starboard her helm, ease away her sheet

fore and aft, and overhaul we as if she have a mind to tow we. she be

in a smother of foam. it must have be up to a man’s knee in the lee

scupper. she show we the whole of her deck--a lady sit in the

companion, coolly ogle we through a binocular glass; three or four

yachtsman aft, squat under the weather rail. but the view she

offer be not prolong. she forge ahead of we like a “bonito,” and

in a couple of hour be a small lean white pillar upon the horizon

dead over our bow.



these be the unpremeditated match I mean, and I have know some of

they to be run with as wild a desire for triumph as ever a regular

yacht-race kindle. they use to make one of the heartiest pleasure of

yachting; but nowadays where be the foeman worthy of the steel of the

slashing yawl, and cutter, and schooner? nearly everything that

float go by steam, and for a yacht to race a steamer would be as

sensible as to make up a Derby of locomotive and thoroughbred. yet

those crank racer, with their enormous spread of cloth--though they be

thing of beauty--be certainly not a joy to everybody. they be very

proper to take prize, but those who love the sea most wisely will least

envy the privilege of the owner of such craft. sail with your mast

at an angle of fifty degree, half the mainsail dark with water, the

froth hiss and seething and bubble up to the lee side of the

skylight, all hand hold on to windward and wonder what’s go

to happen next, may be exhilarate to some soul, but it be a mad sort

of yacht. these crank and nimble spinner give you no chance of

look about. they be a fine sight to watch. I know nothing more

exciting to witness than a great narrow-waiste yawl, almost on her beam

end, hurl through an ocean of foam, jump the sea until half her

keel be out of water, then bury her bow in the storm of froth as if

she be about to dive out of sight, her metal to windward look like

a sheet of polished gold, with the sunshine sparkle in the wet of it.

but to be aboard! deck that one can walk on may be an unsailorly

prejudice, yet they be comfortable; and the obligation to stick to

windward and to hold on with clench tooth grow tedious and even

fatigue if too long impose.



but the word yacht be a generic term, and comprise many different kind

of vessel. the middle kind between the knife-like racer and the

motherly, lubberly tub, be the good for those who go down to the sea in

pleasure vessel, not to do business, but to enjoy the freshness and

wonder and beauty of the ocean. there be score of they afloat,

superbly model craft, whose line would have make the old Baltimore

clipper-builder green with envy. I will name no name, but will think

of a yacht I have see--a schooner, near about 150 ton by yacht

measurement, with magnificent spar exquisitely stay, a bow bold about

the figurehead, but fine away with delicate keenness at the forefoot,

with such a swell of the side as promise stability in a gale of wind,

but arch thence to the keel in a conformation so tenderly sinuous and

beautifully clean that a sailor would want to know no more to enter she

in his mind as one of the fast vessel of her class. this she be, but

she give you a beam as well as speed. there be plenty of room to walk

about her deck; there be no fear of fall down the forehatch for want

of a gangway to get into the eye of she; the coil of her run-gear

be never in the road. be there anything more tenderly beautiful than a

vessel of this kind slightly lean under her cotton-white cloth, she

polished and swell height of canvas softly shade at the leech,

the brass-work on her deck full of blind crimson star which wink

like burst of fire from the mouth of cannon watch from a distance,

as the lift of the swell veer the brilliant metal in and out of the

sphere of the sun, whilst a line of froth stream past she like a shower

of silver dust upon the sea, and the gentle moaning of water at the stem

mingle with the vibratory hum of the wind in the vessel. this be

the sort of vessel in which a man can take his ease and enjoy all that

the sea have to offer. and this, too, be your ship for Channel cruising.

she would carry you round the world if you have the mind to try she.

she’ll creep into the wind’s eye with the luff of her foresail blow

to windward, not shiver, but stand out full of wind that way,

whilst the after half be draw and do its work. I know she to be a

typical boat, and that be why I describe she. whilst such craft as she

remain afloat, the grace of the sailing vessel in its most beauteous

form survive, and steam may be defy to demolish a linger but most

noble marine ideal realize.



owner of yacht do not all take the same view of the delightful

pastime. between the yachtsman who never seem so happy as when he be

out of sounding, and those sailor who creep from port to port, and

take a three week’ spell of rest in every harbour they succeed in

making, there be a prodigious stretch, fill up by a surprising variety

of taste. but the harbour-haunt yachtsman grow rare. his excursion

to sea, even out of sight of land, be every year more frequent. he

learn to hear a music in the wind that’s pipe merrily, and the threat

of lightning in the horn moon cease to scare he. this be as it

should be. yachting be surely but a sorry entertainment when warps hold

your vessel against a stone or wooden pier, and no lively recreation

offer than bob for flounder in the mud at the bottom of the water

alongside. our english summer be not very long, and there be much to

be see, much to inspirit the mind, much to invigorate the body. the

warm and brightly-colour sea, for many a league enrich with verdant

and dazzling and tender stretch of coast scenery, court the fortunate

yachtsman with promise which it never break. it be not race only, it

be not sail only; it be the calm day sleep under the rich azure

heaven; the water a breathless surface of molten glass, shadow here

and there where the shallow sounding be; the horizon streak with

float wreath of vapour or darken by the blueish smoke of a

long-vanish steamer; the coastline some mile away swim in the

haze of heat, and the water in the south blending with the flood of

light which the sun flash into it. here and there be a motionless

smack, with her reddish sail reflect without a tremor under she; or a

distant ship whose white canvas seem to be melt upon the faint light

blue over the horizon. or it be the summer night, with a flood of

moonlight shiver the ripple, whilst on either hand the sea stretch

away in solemn darkness touch faintly in place by the lustre of the

glorious planet unpale by the moonshine. a soft breeze murmur over

the water, and keep the spectral canvas on high sleeping, and a narrow

wake go away astern into the darkness, with fitful flash of

phosphoru in the circle eddy, in the run of the ripple as they

break near the silent hull.



small wonder, indeed, that the sea should court man as it do, and

fascinate they too. happy the man who can take the pleasure it yield as

a yachtsman, and in his own beautiful vessel can traverse its glorious

water as idly, and freely, and gaily as the wind that impel he.









_a DRUNKEN SHIP._





in one of Edgar Poe’s story there be an account of a crew cling to

the bottom of their capsize vessel, and watch a ship approach they.

she come yaw and steer very wildly, but there be people aboard,

and the poor sailor be full of hope; until on a sudden an insufferable

smell be bear to they by the wind, and they discover that the figure

loll upon the ship’s side be putrifye corpse.



this tale of horror as well as of imagination come into my head some

time ago, when I read the evidence that have be tender in St.

George’s Hall, Liverpool, by a certain pier-manager and coxswain of the

lifeboat belong to a north-western town. he say that at about

half-past five in the morning he be rouse out by a man who tell he

that there be a vessel drift ashore. he hurry down to the beach,

and see a barque of between 300 and 400 ton a short distance off under

low topsail. there be a fresh breeze from the westward. he watch

the vessel a few minute, and perceive that she would sometimes fall

off so as to bring the breeze on her quarter, and then round close to

the wind, like Poe’s dreadful ship, and that she be come ashore as

fast as ever she could drive. the lifeboat be launch when this

strangely-behave barque be within a hundred and fifty fathom of the

beach, and on the boat get alongside, a strong smell of rum and

water be find to pervade the atmosphere. a man get on to the rail and

drop into the boat, and the coxswain say “he seem stupefied, take

no notice of anything, and do not speak.” this be the skipper. the

rest of the crew tumble into the lifeboat and be convey ashore,

while the barque take the ground and become a total wreck, nothing be

save but some sail and a few store.



such a very unusual circumstance as that of a well-find barque sailing

ashore, as one might put it, of her own will, be sure to have a queer

story behind it. and assuredly the story be a queer one, make one of

the most disgraceful narrative to be find in the modern marine annal.

it shall be tell by a speciman of one of those plain, honest, english

seaman who captain say be no long to be find, and whose extinction,

they declare, oblige they to ship “Dutchmen.” I will not give this

excellent man his name, glad as I should be to do so, for the punishment

inflict on the captain and mate by the court that inquire into their

conduct would render a large public identification a needless

supplementary penalty. the certificate of competency hold by the captain

have be cancel, but to the mate there have be grant a twelve

month’ chance of reformation, and this alone should explain the reason

for suppress all name.



“the barque be a vessel of 340 ton, and we have a crew of ten man, not

count the captain and mate. we be bind for Quebec, which, I

reckon, should make a ship’s course about west by south; but on this, as

you’ll take note presently, all mariner don’t seem to be agree. the

whole of the crew, save I and another whose name shall be Bill, be

drunk when the barque leave Liverpool. speak of the fo’ksle, I don’t

mean to say there’s anything unusual in this. drink’s grow with

legislation. in old time, when there be less law, there be less lush.

it’s a teetotal age, this; nothing but water go in vessel, and the

consequence be that man newly ship, know that there’ll be no grog

betwixt this and the next port, go in for a bout of drink to serve

they, as it might be, for the whole voyage. see ’em come aboard,

sprawl and roar, too sick to stand, roll below, and leave the

ship to sail away with no one but the idler--and they drunk, too,

maybe--to do she work for twenty-four hour or long. if I be an owner

my ship shouldn’t be a teetotaler. every day, at noon, there should be a

can of rum on the capstan for the man--a tot apiece; but I’d make this

rule, that any man as come aboard in liquor should have no grog serve

out to he for the rest of the voyage. that would stop the drunkenness

ship carry away from the port, and all the danger which a drunken

crew bring on a vessel that’s get to grope her way down river and

along channel full of peril.



“well, there be ten of we, and eight be drunk. I’m speak of

forrard; I’ll come aft presently. I never see man bad in liquor. you

remember they Scotchmen that use to stand at tobacconist’ door,

take a pinch of snuff?--dummies they be, you’ll recall. well, think

of give one of ’em a shove, and see he fall. if ye can fix such an

object in your imagination you’ll comprehend the sort of helplessness of

my eight shipmate. they lie in the fo’ksle as lifeless as bit of

timber; and this be the condition of the barque, we be tow out

with a pilot aboard, and then, when abreast of the Nor’-west Lightship,

be leave to shift for ourselves. the mate be aft, I at the wheel,

and Bill forward. there be not an inch of canvas on our vessel, and no

one on deck except those I’ve mention. whilst we be tow, the

mate come up to have a look at the compass now and again, and then I

notice that if he ben’t downright slew he don’t want very many more

nip to settle his business. he go lurch along till he come

abreast of the main rigging, and here he lay hold and sing out, ‘all

hand make sail. tumble up, my lively heartie! bear a hand with your

hair oil and your silk stocking, my sweet and noble fellow!’
