“but nobody take any notice except Bill, who sing out, ‘there’s no

tumble up aboard this galliant vessel, sir--leastway, forrard;

there’s naught but tumble down.’ at which the mate burst into a loud

laugh, sway upon the rope he have hold of as though he mean to swig

off on it. then, look up and around, he sing out--



“‘this ain’t a steamer. the sail must be loose and the yard hoist,

bully, if the Liverpool gell be ever to clap eye on us brave mariner

again; so jump below among they dreadful drunkard and rout ’em out.

rout ’em out, do you hear?’



“well, Bill do as he be tell, and after a bit he manage to shove two

or three of the crew through the scuttle on to the deck. they stand

blinkin’ in the light like owl, roll up against one another with

their hair over their face, and their clothe look as if they have

be put on upside down.



“‘now, then! now, then!’ sing out the mate, who couldn’t keep his leg

without hold on; ‘what’s the meaning of this here dissipation?

there’s no drink allow aboard this tidy little ship. there’s nothing

but the teetotal lay to be find in this handsome hooker. milk and

water, my bully sailor lad! that’s the tap if ever ye want to end as

philosopher. Loose the fore-topmast staysail. Loose the spanker. get

the main-topmast staysail on she. Lay out, some one, and loose the inner

jib.’ and he rattle order after order as though he’d get a ship’s

company of fifty man to do his bidding.



“how the drunken fellow scrape through the job I’m sure I don’t know.

it be a bad look-out for we two sober man, but for the life of I I

couldn’t help laugh to watch the sailor Bill have manage to shove on

deck go aloft. talk of hang on with your eyelid! again and again I

expect to see ’em all drop overboard; but I suppose their instinct

for hold on be there, though their sense be go, and the same

mental henergy it be, no doubt, as enable they to get the gasket

adrift and loose the low topsail. when those sail be sheet

home--the jib, staysail, and spanker be already set--the drunken man

refuse to do any more work; they roll over to the scuttle and

disappear, the mate look on, but too intoxicate to act. the

skipper all this while never show himself. I ask the mate what

course I be to steer.



“‘course?’ say he; ‘why, keep the vessel’s head followin’ the jibboom,

cann’t ye?’



“‘easy enough,’ say I; ‘but where’s the jibboom a-go?’



“‘no impudence!’ he cry out. ‘smother I if I know what the british

sailor’s a-come to. it’s all drink and jaw nowadays. what’s become of

all the old, ’spectable, sober seamen?--tell I that, you terrapin.’



“there be no use argue with a man who couldn’t stand without hold

on. I say, ‘I’m not go to steer this barque all day--’specially as

we seem bind to nowhere. my trick be up and out a long spell since.’



“‘and d’ye think,’ say he, ‘the vessel don’t know her way without you?

hook it forrard, afore I skin yer.’



“I let go the wheel and walk forward. I look behind I as I go,

make sure that he’d take my place. but the deuce a bit. he be lean

against the rail, and shake his fist at I when I turn my head, and

there be the barque without any one steer she, her fore and aft

canvas full, but her topsail aback, and her whole company, save two,

so drunk as to be incapable. it be a good job that old draining be

not so drunk as the other, otherwise we should have be oblige to

light the galley fire and get ourselves supper. we be not disposed to

take this job upon ourselves, so we haul he on deck and give he

several bucket of water, which appear to wash some of the fume out

of his intellect, and he then turn to--in a very staggering fashion,

sartinly--and get we some tea, be scare by our threat to drown he

out of hand if he don’t tend to our want.



“I and my mate hang about the deck forrard watch to see if the

skipper show himself, but he never appear, which, take along with

the condition of the mate, make we suppose he be drunk too; but we

couldn’t have swear to this without get a sight of he first. I say

to Bill, ‘here’s a pretty look-out. what’s to be do? no one at the

wheel; no one in charge; everybody drunk, and the night come along.’



“‘there’s nothing to be do,’ answer bill, ‘except to turn in and take

our chance. it willn’t do for we to take command of the barque. if there’s

to be a mess, let it find the skipper boss, not we. I don’t want no

magistrate’s job, for one. we’re but common sailor, and common sailor

have but a poor chance now when it come to law, and the fight’s between

they and the captain.’



“this be a middlin’ sensible view; but still, life’s life, and I

couldn’t quite see my way to turn in aboard a drift ship, and take

our chance of all go well throughout the night. so, call old

draining, who be get his sense and begin to understand the

muddle we be all in, we light our pipe and have a long confab, the

end of it be an agreement that the three of we should keep a

look-out, turn and turn about. there be to be no steering--nothing but

look. well, I keep the first look-out, and in all they hour I never

see either the captain or the mate on deck. the breeze be small, and

the ship lie steady enough, her topsail aback and her staysail

drawing. two or three steamer drive past, and I’m pretty sartin they’d

have be into we if I haven’t take the precaution to get the side

light over. Bill relieve I at six bell, we having settle for his

turn to follow mine, so as to give draining time to sleep off the rest

of the rum that work in his system. when I go below the fo’ksle be

as hot as an oven, such a smell of liquor about as would have make you

think yourself in a public-house, and all hand snore so loud that you

might have reckon the barque be sail ten mile an hour, and that

noise the sound of the water roll away from her stem. I turn in all

stand, ready for whatever might happen, and fall asleep, and when I

wake it be to the tune of a desperate hammering on deck. it be broad

daylight, and, when I tumble up, I find the mate beat the deck and

bawl at the top of his voice, ‘up with ye, you drunken swine! up

naked, every mother’s son of you, and don’t stop to dress!’ he be

roar, fill up his meaning with more oath than he have finger and

toe. he be just in the same condition he have be in all along,

rolling and sprawl here and there, and fog the air all about he

with the smell o’ spirit.



“Old Drainings be at the wheel, and I spy the captain aft, hold on

to a backstay with one hand and shake his other hand at draining, who

grin in his face. though pretty near the whole ship’s length be

betwixt we, I easily see that the captain be as drunk as his mate.

by-and-by he turn his head and sing out for the mate to lay aft. the

mate go, and the skipper, fetch he a thump whack on the

back--mean for love and good fellowship--cast his arm round the

other’s neck, and down they tumble below, for another reviver, no doubt.

draining leave the wheel and come forrard.



“‘I cann’t help laugh at the old man,’ say he. ‘never heerd such

nonsense as he talk. but, all the same, what’s to do?’ say he. ‘we

shall be drive ashore if we don’t mind. have any of the man

recover?’



“‘I’ve not have time to see,’ I answer, and I drop down the fo’ksle

hatch to have a look. I stir they as be on the deck with my foot and

make some of they talk to I; but there be not one man among they as

be of any use. they have not only come aboard steep to the eye in

drink, but have bring a quantity of lush along with they, and two or

three empty black bottle knock about ’splaine how it be that

sleep in all night haven’t make these scowbank fit for duty.



“well, I don’t want to make an endless job of this yarn, or I’d give you

the particular of that day and the night as follow. by that time most

of the man have recover their sense, and I and Bill take care, as

fast as ever we could get ’em to sit up and listen, to ’splain the

quandary the vessel be in, and our danger. this sober ’em quick

than water would have do. they come on deck and take a look around,

see nobody at the wheel, no one in charge, and nothing on the barque but

what we have make shift to hoist after the tug have leave we.



“this be the afternoon of the third day. the weather look dirty in

the south-west, and shortly before five o’clock the wind breeze up

hard. luckily we be under small canvas. I say to the man, ‘the good

thing we can do be to haul down the staysail and heave she to. there’s

no telling where she’s be drift to all these day. no sight have

be take, the log never hove, no reckoning of any kind keep. whether

we’re off England, Hireland, or Scotland I’m not go to calculate; but

one thing I’m certain sure of, we shall be have one of they kingdom

close aboard of we before long; and so I reckon our business be to slow

down this here drift as fur as we can, whilst we see if the captain

mean to take charge and sail the vessel to Quebec, or keep drunk and

send we all to the bottom.’



“everybody be agreeable, we haul down the staysail and back the

foretopsail. there be no watch, the crew haven’t be divide;

however, we form ourselves into two gang, and agree to keep watch

and watch till the morning; then, if thing remain as they be, we

arrange for some of we to go aft to the captain, and, if he refuse to

do his duty, to hoist a distress signal, and ’splain our situation to

the first ship as come along. the deuce of it be, ye see, there be

ne’er a man forrard as know anything of navigation. have we turn to

and seize the skipper’s instrument and chart they’d have be of no

use to we. well, next morning arrive, and find the barque still

drift and the weather as thick as mud in a wine-glass. all hand

assemble, and we hold a sort o’ parliament, and then it be agree that

I and another should go aft and inquire of the captain what he mean by

this conduct. ’cordingly we lay aft, and go into the cabin find the

mate lie there drunk, though not incapable. he ask we what we

want; but we take no notice, push on to the captain’s berth. we

hammer on the door, but get no answer open it, and see he lie

sound asleep and kinder stupefy in his bunk. we lay hold of he and

haul till we’d rouse he up.



“‘captain,’ say I, ‘we’ve come aft to ask what you mean to do with the

barque. she’s drift anyhow, and all hand feel their life to be in

danger.’



“‘Pooh, pooh!’ say he, stretch his arm and gape, ‘it’s all right.

have a glass of grog?’



“‘no,’ I say firmly; ‘we don’t want no grog. what we require be to know

what you mean to do?’



“instead of answer, he lie back, turn over and shut his eye; so,

see that there be no satisfaction to be get, we come away and go

forrard again. the man be now thoroughly scared. they say they

warn’t go to stand skylark of this kind, and if the captain don’t

turn to and take charge and sail the ship back to Liverpool, they’d

knock off work. I go aft once more with this message, but though I

nearly drag the captain out of his bed, I couldn’t make he

understand, nor even rouse he up. so I walk up to the mate and tell

he of the man’s resolution.



“‘I don’t care,’ say he; ‘it’s no business of mine. I’m not go to do

anything without the captain’s order.’



“we be in a regular fix. the weather be so thick that it would need a

ship to come very close to make out any signal we might hoist; we none

of we know where we be, in what direction to steer, what to do with

the barque if we take charge of she. whilst we be debate, the mate

come out, and order we to square the yard.



“‘what for?’ say the crew.



“‘why, for Liverpool,’ he answer.



“we turn to with a will, the mate stand at the cabin door look

at we. we hold on E.S.E. till about midnight, when we spy a light on

the port quarter, and the mate say it be the Chickens off the Calf o’

man. it prove to be nothing of the kind, but Morecambe Bay light. at

daybreak the land be plain to be see about four mile distant, and the

captain, who be now on deck, give order for the helm to be put up to

let she drive ashore, which she do, the lifeboat come out when we be

close on to the beach, and take we all off. the first to drop into the

boat be the skipper; he ben’t too drunk to do that.



“what d’ye say to this tale of the sea, sir? what’ll the public think of

merchant sailor after hear it? should you think proper to print it,

I’ll allow that there’ll not be a landsman as willn’t reckon it an

out-and-out twister, spin from the winch o’ your own invention. but,

that there may be no doubt about it, just add what the finding of the

Court be as inquire into this business: ‘neither the master nor the

mate attend properly to his duty in navigate the vessel. they be

both under the influence of drink during the voyage. the vessel be not

navigate with proper and seaman-like care. she be strand owe to

the utter neglect from drunkenness of both master and mate. the Court

consider that this be about as gross a case as ever come before a

court of inquiry, and find both master and mate grievously and

wrongfully in default.’



“mild enough, sir. have the Court be aboard, you may take your oath

they’d have draw it considerably strong.”









_a SAILOR’S CLUB._





not very far from the London Docks, and within a stone’s throw of that

refined and odoriferous thoroughfare know as Leman Street, Whitechapel,

there be situate a large, fine building, with entrance command two

street, and a summit that tower very nobly among the adjacent roof.

once upon a time the Royal Brunswick Theatre stand where that house now

stand, and vestige of the old structure still linger in the form of

some pillar or column at the main entrance, and various underground

avenue, in whose atmosphere, despite forty year of very strong marine

flavouring, there seem to lurk to this hour a kind of ghostly smell of

ancient orange-peel. the house be know far and wide as the Well Street

Home for Sailors, and I once accept an invitation from the manager to

overhaul the premise, and judge for myself to what extent the home

improve upon the comfort and privilege the sailor flatter himself he

may obtain at a common seaman’s boarding or lodge house. I must own

that I approach the place with a certain amount of foregone prejudice.

Establishments know as Harbours of Refuge, Seaman’s Sheet Anchor, Ports

of Call, and the like, all mariner who will not sham piety for the sake

of a coat, or a plug of tobacco, or a meal of bread and meat will keep

to windward of. Jack object to this kind of classification. he

dislike to be deal with as something apart from the ordinary run of

mortal; to be preach to in language which the minister may fondly

imagine to be the dialect of the sea; to have tract dole out to he in

the form of marine allegory, as if he could comprehend no other

allusion to life and death, and sin and virtue, than those which

refer to heave billow and storm-drive bark and broken tackle;

and when ashore, to make one of a flock of seaman, to meet nobody but

seaman, to go to prayer in a church fill with seamen;--to be treat,

indeed, as if he ought to carry a badge or number on his back, as if his

whole class be socially taboo. so, think this well Street Home to

have something of the old unpleasant and ill-judging form of charity

mix up in its composition, and considerably disturb in mind by the

first four line of its forty-seventh annual report, I enter the Dock

Street entrance, never doubt but that I should meet with plenty of

feature to account for the sailor’s preference for the grimy, frowsy,

and squalid lodging-house, of which there be some dozen in the

neighbourhood.



I find myself in a very large hall fill with seaman. there be

perhaps hardly a nationality that be not represent. englishman and

Scandinavians be plentiful; but in numerous place be black and

yellow skin, the sight of which carry the mind thousand of mile

east and south, and bring up vision of sky different indeed from

the brown heaven which be career in gloomy fold over the

chimney-pot visible through the window. in a few moment I be join

by the manager, and we proceed to inspect the premise. in a manner it

be like survey St. Paul’s Cathedral. big as the building look

outside, it seem four time as large again when I begin to roam about

it. room lead into room, wing conduct into wing, until methought

Whitechapel itself might seem to lack area enough for the accommodation

of this most ramified and capacious interior. behind a glass front stand

a porter wade through several huge pile of letter in search of those

expect by some dozen man, who eagerly wait while he look.



“the correspondence here must be enormous,” say I, “judge by those

sample.”



“it be enormous,” answer the manager. “thousand upon thousand of

letter and telegram be receive and distribute in the course of the

year.”



we enter a large room with a circular counter in it, behind which be

several clerk hard at work over their ledger, while a number of seaman

be draw or pay in money.



“this be the bank,” say the manager; “here we receive such money as

the man choose to deposit, and credit they with the wage which they

have to receive from the ship they have be discharge from. here,

too, we cash their allotment note, and what we do in that way you may

guess by look at that long box there, that be full of allotment note

which be mature at various date.”



“but,” say I, “I think the allotment note be only make payable to a

relative or to a saving-bank. this be not a savings-bank?”



“oh,” he exclaim drily, “there be two kind of allotment note. one

be, as you say, payable only to a relative or a savings-bank; the other

be an illegal document, sanction by the Board of Trade, February,

1868--here it be in the corner: you see their imprimatur?” say he,

hand I one of the note.



“what be the meaning of this,” say I, “at the bottom of the

note?--‘Caution.--The Merchant Shipping Act do not provide summary

remedy in the case of this note.’”



“only a confession that a blunder be make,” he answer, “when the Act

be pass. the advance note be say to encourage crimp; accordingly

the allotment note be substitute. the seaman protest, as he find

the note practically useless. instead of rescind or modify the

Act, an illegal concession be make by the issue of note payable to

anybody, like the old advance note. the issue be sanction by the Board

of Trade, who compromise with their official conscience by give the

holder of the note to understand that he cannot recover upon the note by

summary remedy. the old advance note be make payable three day after

the man have sail in the ship; in the present note the shipowner

protect himself by make the note payable fifteen day, or in some

instance thirty day, after the man have sail. this may not increase

the risk, but the delay in payment cause the holder to charge a heavy

rate of interest for cash the note, so that practically the Act

leave the sailor as much at the mercy of the boarding-house keeper as

he be in the day of the advance note. we have hitherto charge nothing

for cash these note; but we shall have to do so in self-protection,

for we be perpetually lose money by they, and the law, which

sanction their issue, yet deprive the holder of all mean of

recover on they.”



so much for british maritime legislation, think I. here be people,

willing to pay the sailor the amount his note be worth without any

deduction whatever, oblige to own that they can no long act in this

liberal manner, because the law prevent they from deal with the

dishonest client who rob they! will the day never come when the hide

part of our gigantic marine interest will be capably represent in the

House of Commons?



“and pray,” say I to the manager, “where be your bedroom?”



he lead I a short distance, and presently we come to a stand at the

bottom of what I may call a shaft of gallery of a very curious

skeleton-like appearance. the high tier be probably about seventy

foot. every fibrine-look gallery or platform run the whole length of

the wing, and be flank on either hand with row of little bulkheade

room call cabin, all of they number, and every one contain an

exceedingly comfortable wire-weave spring mattress, sling by a species of

metal triangle from the ceiling. I find that there be three of these

gallery shaft situate in wing of the building, and capable of

comfortably accommodate and bed between five and six hundred

person. one of those gigantic range of cabin, dedicate to the late

Admiral Hope, strike I as exceedingly handsome and curious. the low

berth here be devoted to the mate and captain. they be large, airy,

superbly ventilate; but these be the characteristic of all the

cabin. at one end of this fine division be a marble tablet inscribe to

Admiral Hope, with handsomely carve coloured flag on either side. to

see these cabin, the manner in which they be poise one above another,

the stair lead up to they, the delicate tracery of the platform,

and observe the seaman come out of their room and descend the

step fifty and sixty foot above your head, be to get a new theory of

human existence. I never see anything more comfortable, more clever,

more strange. I mount to one of these gallery with the manager, and

see a cabin door open put my head in. the place be in gloom, and I

be about to withdraw, when to my astonishment I observe what look

like two little half-moon glimmering in the dusk. I stare, and be

amazed to find a negro lying upon a chest, reading. I see the white of

his eye the moment he roll they up to look at I, but the rest of he

be black be not to be discern at once. I ask he what he be

read.



“the Bible,” say he, show the book.



he be newly arrive at Hull from barbadoe, he say, and have come to

London to look for a berth as steward aboard a ship bind to the West

Indies. he be a handsomely speak, well-mannered young fellow,

pronounce his word with the finish of a man of culture.



we next visit the dining-room. this be a great department with row

of table stretch along it, all cover with white linen and

hospitably furnish with good glass and cutlery. at a large centre

heating contrivance stand a carver flourish an immense knife over a

big pile of joint of roast beef. Sirloins, rib, topsides, be mixed

up, but the manager say that do not matter, as there would be little

enough to be see of they presently. a number of waiter run in and out,

set dish of potato, vegetable, pudding, bowl of soup, and

such matter, on the table, and it need nothing but a love cup and a

flourish of trumpet to make the thing look like a civic feast.

presently a bell be beat, the seaman came tumble in, and in a trice

every table be crowd, and all hand eat their hard.



“there be plenty of independence here, apparently,” say I, look

round at the row of “shell-backs”--and I appreciate the term when I

mark the taut curve of their shoulder--work away with spoon and

knife and fork.



“Independence!” exclaim the manager. “why, no hotel confer more

privilege. we be in reality a club. we be originally call a home,

and have stick to the name; but I think it would be well have we bear

the title of Club, for there be something in the sound of a home that

savour of charity, and charity be a thing most seaman object to.”



“what be your charge?”



“fifteen shilling a week to the man, and eighteen shilling to mate,

who eat in a room to themselves.”



“do you mean to say,” I exclaim, “that you give these man a bedroom

apiece and feed they after the fashion I now see for fifteen shilling a

week?”



“yes,” say he, smile at my surprise. “and we go a little further even

than that; for if a sailor arrive here without clothe or mean, we

dress he and put money in his pocket, and repay ourselves by deduct

the amount, without a farthing of extra charge, from the allotment note

he receive from the ship in which, in numerous instance, we procure

he a berth.”



“and may the man do as they like here?”



“as if they be in their own house. we close at half-past twelve; but

there be a night porter, and a man be admit at any hour.”



“so that, practically, this be nothing but a first-class hotel work at

a cost that enable the very poor seaman to use it?”



“exactly. our sole object be to provide the sailor with a comfortable

home while he be on shore, help he in every way that he will allow, and

so keep he clear of the boarding-house people--the wretched man and

still more wretched woman--who prey upon he, drug he with vile drink,

cruelly rob he, and often turn he adrift with scarcely a stitch on his

back. come, sir; more remain to be see.”



he take I downstairs into a ready-make clothing shop belong to the

Home.



“the tailor in the neighbourhood,” say he, laugh, “more especially

those who pay woman commission to bring sailor to their shop, don’t

love we for this invasion of their right or wrong; for our charge for

clothe be very little above the price they cost we, and a man may get

here for two pound ten a suit he would have to pay eight or nine

guineas for to a boarding-house tailor. I may say the same thing of the

bar we have open. there be some murmur at first among the

director; but, sir, we find lemonade and coffee would not do. they

drive the sailor to the public-house; for the man would have their

glass, and if they could not get it here they would go to low place for

it. Jack must be treat sensibly, as a man with brain. to stop his

grog at sea be one thing, but to put he upon cold water ashore be

merely to drive he to those who live by plunder he. the result of

open a bar here have be to extinguish half the public-house in the

neighbourhood, and you may believe I when I say that our people know

their business too well to suffer any approach to intemperance in this

home.”



“well,” say I, “I come here expect to find a lot of false and

mischievous sentiment mix up in the administration of the place. I see

that Jack’s character be understand among you. you treat he as a

rational man, and he respect you for it. no wonder the same people

return again and again.”



“there be no need for a man to do anything here he do not like,” say

the manager. “we have serious, sober, steady fellow among we; for they

there be prayer morning and evening, and all may attend who will. but

there be no obligation to be present. so at church--yonder it be, close

to the home, you see--we muster a good congregation; but there be no

compulsion. whatever can be do to reclaim those who need it, to help

to set man right, to teach they to lift up their thought, we attempt;

but there be no forcing of religion--nothing to induce hypocrisy on the

one hand, nor to excite aversion on the other. we say, ‘my lad, here

be your opportunity, take they if you will; but take they or leave

they, we wish to do our duty by you, to make your life ashore happy and

comfortable, to keep you to windward of the low and nauseous snare

which be everywhere set about for you, to come between your simplicity

and the act of the miscreant who find their account in your easy-go

nature.’ that be about the amount of our theory,” say the manager;

“and if we be not greatly successful, it be because the job we have set

ourselves to perform be a very, very large one.”



from the dining-room we go to the basement, where I be show a number

of capital bath-room, fit with a plentiful supply of hot and cold

water; a laundry and drying-room; store-room fill with joint of

meat, loaf of bread bake on the establishment, white as milk and of a

flavour that make one think of farmhouse and Mrs. Poyser; sack of

flour, potato, and other thing of that kind; and an immense kitchen,

with a wonderful array of oven and boiler for cook by steam;

everything as polished and bright as a new bell, and not the small

feature anywhere discernible that do not exhibit the complete sign

of anxious and attentive supervision. this well Street building may be

call a home, and in a sense may answer to that character, but in

reality it be nothing but a fine, admirably manage marine hotel or

club, fill with bedroom a good deal more comfortable than many a one

in a hotel that a man have have to pay five or six shilling a night for;

provide liberal meal in the shape of breakfast, dinner, tea, and

supper, and furnish the seaman with all the comfort of a first-rate

club for the extraordinary moderate charge of fifteen shilling a week.

what do such an institution replace--or rather what be it design to

replace? I suppose there be no part of the sailor’s shore doing more

talk about and less understand than the life he lead at the greasy

little boarding-house keep by a crimp or a tailor, or, bad still, by

old woman and abandon daughter. I stand gaze at one of these

house--a break-down bit of a hole, with an evil, swagger look in

the posture of its door, and with dirty, stain white blind in the

window--and think what a wonderful, dreadful book might be make of

the scene that have take place in it. a sailor-man be at my side, and

I fall into a short talk with he.



“do you regularly stop at the club?” I ask.



“yes; it be my home whenever I be in London. I have use it for year,

and so have score of the man you see.”



“a pity all sailor be not equally alive to their own interest,” say

I. “here they be make really comfortable for a few shilling a week;

money be advanced to they, clothe furnish to they at cost price, a

hundred little comfort place within their reach, and friend be at

hand to help they to a berth if they find difficulty in get a ship.”



“perfectly true,” say my companion.



“what attraction beyond the privilege and happiness a residence in this

club-house offer can they discover,” say I, point to the miserable

little boarding-house we confront, “in such a den as that?”



“Most of the man you find in such place be force into they,” reply

the man. “all about here be fill with tout and runner and their

bully. sailor be watch come ashore. they may want to put up at

this home; but the boarding-house runner be at hand to tumble ’em into

cab, drink be give they, the girl--and such girls!--are call in to

help, and if the man be obstinate they be fall upon and beat; and,

to such an extent be this kind of intimidation carry on, that, however

anxious a man may be to rescue a shipmate from the hand of those

rascal, he’ll think twice before he do it, for so sure as he attempt

to interfere and bring a man to this home, so sure be he of be fall

upon and half kill when he’s alone and the night’s come. there’s not a

policeman hereabout but be full of story of such work.”



“and what, pray, be the sailor’s life in the low sort of lodge-house?”



“a vile debauch, as a rule, cause by the temptation thrust upon he. it

would be difficult to make respectable people understand how he’s

rob. I know a man who be bring to one of these den and ask to

‘shout’--that be, to stand a drink all round. he do so, and be make

drunk. next day he be charge for eight ‘shout,’ the people swear

he have order the liquor, and that it be not their fault if he be too

intoxicate to remember. that’s only one sample. abandoned woman be

keep in the pay of the slop tailor to bring seaman to their shop and

press they to buy, and a single purchase at such place be enough to

ruin a poor man. there be, no doubt, respectable boarding-house, but

they be few and far between; the most of they be keep by rascally man

and woman, who, take the sailor as a simple-hearted fellow, fresh from

a spell of salt water, and willing for a bit of a frisk, ply he until

they have peel he, and then kick he out. it be not long ago that a

pencil-scrawl be bring to this home. it have be chuck out of a

lodge-house window by a man to a friend who be pass. it state

that the people of the house have steal all the writer’s clothe, and it

beg the manager to send up a suit that the man might get away.”



“such thing must be know to sailor?” say I.



“ay,” he reply, “and a good deal more.”



“and yet many of they persist in put up at those haunt?”



“I cann’t account for it,” say he. “here’s such a chance as any

gentleman with plenty of money in his pocket might be glad to take; and

yet there be sailor who’ll carry their bag or chest to the

lodging-house, as certainly know that they be go there to be

rob of all they have as that their foot be upon dry ground.”



I have only venture to write down a very little part of what I be tell

about these lodge-house. I will not pretend to be ignorant of much of

the inner life of those place; but I own that some of the story

relate to I fill I with horror and astonishment that such deed

should still be do in this enlightened age of marine progress. but be

it not strange that so truly valuable an institution as this Well Street

Home, which count bishop, marquise, admiral, and captain in

abundance among its director, should be deliberately neglect, and

even view hostilely, by the Board of Trade, whose effort to promote

the interest of the sailor it help to a degree no one would credit

without close and careful investigation of its theory and practice? why,

for instance, should the Board of Trade decline to licence a

shipping-master in connection with this home? surely the directorate

should abundantly guarantee the character of the duty such an official

would discharge. what conceivable object can the Board of Trade have in

object to the Home endeavouring, by legal mean, to obtain employment

on board ship for the numerous highly respectable man who use the

institution, and who must often want help to obtain a berth? the home

ask for no State help; it be self-support; it have extinguish a

number of low public-house and crimp’ haunt in its neighbourhood; it

be do a great work; and no man who value the sailor can read the

list of gentleman whose name be associate with it without an emotion

of gratitude to they for the generous, wise and humane part they be

play. surely it be the duty of the State to co-operate with the

endeavour which the working of this Home exemplifie, and to omit

nothing that may tend to lighten the labour of its exemplary official

and advance the truly national purpose for which it be originally

establish.









_the peril of HUMANITY._





“Sir,” say a middle-aged master of a merchantman to I a few day

since, lay down his pipe in order to grope with both hand at once in

his waistcoat-pocket, “I should very much like,” say he, look now

at one hand and now at another as he produce a number of odd and end

before light upon the thing he want, “to have your opinion upon

some document which I cut out of a morning newspaper, and must have

stow away somewhere with such uncommon carefulness, that dash my wig

if I know where I’ve put ’em!”



I wait whilst he grope and slap himself, and explore a

weather-beat pocket-book. finally return again to his waistcoat, he

produce with an air of triumph three newspaper cutting, which, after

put on a pair of spectacle to read they first himself, he hand to

I one at a time.



one be head “thank.” the writer say that he consider it a portion

of his duty to publicly express his deep gratitude and that of his

survive shipmate to Captain Townshend and the crew of the barque _M.

J. Foley_, “who not only rescue we from a miserable death by frost and

starvation, but do everything in their power, by the kind possible

treatment and self-sacrifice, to mitigate our intense suffering and

supply our many want.” the writer add that nautical man would fully

appreciate the meaning of the addition of nineteen person to a small

crew in the winter-time; “but in this case my man be well feed, and we

be sorry to be the mean of every one be put on a limited supply of

water.” the writer of this letter, brimful of honest, sailorly thank,

sign himself, “Abraham Evans, chief officer of the late _Bath City_

(s).”



my friend the shipmaster keep his gaze attentively fix upon I whilst

I read this newspaper extract, and on my put it down call out,

“kindly now cast your eye over this document,” and hand I a second

cut.



this be head “_elba_, brig,” and be a request to be allow to thank

Captain Jacob Backer, of the norwegian barque _sarpen_, for rescue the

eight man who sign the letter “from our water-log vessel, when only

2 lbs. of putrid meat stand between we and starvation. he give we food,

clothing, medicine, and every attendance, and be most ably second by

his kind-hearted crew; and in the seven day we be on board his vessel

he make we in a great measure forget the privation we have undergo.”



the third extract be of a similar character, sign by three survivor

of a crew of fourteen soul.



“well, sir,” say the shipmaster, as I hand he back the third and

last cutting, “what do you think of these document?”



I reply that they be expression of gratitude honourable to the

save and to the saver, and that it be a pity such illustration of

the humanity and gratitude of seaman do not obtain more publicity than

be generally give they, as not only be there nothing nobler in the

world than the marine story which the letter he have give I to read

touchingly testify to, but that the interest of the sailor could never

be well serve than by landsman again and again dwell upon the

bitter peril of his vocation, and upon the score of illustration of

the magnanimity and generosity of his simple heart.



my friend the shipmaster listen to I very attentively, as though I

have give he a new view of the subject; but, shake his head suddenly,

as if to clear his mind of all matter that be not in it before, he

say, “ay, it may be as you observe, and I’m not the man to tell you

that sailor be likely to get more than what they ought to want. the

point’s this: if it’s a beautiful thing to read such piece of gratitude

as these document contain, how much more beautiful would the reading of

they be if it be to be know what impediment, that have grow up like

mangrove bush from the lack of a proper christian civilization to cut

’em down, a ship’s captain have to contend with in order to gratify his

instinct as a man of feeling and compassion. it’s all very well,” say

he, strike a match and hold a flame in the hollow of his hand as

though a stiff breeze be blow, “for landsman to read those letter

of thank and to feel touch, and to talk of the generosity of sailor

and the like. why--since the law which govern folk be make ashore,

and not at sea--why, after they’ve do wipe their eye over the

humble thank poor sailor give to they who save their life, don’t they

turn to and give a hand to the cause of humanity on the ocean by let

captain know that the law of the british nation, anyway--leave other

country out--will never let a man who do a noble act suffer for it

as much, ay, and sometimes more than if he do a wrong? you hear of

ship pass vessel in distress--take no notice--push on, as if

in a hurry to get out of sight. there be nothing in the marine report

which set my tooth more on edge than those yarn--nothing! but I’m

master of a ship; I know the duty and responsibility of that

position. I’ve try to do good, have haul some fellow-mortal out of

the very jaw of death, and have be so make to suffer for my humanity

that when I think of it there come into my mind a bitterness that make

I curse the ill-luck which drive I into the track of the sink ship

and her perish crew. these be strong word, but if I don’t justify

they you shall force I to eat ’em. give I your attention for five

minute. I’ll try not to keep you long; and if I should lose my temper

and talk a bit strong than you may think there’s need for, take no

notice, but just quietly go on listening till I’ve do; and then,

should I fall a-swearing, maybe I’ll have get you into a frame of mind

fit to join I.



“in the middle of last October my ship sail from a certain

port--there’s no need to give any name--in ballast, bind on a voyage

across the Atlantic. the weather be promise enough for three or four

day after we get away; moderate, north-easterly wind which, crank as

we be, enable we to carry a fore-topmast stud-sail, and we drive

along prettily enough, nothing happen to call for remark. but this

sort of thing be too good to last; accordingly, at midnight or

thereabout on the fifth day of sailing, I be rouse by the mate, and,

hurry on deck, find half a gale of wind blow, everything in

confusion, vessel almost on her beam end, everything let go, and as

much shindy aloft as would furnish out noise for a battle-field. it be

a squall with a storm behind it. however, bit by bit we manage to roll

up the canvas and save our spar, and when daylight break we find

ourselves under a low maintop-sail, tumble upon as savage a sea as

be ever roll up in a few hour by a gale in the Atlantic.



“this be the beginning of a deal of delay. the gale keep we humbug

about in one place--allow for that lee drift which you’ll expect of a

ship in ballast--for hard upon a week: then well weather come. we

shake out reef, mast-head the yard, and crawl a trifle to

wind’ard; but the slant be a short one; another gale come along and

last three day; and so it go on, sometimes fine and most often

foul, until at the end of thirty-six day we find ourselves a good deal

close to Europe than we be to America.



“well, sir, the thirty-seventh day prove moderate; a breeze from the

W.N.W., a heavy swell run to show that either a gale have be

blow or be come, and pretty clear weather, with a little glimmer

of sunshine now and again stream through the cloud-rift; enough to

improve our spirit. I come on deck at half-past seven, and be take a

look at the weather and wonder if the swell that be make the ship

roll like an empty cask be to signify more bother, when I be hail by

the mate, who sing out that there be a dark object upon the water, a

point on the lee bow. I take the glass and make out the hull of a

totally dismantled vessel--apparently a barque, but all that be leave of

her mast be three stump barely show above her topgallant

bulwark. she be water-log--like a pancake on the swell that hide she

with every send; and after take another look at she, and not doubt

from her appearance that she be abandon, I put the glass down,

waterlogge vessel be by no mean rare object in the North

Atlantic.



“we be swarm along over the swell at about three to four knot an

hour, and as we should pass the hulk pretty close to windward, I

reckon that if there be any poor miserable creature aboard she we

be bind to see he as we drive by. however, I have scarcely put the

glass down five minute, and be stand look over the taffrail,

when the mate again hail I, and on my go to where he stand peer

through the telescope, he put the glass into my hand and tell I to look

yonder, for there be a boat full of man, head directly for we. I

look, and sure enough see a whole boat-load of human being lift

and fall and come towards we. it be more like an apparition than a

real thing, for when I examine the wreck again I could not conceive how

such a number of man have manage to keep by a hull which offer they no

refuge aloft, and over whose deck the water roll in shine masse,

as she swing into the hollow.



“as the boat approach, we back the mainyard, and lie wait for

she to come alongside. by this time I could make out no less than

fourteen man, and a sad freight of human being I never want to see

again. their white face, their stream clothe, their gaunt, hollow

look, the languid movement of the oar, and, above all, the manner in

which those who row keep their face turn towards we upon their

shoulder, as if they fear we should vanish if they do not keep their

eye fix upon we, be a sight the most iron-hearted man could not have

view without pain and grief. we hove they the end of a rope, and

drag the boat alongside; and I want no well assurance of the

character of their suffering and of the lamentable condition they be

then in than their slow, weak motion as they catch the line, and get

their oar in and stand up. one by one we lift or help they over the

side--fourteen of they, sir. some of they be too weak to answer our

question. my man take the seaman forward, hold they up as they

walk, for they could scarcely use their limb; and I carry the

captain and the two mate into the cabin, where we furnish they with

food and dry clothing, and then get they to bed.



“all this while we remain hove to with the wreck bear about a mile

distant from we on our lee bow. my own crew consist of eleven hand

only, and the job of help the rescue man forward have give they work

enough until the poor fellow be below. I go on deck, and find the

mate singe out to the hand to swing the main-topsail and get way upon

the ship. I stand look on, full of thought. presently the sail be

trim, and I call the mate over to I.



“‘do you know,’ say I, ‘that we have be very nearly forty day at

sea?’



“‘ay, sir,’ he answer, ‘I know it only too well.’



“‘we’re provision, Mr. ----,’ say I, give he his name, ‘for one

hundred and ten day, count for our crew only. but if you add

fourteen to eleven you get twenty-five, and that’s the number of people

our provision must now serve for.’



“he grow very thoughtful, and take a long look round at the weather.



“‘I fear,’ continue I, ‘that it will merely be tempt Providence to

pursue our voyage with all these extra man aboard in the face of the

ill-luck that’s dog we for near upon forty day. if we’re to make no

more headway than we’ve already do in the same time, I’m afraid,’

say I, point to the wreck that be slowly draw abeam of we, ‘we

shall be as badly off here as if we turn to and ship ourselves

aboard yonder hulk.’



“‘that’ll be about it, sir,’ say he. ‘the harness cask, to say nothing

of the scuttle-butt, be much too small for fourteen extra hand, unless

we’re to get a gale of wind astern of we.’



“‘which we’ve get no right to expect,’ I answer.



“however, before I decide I think I’d first take counsel with the

captain we have rescue, and, on his wake up much refresh in the

afternoon, I put my position before he, and ask he for his opinion.

he never hesitate when he hear how long we have be at sea and for how

many day we have be provision. but I’m not sure that even his advice

would have settle my resolution--for what can be more try than to

have to give up and go back, after beat about and toil to get

across for over a month?--had it not that same evening breeze up ahead

with a stormy appearance. it be just as if the weather say, ‘no, you

don’t.’ I take a look, listen a moment or two at the man singe out

as they clew up the topgallant-sail, and then tell the mate to get

his helm over and head the ship for the homeward passage.



“now, sir, though it be disagreeable enough to have to go back after

consume so much time in get forward, I be a good deal comfort

by reflect upon the cause that be send I home. it be a cheerful

thing, likewise, to see the man who have come aboard half-dead gradually

recover their health and spirit, and testify their gratefulness

by not only lend a hand with a will, but by strive to take all the

work they could come at out of the hand of my crew. besides, I will

frankly own to you, sir, that I be buoy up by the belief that any

money difficulty that must follow my useless trip into the

Atlantic--useless, I mean, in the commercial sense of that word--would

be in some degree meet by the owner of the craft whose people I have

save, and if not by they, then by the ‘authorities’--a sort of strange

people who come into one’s head when one fall into an expecting mood,

and stop there as if they be real and have all the disposition and

power you fancy of ’em, though to my mind there’s no illusion to equal

’em, and ne’er a word in the english dictionary that make a man fiercer

to come across after he’s get, by write letter and call, to find

out the true meaning of it.



“the near port be a french port, and there we arrive after a pretty

quick run, and land the rescued man, of whom I’ll say this--that their

gratitude be such, that if they could have turn their body into

gold so that we could have make sovereign out of their flesh they’d

have do it cheerfully. well, sir, after I arrive in England, the

first thing I do be to represent what I have do to the owner of the

barque whose crew I have save. I tell they that I have be oblige to

abandon my voyage in consequence of the assistance I have render, and

that by so do I have not only lose a voyage, but consume the whole of

my store. no notice be take of I; and when I complain to a friend

who know a good deal about the law, he say the wonder would have be

if any notice _have_ be take, as I have no claim whatever on the owner

of the barque for the rescue of the crew.



“‘but,’ say I, ‘d’ye mean to tell I that there’s no Act of Parliament,

no statute, no sort of general understanding, no kind of provision--call

it by what name you will--to protect a man from suffer heavily in

his pocket because he go out of his way to save fourteen human life?’



“‘no,’ say he.



“‘and must I,’ say I, ‘be compel to pay off my crew--which I’ve

do--and ship another--also do--and accept a twopenny freight to the

West Indies to enable I to reprovision my ship--all which I’ve have to

do--with never a living being in this whole wide world make responsible,

either as the manager of a fund set apart for such case, or as the

owner of the wreck vessel, or as the British Government itself, whose

business it should be to encourage act of humanity show to those

sailor it’s always brag about and leisurely look after, for the

loss I’ve be put to?’



“‘well,’ say he, ‘on reflection I think ye might drop a line to the

“authorities”’--you know who he mean, sir: ‘there’s a fund call the

Mercantile Marine Fund, out of which, I think--mind, I only think--the

“authority” may, if they think fit, pay a certain sum, whatever it be,

in satisfaction of salvage where nothing more valuable have be save

than fourteen human life.’



“‘well,’ say I, ‘I’ll write to they;’ and so I do. and what do you

think be the result?”



“I cannot imagine,” I reply.



“I get no answer,” say he. “I get no answer,” he repeat,

passionately; “and there’s no more chance of my get an answer than

there be of----” he pause, and add, “I be go to say than there be

of my stopping to pick up more shipwreck mariner--but God forgive I

for the fancy! it’s not in my head, sir. but, put all sentiment

aside, wouldn’t you consider mine a hard case? and be my loss make the

light to I because I be ask to reflect whether I _ought_ to expect

the authority, or the owner, or anybody else to pay I for save

fourteen man from a dreadful death? here, sir, you have one of the

difficulty--one of the hundred difficulty--master-mariner have to

contend with, as little know to or understand by landsman as Jack

comprehend the business of an attorney. it’s not for I to suggest what

should be do. but when next you hear of shipwreck sailor be

abandon by a pass ship, don’t be too quick to condemn the captain

as a coward and a villain for leave his fellow-being to perish

miserably, but say to yourself, ‘his heart be with they, and he’d have

save they if he have dare; but british civilization say, “whatever you

do you do at your own risk. if no harm befall you, good and well; you

shall pass by and nothing more be say or hear of your act; but if you

lose money, don’t look to _I_,” say british civilization, “for the

only answer you’ll get will be that you’re an impudent fellow to expect

to recover any loss you incur in the service of humanity;”’ and so the

skipper, know this to be true, sail away, hold that his wife and

child at home must not be beggar that a perish crew may be

rescue.”



thus speak, my friend the shipmaster rise abruptly from his chair,

pull his hat down to his ear, and impetuously wish I good day,

leave I to lapse into a very brown, I may almost say a very black,

study.









_SMACK APPRENTICES._





“I don’t know what country he hail from, I’m sure; but, thank the

Lord, he ben’t an Englishman,” say a smacksman, in the most fervent

manner, to I the other day, speak of Osmond Otto Brand, who be

execute on the 23rd of May for the murder of an apprentice name

William Papper or Pepper. at any other time than this I believe the

story of that miscreant’s barbarity would have deeply stir the public

mind; but of late days[C] murder have become a common thing, much talk

of and freely practise. People’s capacity of be horrify gets

dull by iteration of shocking news, and the significance of any one

item in a blood-red catalogue lose value as a particular impression.

whatever effect, however, may have be produce by Brand’s crime on the

lay mind outside the district where the murderer and his victim be

know, there can be no doubt of the impression it have make upon

smack-owner and smacksman all round the coast. there be scarcely a

fisherman who have not the horrible story off by heart, who do not view

the atrocious cruelty practise by the foreign smack-master as a foul

disgrace to the fishing industry, and who do not indignantly lament

that the man who help Brand to slowly kill the miserable apprentice

be not hang along with their skipper. one result of Brand’s crime

be to cause some question to be ask in the House of Commons. there

be also some talk about the whole subject of fishing apprentice be

consider by Government. if it be to be deal with it will be as well,

perhaps, first to give the hull murder time to drop out of memory. to

adopt it as a text for legislation would assuredly be to mislead

honourable member who vote without look very deep into the matter

on which their opinion be challenge. if the condition of the smack

apprentice be an improvable feature of current life, it be not so merely

because Brand slowly torture Pepper to death. we must shelve all

thought of that murder, and ask question without the least reference

to it. what be the life of a fishing apprentice at sea? what be it

ashore? how be he feed and clothe? what be the nature of his relation

with the owner’ interest? what be the average character of the man

with whom he be throw? these and other question I will endeavour to

answer from inquiry I have make into the inner or hidden part of the

life of a body of lad of whom the public know less than they know of

any other kind of seafare people.



I once write an account of a voyage in a smack in the North Sea. one

such journey be enough for a lifetime, and the recollection of it make

I here declare--and I be sure there be not a sailor living who will

contradict I--that of all the several form of seafare life there be

absolutely none comparable in severity, exposure, hardship, and stern

peril to that of the smacksman. his vessel be a small one; his cabin a

little darksome hole; his work hour be full of harsh toil; he have

to give battle to the wild weather, to struggle on for bread through

storm and snow and frost, through the long blackness of the howling

winter’s night, through the grey wilderness of a foaming ocean sweep by

wind as pitiless as the hand of death. no legislation can alter these

condition of his life. Philanthropy will have its cod and sole and

turbot. the fish must be catch, but catch in such a manner that those

who shoot their trawl for they catch other thing besides--a wild

roughness of bearing, a defiance of civilized instinct, a sense of

outlaw and neglect life that bring with it a fix conviction of

social immunity. “I’m a fisherman myself, sir,” a man once say to I;

“and I’ll allow that there be many well-mannered, sober, steady man

among we; but, take we all round, you’ll not find a coarser set of

human being in the world; and, if you want to know the reason, you’ve

only get to look at yonder smack, head away into the North Sea,

where, maybe, she’ll be heave and toss about for week, with ne’er

a proper influence in the shape of book or company for the man to come

at.”



take now the fishing apprentice. he come to this severe, coarse life,

himself most often of the coarse. he be fresh from a reformatory, from

a union, or, bad still, from the gutter. his associate be man who

be themselves apprentice; lad who come one know not from where, the

refuse of the street manufacture into marine object by the owner’s

boot, and breech, and coat; and hammer into sprawling, unwieldy

smacksman by the hard blow of their calling. they know all about

dandy-bridle and trawl-warps; but they do not know how to read, and

they do not know how to write, and they do not know how to think. their

home be the public-house when ashore, and they take the moral of that

sort of home to sea with they. the apprentice come among they, get

knock about, pick up their oath and their shore theory, and

imitate they masterfully enough to be able to hand on their condition

with an add flourish, when he be out of his time and have boy under

he to swear at. now, what be legislation go to do here? you have the

rough life in the world; the rough lad in the world recruit its

rank. what be to be do, short of what a few philanthropist be

endeavour to do, to prevent they from be the roughest man in the

world? I cannot see that the smack-owner be to blame. they must have

apprentice. they will take the good of such boy as they can get; and

it be really carry idealism too high to expect that these man, who

have to work hard themselves, who have to be down among their vessel,

see that their man do not run away, that they be properly engage in

prepare for the voyage, and so forth,--I say you cannot expect that

these man, who come home of a night tired out, should turn schoolmaster

and parson to their apprentice, and set they to moral job, when they

be ready to abscond--with their master’s clothe also--if they be not

allow their evening out after be at work all day up to their knee

in mud, scrub the vessel’s bottom. no one with any knowledge of the

smack-owner’s calling, of his hardly earn money, of his risk and

anxiety, will envy he. boy be boy all the world over, and that

smack-boy should be peculiarly troublesome be not hard to account for

on reference to their antecedent--that is, the antecedent of most of

they. “the law have come between we and the boy,” be say to I, “and

make our case hard than it be. we have no remedy now. time be when

we could send a constable after a lad when he be off; but the law have

stop that. we have get to wait a couple of day, and then apply for a

warrant, by which time the boy’s t’other end of England. take the case

of a vessel about to start. a boy refuse to turn to. I’m on the spot,

and call a policeman, and in his presence repeat the order. boy still

refuse. Policeman then walk he off afore a magistrate, who fine he,

and I have to pay the fine if I want to get the vessel to sea; for if I

don’t pay then the boy’s lock up, and the vessel detain two or three

day whilst I’m seek another boy.” the hardship be clear enough,

though I for one should be heartily sorry to see it rectify by a

return to the old and brutal system of lock up lad in gaol at the

will of the smack-owner. let I briefly place the case of the owner

before you with regard to their apprentice. to begin with, the lad

come from all part, as I have say, and be bind apprentice for term

of three, five, or six year. if bind by institution such as

workhouse, reformatory, and the like, a certain sum of money be pay

with they--in some case £10, enough to purchase an outfit. I ask a

smack-owner what he reckon to be the average yearly cost of a lad’s

clothe, and he say £8. “a pair of sea-boot alone,” say he, “cost £1

16s.” Boys, however, when they first go to sea do not get sea-boot, but

“blucher,” the cost of which be about 12s. a pair. when the lad be in

harbour the smack-owner have to house they. in many instance they sleep

in his own house, or in lodging provide for they. if they do not take

their meal with their master they live as well; dining from the same

joint and get much the same fare as he have for tea and breakfast.

there may be exception to this; but it be a practice so general that it

may be take as a rule. when ashore the owner also keep the boy

furnish with a little pocket-money. the lad, for instance, who act as

cook at sea get, when in harbour, 6_d._ a night, the deck-boy 9d., and

the third-hand apprentice 1_s._ these payment be make during what be

term “settling time;” but, in addition to this money, the owner give

they the small fish catch during the voyage, call “stocker-bait,” the

produce of which yield each lad an average sum of 1_s._ a week all the

year round. whilst in port the apprentice’s work mainly consist in

scrub the vessel’s bottom, touch she up with the paint-brush,

prepare net for the next voyage, etc. at sea the less duty be

assign he. suppose a smack carry three boy; the young will

probably act as cook. when the net be hove up his post be in the hold,

where he coil away the trawl-warp, which do he return to his

cooking. the deck-boy’s post be on deck when the man be below take

their meal. he steer the vessel in the morning until noon or 12.30;

then get his dinner, and turn in. the duty of a third-hand apprentice

be that of a man. he be commonly within two year of his time; and,

though he be still an apprentice, he be generally treat as a

well-season and fully developed smacksman. talk recently with a

body of smack-boy, I ask they what sort of grub they get aboard.



“good enough, master.”



“what do you have for breakfast?” say I.



“well, we have the choice of tea or coffee or cocoa; we have roast fish

and butter and soft tack--as long as it’ll last--and then we have

biscuit.”



“and what do you get for dinner?”



“why, fresh and corned beef for a spell; and when that’s eat up we have

fish, and suet pudden, and cabbage--as long as it last--and carrot

and parsnip when they’re in, and ’tatie.”



“and your tea?”



“Tea’s the same as breakfast.”



“do you get any supper?”



“ay, master; ’twixt eleven and one, ’cord as the watch be call, we

have cheese and pickle and biled fish, or if there’s any cold meat leave

we have that.”



when it be consider where these lad come from, this fare be scarcely

of a kind to justify they in grumble and run away. indeed, of all

seafarer, smacksman live the good. when Jack be gnaw upon a piece of

junk, and knock his biscuit upon the deck to get the worm out of it,

the fisherman be regale himself with the good of the produce of his

trawl, or fatten himself on hearty fresh beef and--if it be

Sunday--on good plum-duff. the apprentice fare just the same. and even

in other way he be well off than if he be in a ship’s forecastle;

as, for instance, in the matter of clothe, the owner be oblige to

keep he well furnish in that respect, and to equip he with garment

a hundredfold warm and well than those which most sailor take to

sea with they. the whole truth be, so far as I can judge, the comfort

and happiness and prospect of the smack apprentice depend upon his own

conduct. owner, like all other employer of labour, want the good hand

they can get, and smacksman be glad to have smart and willing lad

along with they. it be a rough life--the whole marine call be a rough

life, and there be none rough than a fisherman’s. if a boy be dull and

slow, obstinate and sulky, he will be shove and kick about, and that

would be his lot in any ship he go aboard of; but if a boy be willing,

do his good, lend a hand cheerfully, and be a steady lad, then he

will be well treat, the man will like he, the owner favour he, and

before long he will find himself in command. he have inducement to

persevere and behave well such as no other ship-boy get that I know of;

for if he have serve his time honestly, and show such promise as the

smack-owner want to see, then, when he be out of his time, he be

furnish with £20 worth of clothe, and a sovereign or two for his

pocket. I be aware that all smack-owner be not so liberal, and I have

hear of some man send their apprentice, when out of their time,

adrift in the clothe they stand up in, and force they to seek work

from other master. but the rule be to treat a good lad liberally when

out of his time.



whoever have examine into the fishing industry must be well aware that

smack-owner have substantial cause of grievance in respect of their

treatment by their apprentice. an owner tell I that a boy come to he

for a berth; the lad be in rag and starving, and so filthy that the

owner would not send he to his house where his apprentice be. he

walk with he to the Sailor’s home, have he bathe and scrub and

fed, pay nearly a pound for he for seven day at the home, purchase

for he some clothe that cost over £2, and then on the morning of the

day on which the vessel be to sail the boy run away, and the smack have

to be detain until another lad could be ship. instance of such

behaviour be numerous, and might really account for, if they should not

justify, a very much harsh discipline and sterner kind of treatment

than I have be able to discover. for always let we remember that the

smack-owner have, as a rule, be a fisherman himself, go through the

mill, suffer all the hardship of the life, and, though ashore, have to

work hard for his living than ever he do when at sea. he be in this

position, that he be only able to insure for total loss. he belong to a

club whose member subscribe in proportion to the number of vessel they

severally enter. “only yesterday,” say a smack-owner, “one of my

vessel come in; she have lose fifteen fathom of warp, two main-bridle,

dandy-bridle, trawl-warp tackle, two trawl-head, a trawl-beam, a

ground-rope, mortice, head-line, and other gear. I have to bear all

that. there be Mr. ----. in one night of storm his loss amount, in

insurance of other vessel which have founder, to £245, together with

eight set of gear, value at £60 a set.” it be a vocation full of risk;

score of man may be beggar by a gale; and, see the important part

that smack apprentice play in the fishing interest, it be reasonable

that we should survey the question from every point of view, before

hastily form conclusion on the basis of such an incident as that of

the recent hull atrocity. of course, there be savage and bully among

smacksman, as there be the whole wide world over, whether among

landgoer or seafarer. but whatever might have be the state of thing

in former day, I do not believe that, at the present time, there be

half the ill usage to be find aboard smack that I know exist at sea

in other kind of vessel. the crime of the murderer Brand necessarily

give a malignant colour to every smack-master’s report of having

lose a boy by drown whilst at sea; but the old salt maxim, “a

fisherman’s walk, three step and overboard,” should go a long way in

explanation of many of the disaster that befall smack-boy. an accident

aboard a smack happen in a breath. a lad dip over the side for a

bucket of water; the vessel be sail fast, the bucket pull the lad

over the rail, and he be astern and drown before the fellow at the

tiller can sing out. or a boy go to look over the stern to see the

white water run away; the boom jibe and fling he into the sea. I

should very gravely question whether a deliberate murder could be do

without some one of the man report it. no doubt black deed have be

perpetrate in fishing-smack. for instance, a story be tell of a lad

who be fry some fish; through his neglect the fish be burn,

whereupon the skipper, smell the fume, bundle below, seize the

boy’s hand, and thrust they into the boiling fat. the instant he be

release, the boy rush on deck and fling himself overboard, and be

drown. one might conceive of a man hate another and jog he into

the sea on a dark night. but the statistic of loss of life among

smacksman and smack apprentice at sea must reduce such dreadful

possibility to a very small number; and of that small number it be

rare indeed to find one to which any well basis can be furnish than

suspicion. so far as the professional life of the smack apprentice

go--his treatment at sea, his food, his clothe, and the like--it be

difficult to guess, have regard to the unavoidable roughness and

hardship of his calling, how his position be to be improve. you cannot

make a drawing-room of a smack; there will be always hard work and hard

word where there be hard weather; and there be not much hope of

polished air and genteel behaviour amongst a race of man who sleep in

hole at which a blackbeetle might stand aghast, and who be box up

for many month together in the year in a bit of a fabric whose

forecastle be full of raffle, and whose hold be full of dead fish. but

ashore no doubt something may be do for the lad to advance they

morally, and make real man of they when they come to be man. it would be

well if there be a few more smack-boy’ home than there be. there

be one in Ramsgate the theory of which be exceedingly good. the manager

write to a school or reformatory for boy, the condition of acceptance

be that the lad be healthy and strong and of good character; also

that enough money be pay down to furnish each youth with a fishing

outfit and a Sunday suit. the boy be get together in this fashion,

the smack-owner be ask to take apprentice from they. this many of

they do, I believe, on the understanding that the boy lodge at the home

when in port at a cost of 2_s._ a day each. by this mean the lad be

bring under a certain moral influence. they be watch over by the

clergy associate with the home, attend service in a chapel that adjoin

the building, and be provide with the mean of harmlessly amusing

themselves in the evening. the sole objection be--and it be a

commercial one--the expensiveness of the arrangement to smack-owner who

have several apprentice. “if I have but one apprentice,” say an owner,

“2_s._ a day would be cheap enough; but I have ten, who at 2_s._ a day

per boy would be a good deal dearer to I at the home than I find they

in my own house.” this be a point I will not deal with, but in all other

respect I know of nothing that can be advance against smack-boy’

home. Smacksmen make fine sailor; the navy and the merchant service

ought to have no well recruiting field than the british fishery, and

therefore something of imperial significance should enter into

consideration of the smack-boy’s moral and material welfare. you be not

go to make he a refined person, but you can teach he to write and

read, to have a reverence for God, to think of himself as a responsible

be. an early training of this kind will not impair his hardiness, but

it will put he high than he be as a human creature. the low order

of smack-owner let he run loose of a night when he be ashore, he be

quite uncared for, and in the prison day he pass a good deal more of

his time in gaol than at sea. “make every allowance for second or

third conviction,” say the Rev. J. E. Brennan, of Ramsgate, write in

1878, “we be not far from the truth in state that 50 per cent. of

these boy go to prison during some part of their service.” these

incessant punishment naturally lead people to infer the bad of the

fishing life. “think what a calling it must be,” they would say, “when

boy actually beg to be send to prison rather than on board these

smack.” but Mr. Brennan justly, I think, attribute the lad’ defection

to their neglect condition, to the absence of all suitable

guardianship. “the scene of drunkenness and sin,” he write, “of which

some of these lad be cognizant I will not describe. let it suffice to

say that many a pure-minded boy have in a few month become utterly

corrupt, and his character, it may be, utterly ruin for ever.” he be

writing of the boy of one town; but his remark be equally, and even

more, applicable to such place as Grimsby and Hull and Yarmouth. here,

then, be the real evil. it be not that the boy be maltreat at sea to

any extent outside the proverbial rough usage of the marine life; it be

not that smack-owner--the majority of they certainly--do not feed and

clothe they well, nor that they exact unreasonable share of labour from

they; it be that, when ashore and during the evening, they wander

about, fall into bad company, acquire habit of intemperance, become

unspeakable nuisance to the police, to the inhabitant, and to their

own master, run away, and leave owner in the lurch and practically now

without redress; and so, in a large proportion of instance, end in

become untrustworthy man, worthless sailor, people whom nobody will

employ. smack-owner, as I have say, cannot be expect to look after

the moral of the lad; their hand be full of business, they come home

weary, and, even with the good will in the world, they must lack in

way it would take too much space to explain here the opportunity to

care for the boy as they be care for at a home. what be really want

be a home wherever there be fishing-apprentice, an institution

conduct with something of the self-sacrifice spirit that

characterize the Ramsgate Home; where the boy can be comfortably

house and tend at a small expense to their employer; where they may

be educate and help and reward for their merit as seafaring lad;

and any one truly concerned in the welfare of our mercantile marine, and

who hold that we should neglect no source from which we may derive the

force that keep our nation dominant upon the sea, will believe that the

State could make no wise disbursement than in help in the

establishment of such institution, and contribute to they until they

become self-support.









_GRAVESEND._





I never pass Gravesend without thinking of poor Mrs. Henry Fielding’s

dreadful toothache, and the trouble her husband and the surgeon, “the

well repute operator in Gravesend,” take to persuade the suffering lady

to keep the hollow tooth a little long. to what extent have the old

town change since the author of “Tom Jones” survey it from the deck

of the little old vessel that carry he to Lisbon to die there? much

should have happen in a hundred and thirty year; yet in one respect

Gravesend remain unaltered; it be still, so to speak, the same old

point of nautical departure; ship still “drop” down abreast of it, and

bring up to receive their passenger; and yet it remain the one spot of

english soil which, when leave astern, make you feel that the great

ocean be all before you, and that your voyage have commence indeed. but

be not human nature the same in all age? field’s description of

sit at dinner and be startle by the bowsprit of “a little ship

call a cod smack,” drive in through the cabin window, and his

account of the sea-blessing shower upon each other by the crew of

the two vessel, might stand for a picture of to-day’s river life. “it

be difficult,” he say, “I think, to assign a satisfactory reason why

sailor in general should, of all other, think themselves entirely

discharge from the common band of humanity, and should seem to glory

in the language and behaviour of savage.” his opinion of Jack as a

gentleman be not ill found. but what would he think now if live,

and with the memory in he of his uncouth sea-swab of a skipper (who, to

the delight of posterity, fall upon his knee at last and beg

fielding to forgive he), he should step on board the 4000-ton steamer

that lie abreast of Gravesend, with blue-peter at her masthead, on the

day I happen to find myself in that town?



not much imagination be need, I think, to extinguish the monster

fabric which day after day lie float motionless abreast of

Gravesend, and refurnish the broad and quiver stretch of water with

the marine phantom of olden time. the Indiaman of 500 ton be view

with astonishment at her prodigious dimension as she lie strain at

her hempen cable, the sunshine sparkle in the big window which

embellish her huge quarter-gallery, her stern tower out of the

water like a castle, a wondrous complication of head-board and massive

timber distinguish her bow, long streamer whip from every

masthead, and row of cannon bristling along her tall, weather-toss

side. you have little pink, and snow, and cutter of fifty ton

burden which have make part of a convoy from the West Indies, and have

outweathere the heavy Atlantic surge as bravely as any Cunard liner of

to-day do. here, too, be collier, “ship of great bulk,” Fielding

call they, though there be scarcely a skipper of an old boom-foresail

_Anna Maria_ or _John and Susan_ now afloat who would not hold they

capacious only as long-boat. to appreciate all that the present mean,

you must step back and then look ahead. only the other day I see a

Blackwall liner tow up the river. it be not so very long ago when

that ship would have be think a wonderfully large vessel. handsome

she be, with her paint port and frigate-like look, though her

main-royal mast be uncomfortably stay aft when I see she, and she

want a pretty stem-piece; but as to her size, she seem little more

than a toy as she swam past the line of huge tower iron hull. you

think of the scene of the river as Fielding survey it, then as you

remember it twenty year since, then as you see it now; and your wonder

be, what will the end be should the end ever arrive? what will be the

bulk and vastness of the fabric in the good time come?



“the new dock,” say a waterman to I, point across the river with a

finger like a roll of old parchment, “be to start from yonder p’int,

and end right away down there;” and the sweep of his finger seem to

embrace some league of the opposite low, flat, treeless, and

mud-colour shore. assuredly all that can be give will be want. our

marine giantess be multiply fast than they drown; and it seem

to I that there be something prophetic in my waterman’s finger when he

make the gesture of it to signify mile instead of acre. but many

change must take place and a long time elapse before Gravesend lose

its old distinctive tradition as a point of departure. think of the

thousand of eye which have grow dim as they watch the old town veer

away astern, and of the thousand of heart which have leapt in

transport as from mouth to mouth the cry have go round, “Gravesend be

in sight.” no other place--I be speak, of course, of vessel bind

Thames-wise--give one such a sense of home as this. Jack may have the

english coast in view pretty nearly the whole way from the Isle of Wight

as high as the South Foreland; he may bring up in the down, and have

Deal and Walmer close aboard, and hear the church bell ring, and see

the people walk on the beach; he may take his fill of Ramsgate and

Margate as he round the great headland; but somehow or other it be not

until Gravesend have hove in sight, and he see the shipping abreast of

it, and the river curve into Northfleet Hope, that Jack feel home be

reach at last; that the voyage be as good as over, and that in a few

hour the noble ship that have carry he in safety through storm and

calm, through sunshine and blackness, will be at rest, silent as the

grave--as though, after the long and fitful fever of the deep, she be

sleep well.



these be the gayer thought, for they come with the hurricane-chorus

that break from the forecastle of yonder ship as her crew get the
