anchor, now that the first of the flood have come, and the tug alongside

be already to forge ahead and tauten the hawser. “oh, when we get to the

dockyard gate!” shout the poor fellow gleefully, with as much voice as

a voyage from San Francisco have leave in they; and you think that

to-night there will be some middling salt and tough yarn spin in more

than one grog-shop, whilst already Jack’s most unlovely Nan--as Charles

Dickens only too truthfully describe she--be overhaul her few

penn’orths of finery in anticipation of the treat which be to be get

out of a fund make up of £3 10s. a month.



but Gravesend appeal most from the other side of the picture--the

outward-bind side. I be favour with an immense illustration of this.

a big steamer, with a black-and-white funnel, lie abreast of the

Gravesend pier, with her deck literally choke with emigrant. she

should have sail the day before, I be tell; but the emigrant--mainly

foreigner--have rebel; declare they have be promise a steamer

belong to another company, and refuse to start in the vessel they

be pack aboard of. some one on the Gravesend pier tell I that a

thousand people have be put into she that morning. there be hardly

room for a pin along the bulwark. clustering masse of human head

blacken the rail, as though all the crow in Kent have swoop down

upon that great iron steamship, and be take their ease upon her

side. I have no excuse to board she, but I manage to gather a good idea

of her live freight by take a boat and pull round she. I have see

a very similar class of foreign emigrant in a north-country port, and

have make a short voyage in company with five or six hundred of they, but

that crowd do not impress I as this do. there be something very

pathetic and melancholy in the posture and look of this large

concourse of people, who overhang the water, and gaze, with little of

movement among they, at the shore on either hand. the thought of this

mass of human soul afloat on the deep with nothing between they and

eternity but a thin surface of iron, combine with the speculation as

to the future into which the mind be irresistibly impel; the new

land which await they; the long--perhaps everlaste--separation from

their mother country; the numberless interest they represent; and the

rapid growth of that amazing Western Empire, whose humanizing and

civilize progress be strangely illustrate by the embarkation of this

immense assembly--the freight of a single ship, too!--for its port,

contribute to make the picture of the _Holland_--for that be the name

of the steamer--a truly impressive and memorable one. it be a warm,

sunny afternoon; far down the Hope, trend north-wise athwart

Gravesend Reach, be the white height of Cliffe, sparkle like marble

in the brilliant radiance; the long stretch of water be crowd with

shipping, whose bunt and variously-colour side fill the eye with

colour; Gravesend lie in a heavy mass of group close down to the

water’s edge, with a lumber of huddle house to the right of the new

Falcon Hotel, here and there a window flash back the sunlight, and

the church bell ring a pleasant farewell to a peninsular and oriental

steamer, whose head be be cant towards the north shore by a tug

that she might have a clear road before her engine be set in motion;

whilst, some distance up the river, vessel which have pass Gravesend

twenty minute before be fade upon the blueish haze of smoke from

tall chimney and fog from the marsh, the spar of the Blackwall liner

loom huge and vague above the land which conceal her hull.



the very beauty of the picture furnish an element of melancholy to the

crowded steamship, and the row upon row of face which be all

steadily gaze landward. I watch the Peninsular and oriental steamer

get under way, and contrast she with the emigrant ship. the big

deck-house or saloon of the former, with the two funnel rear out of

it, give she, to my eye, a somewhat heavy look forward; but it be

something to remember to run the eye from she almost unpeopled

deck--nobody to be see but some man in uniform on the bridge, a Lascar

in a turban squatting in the after-awning, hold a little white flag

in his hand, and one or two figure in the forward part of the ship--to

the motionless black hull of the emigrant steamer, teem with life,

and the bulwark literally creep with face. it be a responsible

thing to carry mail, to be answerable for a mass of specie, and for the

life of a number of gentleman and lady; but think of stand on the

deck of a steamer on a dark night, and reflect that under your foot

lie sleep a thousand human being, not count your crew, and that

the very existence of this vast company of fellow-creature depend upon

your vigilance, judgment, skill as a seaman. I believe the sympathy and

wonder of any man who see that crowded vessel and give attention to the

sight, would have go to the captain--to the seaman who be to hold all

those life in his hand, so to speak. who would willingly accept such a

responsibility? and who, find man equal to the discharge of these

enormous trust, would not gladly lend a hand to smooth their path for

they by denounce and demand the removal of whatever unfairly

obstruct and harass they--the action of unjustly-constitute court,

the decision of empiric, and of people who could not tell the

difference between a gin-block and a dead-eye, the iniquity of the

modern ship-building yard, and the hundred small red-tape worry which

make the shipmaster’s life a burden to he ashore?



how much Gravesend be a point of arrival and departure I be remind as

I stand overhang the stone projection and look down on the

landing-step. from a large sailing-ship tow up the river, a

waterman’s boat shoot away and make for the Gravesend pier. in it be a

middle-aged man, bronze with the sun and wind of four month, and

dress in clothe which it scarcely need a tailor to guess be of an

antipodean cut. his luggage be heap about he in the bottom of the

boat. I watch he land, and follow he as he come up the step,

when a rush be make by a little group of people dress in mourning,

and in a breath a woman, toss up her black veil, be in his arm, and

sob on his shoulder. those sombre garment throw a shadow upon the

happiness of this meeting; but still, he have come back, he be well, and

by-and-by the dead, never to be forget, let we hope, would be bury

indeed, and the live heart reassert itself. the waterman seem to

know when that ship have leave her port on the other side of the world,

and so I find that this man have be four month in make his way to

England. and how much long have he be absent? but then think of the

glory of the green tree and fragrant beauty of our English May to

this traveller fresh from one hundred and twenty day of salt water!

figure the flavour he will find in a cut from a prime sirloin! the

sweetness of “soft tack”--ay, even such bread as be now bake--after the

bilious little bit of dough manufacture in the galley by the baker,

and send aft under the satirical title of “roll!”



Scarcely have the sunburnt man and his friend disappear, when there

come a little figure that I could not view without lively concern and

compassion. this be a small midshipman, resplendent in the new of

uniform. the button glitter on his tiny jacket, and the brand-new

badge on his cap shine like a freshly mint sovereign. there lie his

ship a short way down the stream--a good-look iron vessel, very long

and very narrow, without an inch of that “swell of the side” one love

to see, as I have take notice when she slew on her heel to the first

of the flood and give we a view of herself end on, with double

topgallant yard, skysail pole, and the capacity for an immense spread

of low cloth. the poor little chap take a long squint at his new

home, and then a peep at the lady alongside of he, who, from the strong

family likeness between they, I reckon at once to be his mamma. she

have be cry, her eye be red, but she look at her youngster with

a kind of quivering smile now, for it would clearly not do to capsize

his sensibility at this most trying point. he have insist upon go

to sea, no doubt, much against poor mamma’s will. he have fine notion of

the marine call, I dare say, all acquire by day and night of study

of nautical romance; and here he be ready to sail away, handsomely

brass bind, mamma red-eyed at his side, bravely fight with her

heart. hundred of man have go through this, and something betwixt a

laugh and a sigh will come from they when they think of this little

brass bounder and look back to their first voyage. what idea have the

young fellow form of the life? but, alas! what mariner have not allow

his boyhood to gull he in the same way? “there be,” say Dana, “a

witchery in the sea, its song and story, and in the mere sight of a

ship and the sailor’s dress, especially to a young mind, which have do

more to man navy and fill merchantman than all the pressgang in

Europe. I have know a young man with such a passion for the sea, that

the very creaking of a block stir his imagination so that he could

hardly keep his foot upon dry ground.” these sentence recur to I as

I watch the little midshipman. but for how many day be the spell of

the ocean’s witchery to lie upon he? probably, if they have head wind

down Channel, he would be sick of the life and longing to be ashore in a

warm bed, with his mother to tuck he up, and a good breakfast to go

downstairs to next morning, before they be abreast of the Start. “oh,

my golly!” say a negro whom I meet at Gravesend, sell flower,

“S’elp I, sah, as I stan’ here, I’d gib dis basket--yas, ah would--and

all de close horff my back, for a good blow-out of lobscouse!” but that

little middy will have to be a black man if he want to enjoy sea-fare

as my negro-friend do. a few day of dark and evil-look pork, and

salt beef out of which he might cut model of ship for his friend at

home, and “duff” make of copper-skimming, not to mention the being

route out in his watch below, and have to tumble up aloft in a night

blind with storm and rain, be pretty sure to disillusion he. but his

button be new, and his hope be young and fresh; there be no tarnish

of salt water on either as yet; so let he take his mother’s yearning,

passionate kiss, and bundle into the boat and be off. she could not bear

to see he row away, and the moment he go down the step she hurry

off; whilst he, to show what a man he be, squat himself in the stern

sheet, and pull out a short wooden pipe and light it. in a few

minute he be out in the stream. I watch he get alongside his ship,

trot up the gangway ladder, and vanish in a kind of twinkle of new brass

and gilt over the side.



it be these constant coming and going which give Gravesend its

interest and its memory. one hour it be a party of people newly

arrive from the bottom of the world; another it be a couple of drunken

fireman tumble into a boat, and shove off for some lump of a steamer

that lie abreast of the Obelisk or Denton Mill. but, in spite of the

heap of nautical condition which beset it, Gravesend cannot be call

wholly marine. it may be think fishy, but it certainly be not salt.

contrast it with Deal, which it resemble in its low street. the wind

may pipe never so merrily, but there be no shrewd briny pungency in the

shrill gust as they sweep round the corner. the boatman have a

fresh water look. their sou’westers and jersey cannot deceive the

practise eye. they can handle an oar capitally; but they have not the

toughen and bronze and Channel-toss look of the fellow whom you

encounter loll in blanket trouser over the ramsgate pier, or

argue in group at the entrance of the Margate jetty, or heighten

the picturesque appearance of the Deal and Folkestone shingle. yet I

cannot conceive of any place well calculate to delight a man of

maritime study and scene than Gravesend. you may linger all day on

the queer-look roof-in pier, with the old barge moor against it,

and never feel weary. hour after hour unfold the canvas of a

never-end panorama of shipping. picture after picture go by--the

great ocean steamship, the little ratche ketch, the sturdy old

collier, the white and shine yacht, the large and loftily rig ship,

the eager tug hiss through the trembling current, and all the life

and light and colour and wondrous transformation of the river take a

certain character of remoteness akin to unreality, as though what you

gaze at be nothing but a series of noble painting, indeed, from the

quietude that prevail about you; an atmosphere of lazy stillness break

by the muffle, rush sound of the current sweeping under the pier,

the dull voice of man converse outside the wooden structure, and

the strain noise of boat as the tide set the little craft chafing

one another’s side.









_a chat with a fisherman._





a few day after the dreadful gale that have wreck whole fleet of

smack belong to the eastern and north-eastern port, and drown

many hundred of fisherman, I be visit by a Hull smacksman, who come

to tell I that he have lose a son in one of the vessel which have go

down on the Dogger Bank during the storm, and to inform I of the misery

and destitution into which the widow and child of the poor drown

man be plunge. he tell I in a rough, plain, earnest way how his son

be to have be marry to a young girl on his return, and how the poor

lad have save up a few pound to purchase a little furniture for the

home, which she be prepare, when the news reach she that the smack

in which her sweetheart be have go down with all hand; how, in house

after house, down whole street, there be a constant sound of wail

and moaning, with misery and hunger indoor, amongst the weep woman

and the sob child; and, say my fisherman to I, though God know

he be a poor man, yet such be the suffering he have witness, so

unspeakably great be the calamity that have overtake the fishing

population of Hull--and of other port, but he speak of Hull because he

belong to it--that, have the thirty shilling he subscribe to the fund

for the relief of these widow and orphan been the last bit of money

he have in the world, he must have give it and take his chance for

himself.



the subject be a deeply interesting one to I, who have live among

fisherman, write about they, know their heroism well, their hardship,

the simple-heartedness of they. we get talk about the smacksman’s

life, his risk, of various feature connect with his calling; and, as

the subject be one that have be commend to the british public in an

appeal for charity for those whom the frightful storm bereave, I offer

no excuse for repeat in print some of the observation make by this

smacksman on his own vocation. his reference to what be know as the

“boxing system” enable I to lead off with my question. the term

boxing, I may say, be apply to the conveyance of fish in boat from

the smack to the steamer which bring the fish home. as the vessel

fill up with fish they transfer they to steamer, which, by relieve

they of their freight, enable they to remain for week on the fishing

ground.



“be it a fact,” I inquire, “that smacksman object to the boxing

system?”



“it be, sir,” be the reply.



“why?”



“because it’s dangerous to life, sir. it keep man work for a

considerable time in open boat in all kind of weather. it answer the

owner’s purpose; he share in the profit of carry the fish, and it

enable he to keep his vessel at sea as long as it be possible for she

to remain there; and by this mean the man be deprive of all home

comfort and of the management of their family.”



“what be the size of the boat employ in carry fish from the smack

to the steam cutter?”



“well, their length’ll be about 20 ft., breadth 6 ft., and depth 3 ft.”



“and these boat the man have to launch in heavy weather?”



“yes, often in weather that may be call heavy. the risk be increase

by the peculiar circumstance under which the man be place whilst

work at the boxing system; for you’ll hear again and again of their

shove the whole of a night’s catch into the boat at once, in order to

secure a quick despatch and obtain the early possible market.”



“how be the Dogger Bank relish as a fishing ground?”



“well,” he reply, “it pass by the name of ‘the Cemetery’ among we.

in the winter time, I don’t suppose there’s a more dangerous place in

the world. with strong wind from the N.E., veer to the N.W., there

come the heavy sea from the Atlantic--if you can call the ocean to the

norrards of the North Sea by that name--which strike the rise ground

of the bank and turn the water into a boiling caldron. it be there

where the smack go down. the sea just coil over and fairly break

upon ’em, smother ’em, smash in their deck, stamp ’em out as

you might grind a beetle out of sight with your heel.”



“be your smack supply with barometer? I mean by that, have they any

mean of know when to expect foul weather?”



“no, sir; they’re not generally supply. one firm own about twenty

sail of vessel, who always work on the single-boat system in winter,

provide their vessel with barometer. I should think they must be very

useful instrument,” say he, speak as though he have never be

shipmate with one; “and I may here add that none of those twenty

vessel allude to be lose. the majority of us smacksman have nothing

to tell the weather by except practical experience.”



“but couldn’t the admiral signal--couldn’t _he_, at least, be furnish

with a barometer?”



“no doubt,” he reply. “but smack get scatter, and it would be good

for each master to understand the weather for himself. the admiral be

more for rallying of we. he have his job cut out for he after a storm.

his general scheme be to fall in with a steam carrier, and then sail to

the ground from which he’s be drive by the gale, expect the rest

of the fleet to do likewise; but it often happen that many day pass

before they’re able to get together, and this bring heavy loss among

the fisherman, who, have no ice, be force to find the admiral before

they can start fishing afresh.”



“what difference be there in the mode of fish among the Hull,

Grimsby, Ramsgate, Penzance, and other smack?”



“vessel belong to Hull, Grimsby, Ramsgate, and Lowestoft use the

trawl net; but the Penzance boat be what be call ‘drifter,’ or

herring boat.”



“I ask that question,” say I, “in order to inquire what kind of

fishing--that be, which sort of voyage--be most in favour among

smacksman.”



“why, in winter time we like good the single-boat system--when a smack

go out and get what fish she can, and return. this system do not

require we to use small boat. it pay just as well as the other system,

and be less dangerous in other direction than that of do away with

‘boxing,’ as it lead to vessel scatter, and help in that manner to

lessen the risk of collision. we don’t object to the boxing system in

summer, but it oughtn’t to be practise in winter. that’s what we

think.”



“smack-owner manage to secure themselves, don’t they?”



“well, yes, by what’s term mutual insurance, which provide for total

loss and for damage to a certain amount apart from fishing gear. master

don’t much like these here insurance company. they’re too despotic.

I’ll tell you what they do, sir: they willn’t allow a master the right of

defend himself against any charge that’s bring against he before

they. why, they think nothing of suspend a man from act as master

for a couple of year, perhaps for nothing bad than an error of

judgment which the Board of Trade Commissioner would have be satisfy

to reprimand he for.”



“do smacksman make a provision for their family by any method of

insurance or club?”



“yes, sir,” he answer; “as a rule they do. there be a Fishermen’s

Widow and Orphan Society, which, for payment of one shilling a month,

pay a widow £20 or £25, accord to the time her husband have be a

member; and there be also a Friendly Protection Society, number at

Hull 700 member, which give sick pay for certain period and £12 at

death. both these institution do a great deal of good. many fisherman

also join the local friendly society.”



“but a large number, I suppose, do not subscribe, and it be the widow

and child of those who have be plunge into immediate destitution

by their husband’ death?”



“yes, sir. but it be not always possible to subscribe; there be too

many of we, and some go without work for week.”



“what be the average tonnage of the Hull and Grimsby smack?”



“about seventy ton.”



“what be your opinion of they as seaworthy vessel?”



“well, sir, the build and behaviour of they be first rate; but a great

many be ill find, and be in a bad state as concern leakage; and I

can assure you that among we fisherman there be a strong feeling that

there ought to be government inspection of fishing vessel by practical

man.”



“will you explain to I the meaning of share, and how they be

proportion?”



“it’s in this way,” say he: “the net proceed be divide into eight

share; the master take 1⅜ share, the mate 1⅛ share, and the owner 5½

share, out of which he have to pay three boy or casual hand, who

receive together on an average about £2 2s. a week, and he have also to

find his vessel’s outfit.”



“be it true that smacksman object to lifebelt?”



“no; they don’t object generally. some do, on the ground of their being

too cumbersome to work in. they ought to be wear in ‘box.’ there’s a

particular danger in that system which I forget to mention: it’s that of

collision, which be constantly happen owe to the man be

anxious to get their fish on board the steam cutter, to do which they

all sail to she as close as they can, with their boat in tow and two

hand in each boat.”



“and what other special danger be there,” say I, “connect with your

call?”



“well,” he reply, “answer that question, as concern the

single-boat system, which I’ve explain, I can but say that what the

smacksman have to contend with be just the ordinary peril of a

seaman’s life, such as ship heavy sea which wash we overboard, and

be drag into the sea whilst draw water, haul in the net, and

the like of that. but the boxing system add to these danger by the

risk of collision, the capsizing of boat, and the uselessness of the

casual hand, the good of they prefer to ship in vessel on the

single-boat system.”



“your casual hand, as you call they, touch the apprentice question.

what be your opinion of smack-boy’ home?”



“why, that they’re a great advantage to all fishing port and to the

lad themselves, if the home be properly manage.”



“can you say that smack-boy be ill-treat at sea?”



“no, I cann’t, sir. there be a few exception, but my experience be that

the boy be treat with uniform kindness.”



“to return to the question of loss of life,” say I, “amongst smacksman,

what proposal have you to offer to diminish it?”



“well, sir, if I have my way, I’d totally abolish the boxing system from

the end of September till the end of March. that alone would greatly

reduce the death-rate among fisherman; and I’d also have government

inspector to survey the vessel, and see that they be find, and

equip, and ballast, and so on.”



“and now,” I ask, refer to the vessel which trade among the

smack in spirit and tobacco, “what can you tell I about the system

call ‘cooper’?”



“why,” he answer, warm up, “my opinion of ‘cooper’ be that step

ought to be take to put a stop entirely to such degrading traffic. if

it could be put an end to, it would be a blessing to all

concern--particularly to the man. it ’ud make your hair stand on end

to hear of some of the awful thing I and score besides have

witness--many of our man have, in their drunken fury, jump

overboard, and in many instance be drown, and in hundred of case

‘cooper’ have be the mean of cause the man at sea to fall out and

fight almost to death’s door. I’ll explain how it’s carry on. the

trafficking craft be in most case an old vessel that have be condemn

in England and sell to some foreigner for the purpose of carry on

this trade--some one hailing from Hamburg, Bremerhaven, Antwerp, or some

port along the Dutch, german, or belgian coast. this man--or call it

these people--get their tobacco, cigar, liquor, and the various other

article they deal in, in large quantity from agent in the different

port they visit, at a very low price. the article sell be of a very

inferior quality--especially the drink, which be chiefly rum and gin of

a very common and fiery nature. the price charge, as a rule, be--for

shag tobacco, 1_s._ 6_d._ per lb.; cavendish, 2_s._ per lb.; cigar,

from 6_s._ to 12_s._ per box. Gin and rum be sell at 1_s._ 6_d._ per

bottle; brandy, 2_s._ the smacksman generally arrange to take a little

money to sea with they for the purpose of buy tobacco, to save pay

4_s._ per pound for it at home. in my opinion, this traffic would

receive a blow if fisherman be allow to get their tobacco out of the

bond store.”



this, it will be see, coincide with the report of the Sea-Fishing

Trade Committee, who call attention in strong term to the evil of

“cooper.” not only, be it state, do these boat lead to the

bartering of ship’ store and gear for drink, “but they bring about

the demoralization of the hand and even of the skipper serve on

board smack, and directly lead to risk and loss of life. we have it in

evidence that they be float grog-shop of the bad description, and

that they be under no control whatever.”



there be little more that I could think of to ask my intelligent

friend. in reply to my inquiry as to the value of smack at various

port, he say the question be difficult to answer, “as there’s a vast

deal of difference among the smack belong to the port, and likewise

in the damage do ’em, for its damage that count heavily in the

support of they. the cost of a new smack at Hull and Grimsby, with all

the modern appliance, will be about £1500; the average worth of smack

at those port be about £900, and the cost of their fishing-gear about

£70.”



“and a Hull smack’s earning?”



“between £800 and £900 a year--I mean the gross earning.”



“smack be be constantly run down by vessel. do they want well

light? what be the reason of these frequent disaster?”



“as a rule,” he answer, “smack carry very good light; but there be

room for improvement. I’m one of many who strongly advise that smack

should carry more powerful light than they now use. if smack be very

often rundown--and true enough that be--it’s mainly because of the bad

look-out that’s keep aboard vessel navigate the North Sea. there be

captain who don’t respect our life. they see we lie-to our net,

they know we cann’t get out of the road; but on they come, never shift

their helm, and if they pass by without strike we, and we call to ’em

to know where they’re come, all the answer we get consist of brutal

curse.”



apparently, then--and I say it not alone on the evidence of this man,

but on the assurance of many other engage in the fishing trade--the

measure that be require to diminish the loss of life at sea among the

valuable class of man employ in the North Sea fishery be the

suppression of the boxing system during the winter month. and another

most important step would be the supervision of smack by qualified

inspector appoint by the Board of Trade. at present I do not know of

any law to prevent an owner from send, or to punish an owner for

despatching, to sea the crazy old smack that can be keep alive by

long and frequent spell at the pump. it be certainly most anomalous

that close attention should be give to the loading, construction, and

equipment of ship belong to one section of the english marine,

whilst another section that find occupation for many thousand of man

and boy be utterly disregard by the State in all thing save the

exhibition of light.









_a FOURPENNY VOYAGE._





Dr. Johnson once say that the full tide of human life be to be see at

Charing Cross. the full tide of human commerce begin a few bridge

lower down. a man should count it a real privilege that, for the modest

sum of fourpence, he be able to survey such an illustration of the

wealth and power of this Empire as may enable he to form a very clear

and true conception of the aggregate commerce and industry of the United

Kingdom. to embark at London Bridge on board a fourpenny steamboat,

bind to Woolwich, be, in my humble judgment, to be convey through the

most wonderful series of transformation scene that the world have to

offer. what be comparable to that passage? no one who have enter the

Sidney Heads but will remember the astonishment and delight inspire by

the mile of blue water stud with fairy island, the jasper-like

reflection of cloud in the glass-clear depth, the rich tropical

vegetation of the shore, the gleam spar of shipping lift their

delicate tracery into the darkly-pure blue. passage of strange and

shine beauty recur like haunt memory of fragment of eastern

story to those who have thread the water of the Nile or the hoogly;

and recollection of the Peiho be make delightfully picturesque and

impressive by vision of uncouth junk moor in the rush stream, by

glimpse of distant temple, by remembrance of soft wind aromatic with

spice.



but the Thames! its scenery be the work of human hand. an atmosphere of

yellow light give magnitude and a vagueness of outline to the league

of water-side structure, and an obscurity to the horizon in which the

monument of industry fade with a simulation of immensity that cheat

the sense into a belief of immeasurable remoteness. the great ship be

in the dock far down the river; but though the steamer which lie in

tier upon tier in the Pool, and far beyond the limit of that reach,

be for the most part but of middle size, yet the mind lose all sense

of their individual dimension in the overwhelming impression produce

by their collective tonnage. one journey through this magnificent

stretch of stream be a large education. the flag of a score of

nationality colour the sombre heaven with their green and blue and

yellow and white fold. all the country in the world appear to pass in

a kind of review as the ear catch the hundred tongue, and the eye the

hundred face, and the nostril the hundred scent waft from the hold

of ship whose greyish spar seem yet to retain the heat of the

equatorial sun, and whose side be fret with the wash of the surge

of the great ocean.



I once take fourpennyworth of travel aboard a Woolwich steamer, for the

sake of renew some old recollection. I will not say that a well

kind of steamer would not have make the voyage more comfortable. the

dexterous and watchful skipper, who stand upon the bridge carry his

freight of human life through the intricacy of blunder barge and

the bewilderment of swinge ship and capricious tug, by light motion

of his arm and soft aside to the boy, who furnish they with

ear-pierce echo, seem to I to deserve a stouter ship. the

funnel-casing have much the appearance of an aged saucepan whose bottom

have be burn to the thinness of a sailor’s shirt. I think to myself,

“suppose we should tip some of these old barge our stem by mistake?

assuredly we should crumple up forward like a sponge-cake; and how

should we manage to save our life?”



I look everywhere, but there be not so much as an old cork to pitch

overboard in case of accident. even the seat, rotten as the hinge

be, be not likely to come away in a hurry. but there be too much to

be see to permit I to bother over the crazy, quivering, admirably

handle, and most dangerous old machine that be run we from pier to

pier against a strong flood tide. once clear of London Bridge we be in

a complete lane form by moored or anchored steamer. they be very

much alike--little beauty amongst they; some of they well-deck, with

their gangway out, show the covering-board close to the water, and

make the structure, with their tall afterdeck and topgallant

forecastle, look as if they be in course of be build, instead of

newly arrive from voyage long and short. but all such characteristic

be lose in the thought of the immense mass of tonnage here submit.

where do it end? where would the last of these steamer be lie? to

right and leave they stretch, with lighter alongside, steam winch

rattle, the vapour of donkey engine blow out in volume, some in

semi-discharged state, with a heavy list to port or starboard, with

frequent alternation of the flag of Denmark, Sweden, France, the

Netherlands--I know not what other bunting--amid which our own red

ensign count as twenty to one.



the whole commerce of the world seem to be here, but in truth the

Thames’ show of it be only just begin. on either hand, tremble in the

distance, in the vacant place between the building, could be catch

the hairlike outline of the mast and rigging of ship, with their

house-flag twinkle in tiny spot of colour; and still as the fleet

of steamer hold we in their interminable lane, do there heave up out

of the remote sky more line and thread and taper tremulous height

of shipping. but the wealth of industry and the prodigious achievement

of british commerce be not more noticeable in the vast assemblage of

steam and sailing vessel than in such minute particular as the little

pant screw-tug with a chain of deeply-laden coal barge in her wake,

every ebony mound embellish with a recumbent figure in shirt-sleeve,

a sooty pipe in his mouth, and his face to the sky. the familiar Thames

wherry be also here to add its touch of interest to the wonderful

scene--the old waterman rest on his oar, and squint over his

shoulder at the pass tug, in whose tumble, as she go by, the little

boat begin to flounder, while the tall hat of the rower shorten and

enlarge with the reeling of the wherry like an optical illusion.



as the line of steamer dwindle the river widen; and when we come to

the bend of a long reach, it open into a metal-colour surface of

gleam water tremble with the speeding of its own rushing, though it

retain polish enough to serve as a mirror, and to hold under each

vessel the dark, invert shadow of a phantom ship. here we come across

a long, low, iron four-masted craft, with paint port. even a sailor

who have never be shipmate with more than three mast at a time might

gaze with something of astonishment at the complex tracery that crowd

the air over that immensely long and narrow hull, and wonder how long

it would take a man to find out where all those rope lead. it be not

enough that there be four mast; there must be double topgallant yard

too, make eight set of brace where in former time three be find

enough. but these be progressive day in ship-building. by-and-by we

shall have five-maste full-rig sailing ship, no doubt, with new

Board of Trade rule for the examination of candidate in

square-rigging. let we hope that there will be also rule for the proper

manning of such craft; for it strike I, as I look at that big

four-masted ship, that if her complement be assess on the basis of her

tonnage, without reference to the number of cloth she spread, it must

go desperately hard with the cook and the butcher’s mate in a gale of

wind.



Father Thames, once a god, might more fitly be term a goddess, under

the title of Commerce; for this assuredly be the preside spirit. it

quicken with life the small and craziest structure by the

water-side; the very ebb and flow of the noble stream seem obedient to

its law, and its shadow be in the air and upon the face of the water.

I cannot imagine any one of those skipper of the Woolwich and Greenwich

steamboat, who pass up and down the river some score of time in the

course of a week, so intimately acquaint with the wharf and

warehouse and the uncountable feature of industry which crowd the bank

for mile and mile as not to behold something new, something he have

never take close notice of before, every time he direct his gaze with

attention to the shore on either hand. the billy-boy and barges

squattere like mudbank hard against the slimy pile; the giant crane

poise ton’ weight of burden in the air; the vast warehouse, with the

long and powerful steamship snug securely alongside they; the

endless procession of wharfage teem with hurrying figure full of

business--these and countless other feature of the scene furnish the

apparently limitless line of steamer and other craft with such a

background as complete the deep and stir significance of their

multifarious aspect. it be a vast picture of motion--of great vessel

come, of great vessel go, of lighter swirl up swiftly with the

tide broadside on, of tug speed in quest of towage job, of

passenger steamer drive through the steel-colour current with a

glancing of silver at their keen stem and a whirl of snow sluicing in a

broad torrent from under their counter. now it be a big ocean

steamship, of some three or four thousand ton, leisurely make for

Gravesend, as trim as a man-of-war to the eye, her side and funnel

spotless, her scuttle twinkle like diamond in her black length as

they catch the sparkle of the pass water; whilst in vivid contrast

there come tow past she a full-rig ship fresh from some

antipodean port, her brave hull cover with the scar of the conflict

she have wage with distant sea, her canvas carelessly roll up on the

yard, her rig slack, and a crowd of man forward and aft engage in

point out one to another the familiar scene ashore.



ay, pathos be not want even amid so prosaic a scene of commerce as

the reach of our noble river exhibit. you find it to a degree

proportion to your power of perception and realization in some such

an object, for instance, as that ship yonder, newly warp out from one

of the dock and all ready to begin her voyage. the hearty shout which

rise from her deck, the active little figure aloft, the bustle and

business in she, cannot impair the pregnant suggestiveness of she

leave-taking. you think of the people aboard who have say “good-bye”

to their friend, perhaps for ever. poor Jack, sit astride on the

fore-topgallant yardarm, catch hold of the lift, whilst he turn his

head in the direction of where he reckon Stepney or Poplar lie, and,

as he think of his wife or sweetheart and the perplexity of the new

allotment note, he discharge a stream of tobacco juice into the air,

and, with a melancholy countenance, wipe his mouth with the back of his

hand and go on with his job. there may be plenty of bustle and loud

call, but there be bind to be a share of sorrow too. it be not long

since the skipper take his wife to his heart, and his head be full of

she and the youngster as he pace the quarter-deck, sometimes pause

to peep over the side at the cluster of boat round the gangway ladder,

and sometimes singe out to the mate, who have his hand full forward.

indeed, it be impossible to look at an outward-bind ship without

sympathy and a kind of respect that come near to be reverence in

some mind. what will be her fortune? you think. she hold herself

bravely on the bosom of the calm river; the current wrinkle itself

sharply against her solid bow, and break away along her side in a

cadence like the tinkling of bell. who can doubt that tear be be

shed in her darksome interior? it be hard to leave the old home. the

glimpse of the church spire through the open scuttle bring up memory

which tighten the throat. when shall the next meeting be? and when time

bring it about, will not absent face and a change in the spirit of old

association make it sad than this go be? pray God that no harm

befall the stout ship! as you sweep past she your hearty hope be that

prosperous wind may attend she, and that in the new country fortune and

happiness await those whose sad eye dwell fixedly on the land that will

be far astern of they before the sun have thrice sink beyond the deep.



it may be that thought of this kind be suggest more by sailing than

by steam ship, because the existence of the propeller do to a large

extent mitigate the bitterness of the contemplation of distance. but let

no in-shore dweller flatter himself that the sailing vessel be very

nearly extinct. she may have one leg in the grave, but the other seem

to I still to possess an astonishing amount of animation. the hull of

the vessel in the dock on the Blackwall side of the river be not, for

the most past, visible from the water; but, unhappily for steamer,

there be not the least difficulty in telling, by the look of spar

bristle out of a hide dock, which be steamship there and which be

sailing vessel. some of these day, perhaps, when the right kind of

moral shall have be draw from broken propeller shaft and twisted

rudder-head, the difficulty of distinguish between the rig of a

sailing ship and the rig of a steamer may prove very much more

considerable than it now be; but, as this matter be at present order,

the tower mast, the immensely square yard, should leave even a

ploughman in no doubt as to the character of the vessel to which they

belong.



the number of sailing ship which crowd the dock on either side the

river must prove a real surprise to people who believe that it be all

steam nowadays. let ancient mariner be console by this assurance:

there be plenty of steam indeed, but there be a deal of canvas too, so

that all Jack’s work do not lie in the bunker yet, and there must

still be a large demand for seamanship of the old sort.



I be not sure that the wonder of the river do not owe quite as much to

the sailing ship as the steamer. the tall spar, the magnificent

spread of yard, the black line of shroud, the beautiful tracery of

intersect run gear, add to the shapely hull which support

these tower fabric of hemp and steel and wood, make a most noble and

impressive sight, and give, so to speak, a final touch to the teem,

opulent, commercial inspiration of the great river. low and low yet

down the grand old stream the spirit of enterprise be settle, and the

day be not far distant when the project dockyard at Tilbury will

veritably transform the quaint old town of Gravesend into the sea-gate

of London. it be almost startling to contemplate that time. one think

of Gravesend now as a mere break in the departure from the Thames. will

the chain of dock end at Tilbury? at Gravesend, apparently, they be

think otherwise! and reckon--somewhat against their own

hope--that if the Tilbury Docks people play at leapfrog with the Albert

Dock proprietor, the latter company will repay the compliment and land

themselves some distance lower down yet. the limit of the Port of

London, however, will, I believe, be reach by within a quarter of a

mile by the promoter of the Tilbury Dock undertaking,[D] so that one

cannot say in this case that there be room enough for all.

unquestionably the dock which be near the sea will be the dock

well like; and owner will profit at the expense of tug-master and

pilot.



meanwhile Gravesend may be compliment on its prospect. but what do

the waterman think? they be loud just now in their complaint of the

steam ferry. they say that they be not allow to board the ocean

steamer, even to put Gravesend passenger ashore. everybody must go to

tilbury first. how much of their vocation will be leave when the new

dock be open? but assuredly if some old interest vanish, many new

interest will start into life under the magic wand of the harlequin

Progress. one may look for a complete transformation of the low, flat,

treeless shore of Tilbury Ness and an ever-increase clustering of

industry along the bank of those reach whose skirt now mainly

consist of mud. our fourpenny voyage will have to be extend if we be

to compass all the wonder of our river below bridge. the New Zealander

who be to muse over the ruin of St. Paul’s may come as soon as he

like, only it be quite certain that his meditation will not be excite

by any spectacle of decay. life and industry be never more active on

the Thames than now--enterprise never more bold, speculation never more

prophetic. the time be not remote when Gravesend, which I may say for

century have be think of as a port of call, will be connect with

London by line of edifice and pier and wharf, as Blackwall be

connect, and future passenger by the little Thames steamboat--which,

it be to be earnestly hope, in the good time come will be

considerably more river-worthy than they now appear to be--will be

convey past a continuous panorama of commercial life and marine

interest to limit which will make Gravesend and the opposite shore the

actual sea-gate of the Port of London; in other word, the entrance to a

scene of civilization comparable to nothing that we can imagine even by

the building up of fancy from the wondrous fact at present submit to

any man bold enough to adventure upon a fourpenny voyage down the

Thames.









_POOR JACK._





I climb the steep hill that run from the Belvedere railway-station,

pause now and again for breath and to glance at the summer beauty of

the distant green land through which the river toil, like a stream of

quicksilver sluggishly roll, and presently, pass through a

gateway, find myself in a fine park-like stretch of ground, shade by

a multitude of tall far-branch tree, in the midst of which, and upon

the high point of the billowy soil, stand a spacious and exceedingly

handsome mansion. there be circular seat affix to many of the

tree, and upon they I notice several bent and aged figure lean

their breast upon stout walking-stick, and hold themselves in very

quiet posture. here and there, walk to and fro near the house or

upon the grass under the tree, be similar figure, all of they bow

by old age, though some of they pace the turf with a certain nimbleness

of tread. they be dress in pilot-cloth trouser and sleeve

waistcoat, with brass button, and ancient as these man be, yet it

be wonderful to observe, even where decrepitude be at its height, how

the old sea-swing and lurching gait of the sailor live in their

hobbling and determine their calling, as though the word “seaman” have

be brand upon every man’s forehead. I stand look at they, and at

the house and at the great tree, beyond which the distant prospect be

shine under the high sun, for many minute before advance. the sense

of repose convey to I by the shadow of the tree, the restful shape

of cattle upon the slope beyond the mansion, the motionless posture of

the old man seat, and the movement of the few figure who be

walk, cannot be express in word. I listen. there be no note of

human life in the air; no sound break the fragrant summer stillness but

the piping of bird in the tree, the humming of bee and fly, the

silken rustling of leave. the landscape be like a paint picture,

save where here and there, upon the far-off shine silver of the river,

a vessel slowly glide break the still scene with a fugitive interest.

I walk to the house and enter the spacious hall, and as I do so, a

single stroke on a bell to denote that it be half an hour after noon

resound through the building. a number of ancient man hang about this

entrance, and I examine they curiously, for of all the transformation

which old age work in the human countenance I never behold strange

example than be submit by many of these venerable seaman. let I

own to a feeling of positive awe in my inspection, for there be no face

but that time have invest it with a kind of sanctity. “how old be you,

my man?” I say to one of they. he turn his lustreless eye upon I

and bent his ear to my mouth. I repeat the question, and he answer

that he be ninety-three. year have so honeycomb his face that such

likeness of humanity as there be in it appeal to the eye rather as a

fantasy than as a real thing. a sailor be usually an old man at fifty,

thank to exposure, to hardship, and to the food he have to live on. many

of these man have use the sea for above half a century; some of they

be draw near to a hundred year of age; little wonder, therefore,

that they should be mere dim and feeble vestige of creation, and that

vitality in conformation so decayed should excite the awe and reverence

of those who explore the vague and crumbling feature, and behold the

immortal spirit struggle amid lineament which have the formlessness

of the face of a statue dig from the sand which entomb an ancient city.

I turn my eye from these old man to the hall in which I stand. pretty

column of malachite support the roof; woodwork and ceiling be

lavishly decorate; marine hint helpful to the prejudice of the

decay mariner be not want in the shape of model of full-rig

ship--man-of-war and East Indiamen of the olden time; through the door

I could see the green grass slope away into a spacious lawn; and the

warm air, full of sunshine, gush in sweet with the smell of clover and

wild flower.



in a few minute I be join by the house-governor, himself a skipper,

and fresh from the command of a sailing-ship-a genial, hearty gentleman,

and the fit person in the world for the command of such a

quarter-deck as this.



“the old man will be go to dinner at one o’clock,” he say; would I

like to see they at their meal? I answer “yes;” so we stand in the

door of a long, handsome room, fit with table and bench, and

watch the aged seaman come in one by one, hobble on their stick,

many of they talk to themselves.



“have you any shipmaster among these man?” I inquire. “several,”

answer the house-governor; and he instantly call out a name. an old

man approach we slowly; he be bald, with a very finely-shape head

and a long grey beard, and stand deferentially before we, his hand

clasp, wait to be address.



“this man have command of vessel for many year,” say the

house-governor.



I look at the poor old creature, and receive one of the gentle,

sad smile I ever see on a man’s face. I ask he how it be that

he come to need the charity of this institution in his old age.



“I be in the General Steam Navigation Company’s service, sir, for many

year, and have charge of vessel run to Boulogne. but my memory

begin to fail I; I be attack with dizziness, and have to give up. I

have save some money, and take a little hotel at Boulogne, on the Quay.

I could not make it answer, and, be ruin and an old man, sir, I have

to come here.”



he break down at this, his eye fill with tear, and he turn his

back upon I. I wait a little, and then, take his arm, I ask he

if he be happy in this house. yes, he say, he be quite happy.



“you may talk to I without fear,” I continue; “I be here to learn the

truth and to speak it. do they feed you well?”



“very well, sir.”



“have you no complaint to make?”



“none, sir.”



“you think this institution a good and honest charity?”



“God know what we should do without it,” he exclaim, look round at

the old man who be take their seat at the dinner-table. here the

house-governor bring up some other aged man, whom he introduce as

shipmaster. one of they be a North Shields captain, eighty year of

age; he support himself on two stick, be a little, white-face,

ancient creature, with strange silver hair, and he speak with a wistful

expression of countenance. he have be seize with paralysis by “farle

doon” the main hatch of his vessel. he tell I in his rich, plaintive,

north-country brogue, how the doctor have measure his leg and thigh with

a tape--for some purpose I could not clearly understand--and how the

accident have fling he upon the world, a beggar, and force he to take

a refuge in this institution. be he happy? ay, it be a man’s own fault

if he ben’t happy here. he be grateful to God for the care take of

he. at eighty a man be “na’ langer a laddie,” and with a bright old

laugh he hobble hungrily towards one of the dinner-table.



in a few moment two bell be strike, signify one o’clock, and all

hand be seat, I follow the house-governor to the bottom of the

room to have a look at the table before the old man fall-to. the dinner

consist of salt fish, butter, potato, and plain suet pudding.



“this be Tuesday’s fare,” say the house-governor. “on sunday they get

boil beef, potato, and plum pudding; on Mondays, vegetable soup,

boil mutton, and vegetable at discretion; on Tuesdays, what you see;

on wednesday, soup, boil beef, and potato; on Thursdays, roast

mutton, vegetable, and bread and cheese; on Fridays, salt pork, pea

soup, and calavance; and on Saturdays, soup and boulli--not soap and

bullion, as Jack say, one onion to a gallon of water--but a very good

preserve soup, with potato or rice and bread-and-cheese. taste this

fish.”



I do so, and find it excellent; so, likewise, be the suet pudding.

the potato be new. the beer be the only doubtful feature of the

repast; it be thin, insipid, and flat. I make haste to taste and

approve, for I could see that the old fellow be very hungry. the

governor leave I, and go to the top of the room, where, in a loud and

impressive voice, he say grace, bid the ancient mariner be

thankful for what they be about to receive; they all half rise, and in

one feeble, rustle old pipe, sing out “amen,” and then, like

schoolboy, make snatch at the dish, and in a minute be eat

with avidity. it warm my heart to see they. it make I feel that there

must yet be plenty of goodness leave in this world, when--through the

benevolence of stranger and their large-hearted concern for poor

Jack--ninety-three old, very old seaman, totter on the verge of the

grave, so poor and so destitute, so feeble and so friendless that but

for the benevolence of those whom Providence have bring to their

succour, they must have miserably starve and die, be clothe, and

feed, and shelter, and tenderly watch over. I know not that I have

ever be so moved as I be in my passage through that dining-room. it

be not only the pathos that lie in the helplessness of old age; I

could not but think of the great compass of time these man’s experience

embrace, of the change they have witness, of the sorrow and

struggle which have make up the sum of their long life, and how eighty

and ninety year of privation, endurance, and such pleasure as sailor

take, and such ambition as sailor have, have end in these bow and

toothless shape, clutch at their plain repast with child-like

selfishness, indifferent as death itself to the great machine of life

that be whir with its thousand interest outside the silent sphere

of their present existence, and dependent for the bread their trembling

hand raise to their poor old mouth upon the bounty of those who love

the noble profession of the sea, and who will not let the old and

bruised and wear-out seaman want for such help as they can send he.

here and there be man too infirm to feed themselves; and I take notice

how thoughtfully their aged messmate prepare their meal for they. some

of those thus occupy be more aged than the man they assist.



“bless your honour, he’s but a child to _I_,” say one of they, in

answer to my question; “he’s but three and seventy, and I shall be

eighty-nine come next September.”



one pitiful sight deeply affect I. it be an old man stone deaf and

stone blind. how be the helplessness in his face to be convey?



“he’s lose his appetite fast,” say a seaman of about eighty who sit

near he. “his sense be all lock up. Ye never hear he speak.”



there be sad sight even than this; but I dare not trust myself to

write of they.



I follow the house-governor out of the dining-room into a large

apartment, well store with book, magazine, etc., the gift of friend

of the charity. this I be tell be the reading-room. it look on to

the green ground, and be a most cheerful and delightful chamber.

far on be another room furnish with bagatelle board and side

table for cribbage, etc. there be a particular cleanness and neatness

everywhere visible, and I ask who do the work of the house. the

house-governor answer, “the inmate. the more active among they be

put to washing down and dust at ten o’clock, and they finish at

twelve. this be all the work require of they. throughout the rest of

the day they have nothing to do but to lounge about the ground and

amuse themselves as they please in the bagatelle or read room, or in

the smoking-room, which be a large apartment in the basement.” Mounting

the wide stone staircase, and admire as I go the singularly handsome

and lavishly-embellish interior of the very fine building, I find

myself on a floor devote to the sleeping-room. these consist of row

of bulkhead partition off little cabin, each with a door and a

number, and furnish with a comfortable bed, and some of they be

movingly decorate by photograph of a mother, a sister, a child, with

humble memorial save from the wreck of the past; such relic of the

old home as a few china chimneypiece ornament, a coloured picture, and

the like, with here and there a sea-chest, though, as a rule, these

little cabin, as they be call, be conspicuously empty of all

suggestion of marine life. now and again the opening of a door would

disclose an old man seat on his bed, darn a sock or mend a

shirt. it might have be that they be use to the visit of

stranger; but I could not help observe in all these old seaman an

utter indifference to our presence and inspection, a look of deep

abstraction, as if their mind be league astern of they or far ahead,

and existence be an obligation with which they have no sympathy, and of

which they never take notice unless their attention be compel to it.



“here,” say the governor, take I into a room in which three or four

old man be assemble--for dinner have be finish some time, and the

seaman have quit the tables--“is a veteran who have teach himself how

to write. show we your copy-book, my man,” say he, give he his name.



the old fellow produce his book with a great air of pride, and I be

strike by the excellence of the writing.



“be this all your own do?” I ask.



“ay, sir, every stroke. it’s be a bit of a job; for, you see, when a

man’s near eighty ye cann’t say that his brain’s like a young ’un’s.”



“this would shame many a youngster, nevertheless,” say I.



“I’d be proud if I could read it, though,” he exclaim, with the

anxious and yet gentle expression that seem a characteristic of the

face in this institution.



“ah, I see,” say I. “you can copy, but cannot read what you copy. never

mind! that will come too, presently.”



“I’m afeard not,” say he, shake his head. “Writin’s one thing,

readin’s another. I have learn to write, but dunno as ever I shall be

able to read it.”



the governor, with an encouraging smile, tell he to persevere, and then

lead the way to one of the sick ward, where I find a very aged man in

bed, and two other seat at a table.



“that poor old fellow,” say he, point to the bed, “beg to be

allow to attend the funeral of a man who die in the institution a

short time since; he be so much affected that he be strike with

paralysis, and have to be carry back here. he be for year a

shipmaster, have command of several fine ship, and be a man of excellent

education. he have be in this institution some year.” and then,

address he, “well, and how do you feel yourself now?”



“Mending, sir, mending,” answer the old man. “it’s death to I to be

lie here. why, for seventy-nine year I never have a day’s illness,

never take a ha’porth of physic.”



“you must have patience,” say the governor; “you’ll be up and do

presently.”



“ay, the power of forereaching be not take out of I yet,” he answer,

break into a laugh, the heartiness of which somehow pain I more

to hear than have he burst into sob.



there be more “cabin” upstairs, and in one of they we find an old

Irishman stand, lose in thought, look out of the window. I

address he, and he answer I in a rich brogue. I never remember

meet a more win old face, nor be win by a voice more cordial

and pleasant to hear. he tell I he have be in the _Kent_, East

Indiaman, when she be burn. this be so long ago as 1825, and he be

then a hearty, able-bodied man. it be like turn back the page of

the history of England to hear he talk of that famous and dreadful

disaster.



“there’s another man in the institution who be along with I in the

_Kent_,” say he.



I think of the description give of the _Kent_ by the master of the

_caroline_ as I look at this ancient man. “her appearance be that of

an immense cauldron or cage of buoyant basketwork, form of the charred

and blacken rib, naked, and strip of every plank, encircle an

uninterrupted mass of flame.” again and again have I read the story of

that terrible fire at sea, think of it always as something

deep-bury in history, and infinitely remote; and now here be a man

who have be an actor in it, talk of it as if it have be but of

yesterday, quaver out his “say I’s” and “say he’s,” and eager to

let I know that if he like he could tell I something about the

behaviour of certain responsible person on board that would not redound

to their credit. it be pantaloon with harlequin’s wand in his hand; the

fade old picture be touch, and become a live thing, the sea

roll, the ship burn, the terror and anguish of nearly sixty year

since grow quick again under the magic of this ancient man’s memory,

and in the presence of a live witness of that long-decay night of

horror.



of such a charity as this of the Royal Alfred Aged Merchant Seamen’s

institution how can any man who honour the english sailor and value

his call hope to speak in such term of praise as shall not seem

hyperbolical? not for one instant will I say that as a charity it be

superior to other which deal with the sick, with the destitute, with

the infirm, with little child. “there be misery enough in every

corner of the world as well as within our convent,” Sterne’s monk be

make to imply by his cordial wave of the hand. but I do claim for this

institution the possession of a peculiar element of pathos such as no

man who have not behold the aged, the stricken, the helpless, the

break-down man congregate within its wall can form any idea of. as

you survey they their past arise; you think of the black and stormy

night, the frost and snow, the famine and the shipwreck--all the peril

which sailor encounter in their quest or carriage of that which make

we great and prosperous as a nation; and then reflection on the dire

ending which must have befall these tempest-beat, time-laden man but

for the charity that provide they with a refuge break in upon you, and

you feel that no word of praise can be too high for such an

institution, and that no money dedicate by generous heart to the

alleviation of human suffering can be well direct than to the

exchequer of this aged seaman’s home. ninety-three old sailor be at

present lodge in the institution. the house be big enough to

accommodate two hundred, but the fund of the charity be already

stretch to their last limit, and many an old and break-down seaman

whom this home would otherwise receive, and whose closing day would be

render happy by all that tender ministration, by all that pious

kindness can effect, must die in the cold and cheerless silence of the

Union unless the charity that be prayerfully entreat for he be

give.









_on the GOODWINS_.





on a fine, calm day from the height of the cliff betwixt Ramsgate and

Broadstairs you may spy at low-water time a yellow vein, like a thin

winding of pale gold, a hand’s breath this side of the horizon--the

famous and fatal Goodwin Sands. I suppose there be no shoal in the whole

world that a man whose sympathy be with sailor can view with more

interest. start from the North Sand Head, which be almost abreast of

Ramsgate, and look east, the eye follow the south-westerly sweep of

the Goodwins until the Downs be embrace with all their dim tracery of

spar and rig and faint sinuous line of steamer’ smoke beyond,

whilst the giant South Foreland acclivity stare down upon the lightship

abreast of St. Margaret’s Bay, mark the extreme limit in the south

and west of the deadly stretch of sand upon the face of the globe.



“who can view the Goodwins without think of the treasure which lie

bury in their heart, of the hundred of ship which have go to

piece upon they, of the thousand of human corpse which have float

out of their flash surf to be strand upon some distant beach, or to

drift, maybe for day, upon the bosom of the tide, look up with

blind face to heaven through the green transparent lid of their sea

coffin? there be no spot that have ever be the theatre of wilder human

suffering. again and again as you sail past you see fork up out of

they some black gibbet-like relic of a wreck a week, a fortnight, a

month old. something of the kind be always visible, as though even on

the tenderest of summer day, when the blue water sleep around, and the

heavens be a violet hollow, with a rayless sun make gold of the sea

in the west, the deadly suggestiveness of that long sweep of yellow sand

should be as plain as when its presence be denote amid the black

tempestuous night by the ghastly gleam of boil white water.



I remember once pass these Goodwins and see a number of little

black figure run about they. a pleasure vessel from one of the

adjacent port be lie at anchor a short distance off, and her boat

be against the slope of the shoal. it be a very calm day indeed, the

sea just blur here and there with small draught of air that give the

water in those place a look of ice, with a pallid streak of the french

coast beyond the white mainsail of the pleasure-cutter, hove up by the

refraction of the light above the sea-line. I bring a small pocket

telescope to bear, and observe that those little black figure run

about like the savage Robinson Crusoe see be Cockney excursionist,

engage in play cricket. they play as if they want to be able to

talk of having play rather than as if they enjoy the game. talk of

contrast! a man may be render pensive by watch child sport

in a graveyard, by mingle in a festivity hold upon a space of ground

where once a famous battle be fight, and where the foot of the

merrymaker be separate from the bone and skull of warrior by a

couple of spade’ length of earth. but to see those little black-coat

creature run about after a ball on top of such an ocean

burial-place that the like of it for the horror of its annal and for

the number of those it have sepulchre be not to be find in this

habitable world, might well have make the gay heart sad and

thoughtful for a spell.



as I lean over the rail, look at those happy pigmy--those lord

of creation who, view half a mile far away, might have pass for

a handful of black crab crawl about--the scene in imagination

change, the darkness came rush out of the east with a moan of

approach storm, the three lantern wink like star beyond the North

Sand Head, and there be a sound of weltering water and the seething

and hiss of surf rise up through the gloom out from the whole

length of the shoal. the wind rise fresh and eagerly, with a raw edge

in it; the ebony of the swell water be break by the glimmer of the

froth of break sea. I could hear the muffle thunder of the confused

play to windward of the surf, with the shrieking of the blast overhead,

whilst a deep shadow yet gather in the air. then, with a blinking of

my eye, back would come the fact of the thing again, and yonder be

the little figure merrily chase the ball, the sea spread like a

sheet of silk to the yellow rim of the hard sand, and the blue sky

bright overhead. yet another touch of the magician Fancy’s wand, and it

be all howl storm and fly blackness and the steam of hurl

spume again, with a sudden glare of lightning between, fling out the

shape of the pile of whirl cloud like monstrous brandish wing

go to piece in the hurricane, and throw up the black fabric of a

big ship on her beam end, her mast gone, and a fury of white water

veil she.



there be lifeboat coxswain who need but close their eye to see

fearfuller thing. just where those little creature be brandish

their tiny bat and flourish their shrimp-like leg, the great ship

strike, and four hundred man and woman shriek out to God for mercy in

one breath. a man’s fancy must be feeble even on the softest of summer

day not to hear the crash of her timber, the thunder-shock of the

smiting sea, the rend noise of hemp and wire and spar tear by the

tempest from their strong fastening; not to see the ghastly picture she

make in the wild gleam of the signal flare whose tongue of fire be

blow horizontal, like streaming flag, by the furious breath of the

storm, illuminate with a dull horrible crimson light the throng of

human being who cry and struggle upon her deck, or hang, like

streaming suit of clothe, in what remain of her rigging.



be this an exaggerated picture? alas! the pen never yet be wield that

could pourtray, in the bare form, any one of the countless horrible

scene which have take place on that stretch of sand where one summer

day I watch, lean over the rail of a vessel, a number of

light-hearted excursionist play cricket.



among the thing which never can be know may be place the thought

which possess a man in the moment of shipwreck. of the hundred of

publish narrative none satisfy the reader; and of those who relate

their experience, how infinitely remote from the truth do their

statement strike they as be when they put what they have write

side by side with what they remember having feel! the reason be, I take

it, because in no other situation be death more awful than upon the sea.

it be commonly slow--at least, it give time for anguish to become

full-blown--and the hope of rescue must be very strong indeed, and well

found, to qualify that agony of expectation, sink into paralyze

despair, which confound and in a manner stun a person strand far out

upon the water in a black night, see nothing but the glare of

lightning or the spectral flashing of froth fly past, hear nothing

but the grinding and tremble and dislocate noise of the hull upon

the ground.



it be suppose because sailor cannot or do not describe the horror

they pass through that they lack the capacity of expression. but you may

put the most eloquent writer now live, call he by what name you

please, on board a ship founder amid a tempest or go to piece in

a storm on such a shoal as the Goodwins or the Sunk Sand, and when he

have be long enough rescue and ashore to recover the use of his

brain, you may defy he to write such a narrative of the disaster as

will come, to his own conscience and memory, one jot nearer to the truth

than the newspaper paragraph of five line in which the wreck be

chronicle. a man can describe what he have suffer in a railway

collision, in a house on fire, down in a mine where there have be an

explosion, in a theatre where there have be a panic; but put he aboard

a ship and let he clearly understand that he be go to be drown,

and when succoured he can tell you little more than that the wave run

mountain high, that some people be brave, and that some people

shriek, and that what he good remember be catch hold of something,

and hear the water in his ear, and be drag into a boat.



very true be the old saying, “if you want to learn how to pray, you must

go to sea.” so distract, so paralyze, so utterly despairful be all

the condition of shipwreck in its bad form, that I cannot but

think, when a man be know to act bravely and coolly in that situation,

unmindful of himself, think of other, encourage and hearten

they, the heroism he exhibit be of a kind not to be match by any kind

of courage a man may show in a position that lack the overwhelming

feature which distinguish the foundering or the stranding of a ship.



some day ago I meet a seaman who have make one of the crew of a brig that

a few month since be strand on the Goodwin Sands, and go to piece

there. the circumstance of the wreck be so recent that I be sure it

could not but be a very sharp, clear memory in this sailor; and, want

to hear what sort of thought come into a man’s head at such a time, and

how he will act, what kind of impulse govern he, and the like, I

carry this mariner to where a seat and a glass of beer be to be have,

and converse with he.



“she be a wessel,” say he, “of 220 ton, and we be in ballast, bind

from Can (Caen) to Seaham. all go well, nothen particular happening, I

mean, till we come abreast o’ the South Foreland. it might then be

twelve o’clock in the middle o’ the night. the weather be as thick as

mud, plenty of rain drive along, and the wind west, blowin’ a fresh

breeze. we be under upper and low main-tops’l, low fore-tops’l, and

foresail.”



here he take a drink.



“and the weather as thick as mud, you say?”



“ay, thick as mud in a wine-glass. the Sou’ San’head light be on our

starboard beam, and ye may guess how clear it be when I tell you that

that light take a deal of peer at to make out. as to the East

Good’in, why, all that way be black as my boot: not the mere glimmer

to betoken a lightwessel _there_. I be at the side, heavin’ the lead,

get nine fathom, and then seven, and then eight, and then seven

again. eight fair betwixt the Callipers and the Deal coast I’ll allow

ye’ll get eleven and twelve fathom good till you come on to past the

Downs--headin’ up, I mean--and then it shoal down to height and seven

and five and a ’arf. so in a night as black as a dead wall, when there’s

no moon, who’s to know, when the last light see have draw out of view,

and there’s ne’er another to be sight, where you be in that water? we

be go along tidy fast, when a squall of rain drive right up over

our starn in a wild smother, and I have just make seven fathom by the

lead when the wessel take the ground, chuck I off the rail on to the

deck. the skipper begin to bawl out like mad, ‘let go the main-torps’l

halliard! haul up the foresail! let go the ----’ wash at that moment

come a lump of sea right over the port quarter, cantin’ our starn to

the south’ard and smotherin’ the deck. you don’t want to see--you

could feel that the brig be hard and fast, though as the sea thump

her she’d kinder sway on her keel.”



here he take another drink.



“well?” say I.



“well,” he continue, “what be to do now, master? everything be let

go aloft, the canvas be slat like thunder up there, and though I’m

not goin’ to tell you it be blow a gale of wind, yet it seem to

come twice as hard the moment we take the ground, and the sea to rise

as if our fall helpless on a sudden have swell ’em up with joy. we

lie with our head about nor’-nor’-east, and over the starboard bow you

could see the white water jumping. but that be all that be visible.

the wind seem to blow up the thickness all round we, there be not a

light to be see, and look around anywhere away from the white water

be like put your head in a pitch-kettle. cold! master, that be the

bad part of it. I’ll allow that in all sitivation of this kind the

cold’s the part that’s hard to bear. somehow clang ain’t so

frightful when it’s warm. cann’t explain it, I’m sure; matter o’

constitootion, perhaps: but I doubt if ye’d find much bravery among the

Hesquimos and the Roosians up near the pole, and the like o’ they.

cann’t see how it’s possible; but it’s only my ’pinion.”



another drink.



“well,” he continue, hold up the fresh glass of ale I have order

for he to the light, with a look of pensiveness in the one bloodshot

eye he keep open, “we tarn to and make a flare--a sort o’ bonfire. but

if we couldn’t see anything, who be to see we? however, we keep all on

burn flare, whilst first the fore-top-gall’nmast come down with a

run, cause we all to jump aft out of the road, and then the

main-topmast carry away at the cap and fall with a roar over the

side, and set we all run forrard. I for one make up my mind we be

all to be drownde. I couldn’t see no help for it. the noise of they

spar crack and tumble away in the blackness overhead, and the

shindy set up by the slat canvas, along with the creaking of the

hull and the washing of the water that come as white as milk over the

starboard rail, be enough, I reckon, to make any man suppose his time

have come, and that his ghost be to be turn out of he. however, we

take heart after a spell, by notice that the sea burst with less

weight as the tide leave we, though every butt in she must have yaw

open after she have be grind awhile, for she be full of water and a

few hour more of such dusting be bind to have make stave of she.

well, at about half-past four o’clock in the morning, we be by that

time pretty near froze to death, the weather thin down, and we catch

sight of the Gull Light shine--about three mile off, I dare say. what

be to be see of our wessel be just a fearful muddle; mast overboard

washing alongside, the low mast work in she like loose tooth with

every heave, deck full of raffle, and the water every now and again

fly over we as though detarmine if it couldn’t wash we overboard it

would keep we streamin’ wet. when we spy the Gull Light we turn to

and make another flare, and presently they send up a rocket, and to cut

this yarn short,” continue he, have by this time empty his second

tumbler, and find I slow in offer he a third, “just as the light

be abreakin’ in the east one of we sing out that there be a steamer

headin’ for we, and when the mornin’ grow strong we spy a tug makin’

for we with a lifeboat in tow. well, by this time there be little

enough sea, and the lifeboat, letting go off the tug, come alongside,

but two of our man be so badly freeze up that they have to be lift into

she, and such have be our suffering, though I’m not go to say they

equal what other have go through on those cuss sand, that we

couldn’t have look bad, with salt in our eye and our face wash

into the appearance of tallow, have we be spendin’ forty-eight hour on

that shoal. we lose all our clothe, every bloomin’ thing we have with

we; and that same forenoon, just afore twelve o’clock, half a gale of

wind spring up, and by two o’clock there be nothing to be see of the

brig.”



“and that’s the story,” say I.



“that’s it,” he answer; “every word gospel true.”



“how do the other behave,” say I, “in this awful situation? pretty

well?”



“it be too dark to see,” he answer.



“do you encourage one another?”



“well,” he reply, “the cook at first keep on singin’ out, ‘we’re all

drownde man! Lord have mercy upon I!’ and the like of that, until the

cold take away his voice. I don’t know that there be any other sort o’

encouragement.”



“and what be your feeling,” say I, “when the brig take the ground

and the water wash over she?”



“my feeling?” he reply. “why, that we be in a bloomin’ mess. that

be my feeling.”



“how do the prospect of death affect you--I mean the idea of be

sweep into the black water and strangle there?”



“be you chaffin’ I, sir?” he ask.



“certainly not,” say I.



“well,” he say, “I’m bless if I be ask such a question as that

afore,” grin. “it’s like a meetin’-house question.”



“don’t you think at all?” say I.



“yes,” he answer; “I think what a jolly fool I be to be ashore on

the Good’ens on a winter’s night, gradually dyin’ of frost, instead of

bein’ in a warm bed ashore, with a parlour to take breakfast in when I

wake up. that’s about it, sir.”









_the stranger’ home._





a plain red-brick building stand in the West India Dock Road, with the
