follow lengthy name or description write along the front of

it:--“The Strangers’ home for Asiatics, Africans, and South Sea

Islanders.” on the day I visit this house there be three or four

people stand on the doorstep, with face which do more in an

instant to express the character of the place than could have be

effect by ream of report of annual meeting and descriptive

pamphlet. they be, it be needless to say, person of colour, and of

very decide colour too: one as black as a hat, another of a muddy

yellow, a third a gloomy brown. they be dress in european clothe:

they might have belong to nation which be in a high state of

civilization when the Thames be clean water, and roll its silver

stream through a land whose scanty population hang loose and unclothe

among the tree; but for all that, they have the look of wild man in

breech, and the very black person need little more than a boomerang

or a bow and arrow to give he the aspect at least of an unsafe object.

I have, however, but little time to inspect these man, for a commotion in

the hall of the building, couple with an assemblage of some dozen or

twenty people on the street pavement, call my attention to a

spectacle of real interest. this consist of the starting of a troupe

of javanese musician for the place of entertainment where they be

then perform. there be a number of man and four woman--at least, I

think there be four woman; yet it be possible that I may have mistake

a man for one of the other sex, for some of the man and woman be very

much alike, especially the man. they stream out in a great hurry,

their bright black eye sparkle in their brown face, the man smoke

short pipe of a decidedly West India Dock Road pattern, and the woman

bundle along in such queer raiment that it would be as hopeless to

attempt to describe its colour and cut as to catalogue the stock of a

rag-and-bottle merchant. a kind of large private omnibus stand at the

door, into which these strange people get, some of they climb upon

the roof; and strike indeed be the appearance of the window of the

vehicle, framing, as they do, every one of they, a dark, content

face, whilst the roof of the omnibus be crowd with black and white,

like the key of a pianoforte.



“who be those people?” say I to a Chinaman, as the omnibus roll

away.



“Hey?” answer John.



“those people,” I say, point towards the retreating vehicle, “they

be not sailor, be they? there be woman among they.”



“no, no, not sailor, no, no,” cry the Chinaman with great earnestness,

and wag his head so violently that he nearly shake his hat off.

“music-man, not sailor; play tic-a-tic, tic-a-tic;” and here he screw

an imaginary fiddle into his throat and fall to saw the air with his

elbow.



at this moment I be join by the secretary--a gentleman, let I say

at once, who, after spend many year of his life in India, be now

gratuitously devote his service to the poor Asiatic who find himself

homeless in this great wilderness of London, often penniless, and

speak a tongue with which he may journey from Mile End Gate to

Hammersmith without find an ear capable of comprehend a word he

say. this gentleman tell I who those queer-look people be, how

they be in charge of a Dutch _entrepreneur_, and how they be

“put-up” at the Strangers’ home because there, and at no other place

in London, they be likely to meet people who, even if they do not

speak their language, would impart a sense of home.



we now proceed to inspect the building. as at the Well Street Sailors’

Home, so here, the common room, if I may so term it, be the central

hall, a large place furnish with seat and table and heat by an

immense stove. here of an evening, when it be cold or damp out of door,

the inmate of the home assemble, and the bright lamp shed their light

upon as many diverse countenance and costume as there be

nationality to the eastward of Russia and in the great ocean which

wash the Capes of Africa and South America. strange, indeed, be the

admixture to a european eye: the Hindoo sit cross-legged on a bench

listening, with dusky eye roll in his black attenuated feature, to

the pigeon-english of a round-face Chinaman; a Malay endeavouring by

gesture to make himself understand by a Kanaka; a native of Ceylon

smile over the porcine guttural of a couple of Zulus; with here an

arab reis pace the floor in lonely dignity, or a red man of a

paternity indistinguishable in his feature, which seem compound of

the Nubian, the last of the Mohicans, a dash of Polynesia, with a hint

of Liverpool or Bristol, spell over a volume full of murky and

eye-confound hieroglyph.



“when this hall be full the sight be a remarkable one,” say the

secretary. “what with the hum of the strange language--perhaps as many

as twenty all go at once--together with the various face and

clothe, I assure you it need no small effort of mind to convince one’s

self that one be still in London, and that just out of door omnibus

be roll, small boy call out the evening paper, and policeman

stand at the corner.”



I feel the force of this, thinly people as the hall be, when I stand

in it gaze around. be it the strange haunt Eastern smell--vague

fume, as of a hubble-bubble recently smoke out; a linger whiff as

of curry; a thin, ghostly odour of bamboo, chilli, oil, nutmeg, and

cedar-wood? or be it the turban, the pigtail, the almond-shape eye,

the black, bronze, and yellow skin of the few Asiatics who be seat

in a body on a central bench, that carry my imagination out of the

West India Dock Road into the tangled forest, the hot, blue heaven,

the joss-house, the sampans and junk, the rush river, the

jackall, the dusky figure, the blue gown and red or yellow shoe of

the distant, spacious province of the sun? Hark to the sing-song

chatter babbling from that mongolian visage! what be the magic of it,

that this hall, gloomy with the smoke of the great city blow

riverward, should be transform into a shine eastern city, whose

shore, rich with the green of tropical vegetation, be wash by a sea

whose breast reflect a heaven of sapphire? the voice cease; the spell

be break; the muffled roar of the toil world outside break in and

establish one of the very sharp contrast in life--that of the

condition of an asiatic, fresh from the hot sun and thick jungle of

his own country, plunge amid the smoke, the turmoil, the unspeakable

odour of the east end of London, incapable of make his want know,

languish in misery and cold in the gloom of railway arch or some

unfrequented court, feel what solitude be in a sense never imagine

by Byron. for the Asiatic’s loneliness be that of the dumb brute; he have

a language, but he might as well be voiceless, and, bad of all, he be

the victim of the unnatural, ignorant, and wicked prejudice which find

in the coloured skin nothing but what be fit for derision, contempt, and

cruel neglect. this be the lot of the eastern stranger before a number

of humane englishman band themselves together to furnish he with a

refuge. I own that the fine humanity of this institution affect I

strongly as I stood look at the knot of dark-visage, strangely

apparelle man, and consider what would be their fate if this home

be not at hand to help they, to receive they, to interpret their

want, and to assist they to return to their native country. time be

when few tragedy be common than that of the finding the body of

some coloured man who have make his way as a sailor or stoker to London,

be rob by the beast of prey who wander hungrily round the dockyard

gate, and have lain himself down in some corner of this opulent city to

die of cold and hunger. such horror be thing of the past, and

honour be those and the memory of those who have make they so. no

asiatic stranger need perish for the want of a friend in London now. the

Home will receive he, and for a very moderate sum--which he may easily

pay either from the wage due to he from the ship he leave, or by the

note advanced by the owner whose ship he join--feed and lodge he,

and spare no trouble to restore he to his own country. hence, to a

large extent, the institution contribute to its own support. but the

charge it make be so small, the loss it incur through the new

allotment or bonus note be so frequent, and the case of absolute

destitution it deal with so numerous, that it be bind to continue to

be dependent upon outside help to a certain extent; and I believe that

no one who have any knowledge of the work it be do, no one with

sympathy for the helpless of his own specie, but will admit that there

be not an institution in existence that well deserve the gift of the

charitable than this home for Asiatic Strangers.



but all this time I be leave the obliging and kind-hearte secretary

wait to show I over the premise. we pass out first of all into a

space of open ground at the back of the building, of which a substantial

piece have be convert into a flower garden. this the superintendent

of the home, who have join we, contemplate for a while with silent

satisfaction, and then, with considerable pride, draw my attention to

it.



“it have all be do within the last two or three year,” he say. “the

Asiatics lend a hand, find old seed knock about the bottom of their

chest, and plant they, but they never come to anything. they willn’t

grow, you know, in this climate. here’s a sample,” he say, point to

a row of shoot which look like the first budding of that patriotic

vegetable the leek; “they be plant three day ago by those Javanese

woman you see, and this be what they’ve already come to. but I suspect

they’ll end at that.”



a balcony run at the back of the house, and along it there be stump

a John Canoe, smoke something strange, whether a pipe or cigar or

cigarette or piece of cane I could not tell. he vanish through a door

when we mount the step, which I regret, as I should like to have

examine the thing he have in his mouth.



“this,” say the secretary on our re-enter the building, “be what we

call the fireman’s dormitory.” it be a large room with a bulkhead

divide it, and on either hand of the bulkhead go a row of narrow

bed furnish with coarse coverlet and mattress stuff with fibre.

there be no carpet, and I venture to ask the reason, as the bare

board have but a cheerless look.



“Carpet!” exclaim the secretary; “my dear sir, these Asiatics wouldn’t

know what to do with such a thing. they’d pull it up and make trouser

of it. you cannot conceive the strangeness of their habit and custom.

for instance, to give they a table-cloth would be like ill treating

they. nothing bother they more than a fork; and you may see they eat

egg with clasp-knife, which they pull out of their pocket.” then

see I eye the bed, he continue, “it would hardly do to give the

fireman fine linen to lie in. Sir, they arrive here thick with grime,

they foul whatever they touch, and it take several day of hard bathing

to clean they.”



there be several of these dormitory, each of they divide by

bulkhead, uncarpeted, and contain the same kind of bedstead, every

one bear a number at its head. the javanese troupe occupy one of

these dormitory, the man sleep on one side of the bulkhead and the

woman on the other. I look for their luggage, but could find nothing

but a fiddle and an old sword. I think, if the public have see where

these musician sleep, they would reckon the sight stranger than any

other part of the performance these eastern people be give. there

be one dormitory, however, upstairs fill with cabin similar to what

they have at the old Sailors’ home at Belvedere. these be occupy, I

be tell, by the well class of Asiatics.



“and who might they be?” I ask.



“why,” I be tell, “japanese officer, steward, chinese carpenter,

native doctor, and the like.”



these be the “dignity man.” they have a little room in which they may

dine apart from the Lascars, Kanakas, John Chinamen, and the other;

but, somehow, they don’t seem to value exclusiveness, for most of they

will quit their table to join the pigtail and half-caste in the big

eat-room downstair, where they find a relish in their rice and fish

which appear to be want in the dish in the other apartment. in one

of the dormitory we come across a Javanese--one of the troupe--sit

cross-legged on his bed, ill with a cold in the head. his unsmoked pipe

lie by his side, and he be listlessly handle some piece of print

calico, though the use he mean to put they to I could not divine. there

be a no more melancholy object than a coloured man suffer from a bad

cold in the head. I see he shiver, and then roll his eye--black as

ebony set in orange--upon the window, and I think to myself, “how this

harmless, coloured man, who speak nothing but Javanese, and who belong

to a country where the air be radiant with beautiful bird and fragrant

with delicious fruit, must enjoy the climate of the West India Dock

Road!”



we struggle to impart sympathy by several kind of gesture and

motion, but it would not do; we could not get far than alarm

he, and so we leave he. in another dormitory we find a Ceylon man, a

Madrassee Lascar, and a Japanese. the Ceylon man be a very handsome

fellow, his hair part down the middle, and he have as fine a pair of

eye as ever I see in the human countenance, regular feature, and a

wonderfully good figure. he be read an English book, and speak

English so well that, what with his correct utterance, the colour of his

skin, and his strike face, a misgiving seize I.



“be you a pure cingalese?” I ask.



“no, no,” cry he, with much anxiety in his manner; “my father be an

english sailor!”



but the Madrassee man, in a measure, atone for this disappointment. he

be the real thing--just the sort of conformation to tumble about in a

surf-boat, very black, very lean, with snow-white tooth, and a high long

nose as thin as a hatchet. the secretary converse with he in his

native lingo, and it seem to do the poor fellow good to talk. the

Japanese have a wooden face, and have very little to say. indeed, I always

think that the people of his race and the chinese view we and our work

with a good deal of contempt. what a mean opinion they must have of our

toy, of our painting, in which the literal be sacrifice to the

poetical; of our clothe, tea, head-dress, coiffure, and a thousand

other matter! they have a chinese porter at the home, who be dress in

a black coat and wear a hat. I do not speak to he, but I should

judge, from observe the expression on his face when in a state of

repose, that he have but a poor opinion of Great Britain. in another

dormitory be a couple of Arabs mend shirt; and downstairs, in the

scullery, I meet a Zulu, who tell I that he be a subject of Cetewayo,

and have call at that King’s lodging when he be in London, but have

not manage to see his Majesty. one of the suite promise to write and

appoint an hour for an interview; but no letter ever reach the youth,

and the next thing he hear of Cetewayo be that he have sail for

Africa. this scullery lead into a large kitchen, very well appoint, and

in spick and span condition. Adjoining be the provision room,

contain one large sack of rice, a quantity of smoked herring, a jar

of chilli, another jar of curry powder, and other eastern relish.



“the mackerel be the favourite dish with our inmate, be they of

whatever nationality they will,” say the secretary. “they consider it

the fine fish that be catch in european water, and lament when the

season for catch they be over.”



I ask what food they be supply with in the home.



“we have,” he reply, “what we call three mess. the first-class mess

be sixteen shilling a week--this include a separate cabin; the second,

without a cabin, be fourteen shilling; and the third, which we term the

curry and rice mess, be ten shilling. the first two mess comprise,

for breakfast, fish or egg, coffee, bread and butter; for dinner we

give beef or mutton, with vegetable, and curry and rice always; tea,

the same as breakfast.”



“the charge be small enough,” say I.



“but they have other privilege,” say he. “for instance, there be hot

and cold water bath downstairs, for the use of which no charge be make.

we also receive and take care of their money and valuable--for some of

the people who come here bring real valuable, such as jewel, with

they, I assure you. since last January the amount deposit in money

with we have amount to £2,285, of which I do not scruple to say that,

but for the existence of this home, the great portion would have be

steal from its owner by the crimp and boarding-house people who haunt

our neighbourhood. that room you see there be our shipping-office;

captain come to we and select man for their vessel, and when the

choice have be make we accompany the man to the marine office, see

they sign article and that the advance be duly make. indeed, we do all

that we possibly can to help and protect these poor stranger.”



“what I have see assure I of that,” say I.



“this,” he continue, as we go upstairs and enter a large cheerful

dormitory, “be what we call the ayah’ room. it be mean for native

woman who be bring home as nurse and discharge. sad case of

destitution be often occur. not long ago a City missionary find a

native woman in an empty house in Shepherd’s Bush. he bring she here,

and, having learn the name of her mistress, we go to she, and be

tell that the ayah be insane, that she have be keep as long as

possible, have at last refuse to go, and be accordingly turn out. we

take charge of she for awhile, but her madness increase, and we be

force at last to send she to a county asylum, where she now remain.”



the inspection of this room exhaust all that be to be see; so,

bid the cordial secretary farewell, and take a linger look at a

knot of dusky man who be talk in the hall, I quit this

hospitable and most valuable institution, resolve to record all that I

have hear and view, in the earnest hope that of those by whom this

record of my visit will be read some may be induce to help an excellent

charity by send donation to the manager of the Strangers’ home, West

India Dock Road, London.
